Two Premises for the Discussion of Seeing God
Razi lays down two premises for the discussion of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conceptual premises that Razi lays down as necessary before any discussion of whether God can be seen: what 'seeing' means in relation to God and that God is transcendent beyond physical categories.

Core Idea
Seeing God is not physical vision but a state of manifestation; God is beyond body, place, and substance.

Razi begins the nineteenth issue of his work on the visibility of God by setting out two foundational premises. The first clarifies what we mean when we ask whether God can be seen—it is not about physical sight but about a state of unveiling. The second affirms God's transcendence over body, substance, place, and region, which his opponents often deny. These premises frame the entire debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let Razi's own structure come through: he announces two sections, then the first section contains two premises.
Pay attention to the logical flow: definition first, then transcendence.
2
Understand the first premise
Identify how Razi defines the question of seeing God. He clarifies that 'sight' (ibsar) is an additional state beyond knowledge and involves the sense organ, but since God's essence is unique, the question is about a state of unveiling analogous to physical sight.
This premise avoids misunderstanding that we are asking about physical eyesight.
3
Understand the second premise
Razi states that God is exalted above being a body, substance, place, or region. Then he claims that such a being can be seen. He notes that philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this, while Karramiyya and anthropomorphists only affirm it because they falsely believe God is a body.
This premise shows that the debate hinges on whether a non-physical being can be 'seen'.
4
Ask: why are these premises necessary?
Consider why Razi insists on these two premises before any argument. They define the terms and the conditions of the debate, ensuring all parties have a shared starting point.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر في أنه تعالى مرئي. والكلام فيه مرتب على فصول: وهما اثنتان: الفصل الأول: في المقدمتين اللتين يجب تقديمهما. الأولى في تفسير قولنا: الله تعالى مرئي أم لا؟ اعلم أنا بينا في مسألة السمع والبصر أن الإبصار حالة زائدة على العلم، وعنى تأثر الحاسة، وبينا أيضًا أن ذاته تعالى ذات مخالفة لسائر الذوات، فالمراد من قولنا: إن الله تعالى هل يصح أن يرى؟ هو أنه هل تمكن حالة في الانكشاف والظهور، نسبتها إلى ذاته المخصوصة، كنسبة الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والرؤية إلى هذه المرئيات أم لا؟ المقدمة الثانية: اعلم أنا بينا أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن أن يكون جسما وجوهرا ومختصا بمكان وحيز، ثم إنا ندعي أن هذا الشيء الموصوف بهذه الصفات يمكن رؤيته، وهذا القول يخالفنا فيه كل من ليس على مذهبنا، أما الفلاسفة والمعتزلة فذلك ظاهر، وأما الكرامية والمجسمة فهم إنما يسلمون جواز رؤية الله تعالى لاعتقادهم أنه جسم وفي مكان، وهم متفقون على أنه تعالى لو لم يكن جسما ولم يكن في مكان فإنه يمتنع فضلا وجوده.
The nineteenth issue: On His being visible. The discussion is arranged into sections, which are two: The first section: On the two premises that must be presented. The first: On the interpretation of our statement: Is God visible or not? Know that we have explained in the issue of hearing and sight that seeing (ibsar) is an additional state beyond knowledge, and it means the impression of the sense organ. We have also explained that His essence is different from all other essences. So the meaning of our statement: Is it valid for God to be seen? is whether a state of unveiling and manifestation is possible, whose relation to His specific essence is like the relation of the state called sight and vision to these visible things, or not? The second premise: Know that we have explained that He is exalted above being a body (jism), substance (jawhar), and being specific to a place (makan) and region (hayyiz). Then we claim that this thing described with these attributes can be seen. This statement is opposed by everyone who is not upon our school. As for the philosophers and Mu'tazila, that is obvious. As for the Karramiyya and the Anthropomorphists (mujassima), they only affirm the permissibility of seeing God because they believe He is a body and in a place. They agree that if He were not a body and not in a place, then it would be impossible, let alone His existence.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. Razi introduces the nineteenth issue on the visibility of God. He arranges the discussion into two sections, and the first section contains two premises. The first premise defines what is meant by 'seeing God'. He clarifies that seeing is an additional state beyond knowledge, involving the sense organ, and since God's essence is unlike others, the question is whether a state of unveiling can occur to Him analogous to our seeing physical objects. The second premise states that God is transcendent beyond body, substance, place, and region. Despite this, Razi claims that such a being can be seen. He notes that philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this, while Karramiyya and anthropomorphists affirm it only because they believe God is a body. Thus these two premises set the stage for the subsequent arguments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'seeing God' means physical eyesight, like seeing a physical object.
Correction: Razi explains that ru'ya is a state of unveiling and manifestation, not physical vision, and God's essence is unlike any other essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first premise, what is the state of seeing (ibsar) in relation to knowledge?
A) It is the same as knowledge.
B) It is an additional state beyond knowledge.
C) It is the absence of knowledge.
D) It is a type of imagination.

Key Takeaway
Razi's two premises—defining ru'ya as a state of manifestation and affirming God's transcendence over physical categories—provide the necessary conceptual foundation for the debate on seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing God is not physical vision but a state of manifestation, how does that change your longing to see Him? Do you truly desire that unveiling, or are you satisfied with only the information you have now?
?
Razi begins by defining the terms. Are you willing to let go of your own assumptions about God's nature in order to understand what the scholars actually say? What assumptions about God do you hold that might actually be borrowed from imagining Him as a body or in a place?
?
The second premise affirms that God is beyond body, place, and substance. When you worship, do you ever fall into imagining God in a specific direction or location? How can you train your heart to worship without such limitations?

The Second Proof: Ru'ya is Not Self-Evident
Razi's second proof uses comparative necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's second proof that the possibility of seeing Allah is not as evident as basic mathematical truths, thus requiring rational proof.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is not self-evident; comparing it to 'one is half of two' reveals its need for proof.

Imam Razi continues his defense of the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) by arguing that the Mu'tazila often treat its impossibility as self-evident. In this proof, he uses a comparison with a necessary truth to show that the proposition of ru'ya is not inherently certain. This challenges the Mu'tazila's claim of necessity and underscores the need for reasoned argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that it begins by labeling the discussion as the nineteenth issue.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the comparison being made.
2
Identify the analogy
Razi compares the proposition of ru'ya to the necessary truth that one is half of two. Understand that he is testing the strength of the ru'ya claim against a self-evident truth.
The second proposition is a mathematical truth accepted by all.
3
Grasp the implication
Since the ru'ya proposition is not as strong, it is not a necessary truth. Therefore, its impossibility cannot be claimed without proof.
This undercuts the Mu'tazila's argument that ru'ya is impossible by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر
الحجة الثانية: أنا إذا عرضنا على عقولنا رؤية هذا الموجود بالتفسير الذي لخصناه وعرضنا على عقولنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين لم نجد القضية الأولى في قوة هذه الثانية.
الحجة الثالثة : أن حكم الوهم والخيال في معرفة الله تعالى إما أن يكون مقبولا أو لا يكون مقبولاً ، فإن كان مقبولا لا يمتنع إثبات ذات منزهة عن الكمية والكيفية والجهة والمعتزلي يسلم أن ذلك باطل، وإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن حكم الوهم بأن ما كان منزها عن الجهة كان غير مرئي واجب القبول لأن الوهم والخيال لما صار كل واحد منهما مردود الحكم في بعض الأحكام لم يبق الاعتماد عليها في شيء من المواضع.
وبالجملة : فإن كان حكم الوهم حقا كان الحق مع المجسم، وإن كان مردودا كان الحق معنا، أما المعتزلي فإنه يرد حكمه في إثبات التجسيم والجهة، ويقبل حكمه مسألة الرؤية، فكان كلامه متناقضا.
فثبت بما ذكرنا أن من نفى الرؤية بالوجه الذي ذكرناه لا بد أن يعول في نفيها على الدليل لا على ادعاء الضرورة.
وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدمة لأن هؤلاء المعتزلة في أول الأمر يلتزمون الاستدلال، وفي آخر الأمر عند العجز عن تمشية الدليل كأنهم يسرعون في ادعاء الضرورة، فقدمنا هذه المقدمة صونا للكلام عن هذا الخبط.
The Nineteenth Issue. The second proof: If we present to our intellects the vision of this existent according to the interpretation we summarized and present to our intellects that one is half of two, we do not find the first proposition to be as strong as this second. The third proof: The judgment of wahm (estimation) and khayal (imagination) regarding knowledge of Allah the Most High is either accepted or not accepted. If it is accepted, then it does not prevent affirming an essence transcendent of quantity, quality, and direction, and the Mu'tazili acknowledges that is false. And if it is not accepted, then the judgment of wahm that what is transcendent of direction is not visible is not necessarily acceptable, because since wahm and khayal each have their judgment rejected in some cases, reliance upon them does not remain in any matter. In summary: If the judgment of wahm is true, then the truth is with the mujassim (anthropomorphist); if it is rejected, then the truth is with us. As for the Mu'tazili, he rejects its judgment in affirming corporealism and direction, and accepts its judgment in the matter of ru'ya (vision); thus his statement is contradictory. So it is established by what we have mentioned that whoever negates ru'ya in the manner we described must rely in his negation on proof, not on claiming necessity. We only presented this introduction because these Mu'tazila at first commit to reasoning, and at the end when unable to sustain the proof, they hasten to claim necessity; so we presented this introduction to protect the discourse from this confusion.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument section where Imam al-Razi presents two proofs against the Mu'tazila's denial of seeing Allah, followed by a conclusive summary. The passage begins by labeling the discussion as the nineteenth issue. The second proof is an analogy: when we rationally consider the possibility of seeing an existent (as defined by the author's interpretation) and compare it to the necessary truth that one is half of two, the first proposition does not have the same evident strength. This shows that the permissibility of ru'ya is not self-evident and requires proof. The third proof attacks the Mu'tazila's use of wahm (estimation). Al-Razi argues that if the judgment of wahm is accepted in matters of divine knowledge, then it could lead to affirming a transcendent essence—which the Mu'tazila reject. If it is not accepted, then the Mu'tazila cannot rely on wahm to claim that what is without direction is invisible. Since wahm is unreliable (its judgments are sometimes rejected), it cannot be used as a basis for absolute denial. The summary (وبالجملة) highlights the contradiction in the Mu'tazila's approach: they accept the judgment of wahm when it supports anthropomorphism (which they reject) and reject it when it contradicts their denial of ru'ya. Thus their position is inconsistent. Al-Razi concludes that anyone who denies ru'ya must do so based on rational proof, not on an alleged self-evident necessity. He ends by explaining why he introduced this prefatory remark: the Mu'tazila initially engage in reasoning but when pressed, they fall back on claiming necessity, so he preempts that rhetorical move.

Key Terms
رؤية
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the impossibility of seeing Allah is self-evident and needs no proof.
Correction: Razi shows that the proposition of ru'ya is not as strong as a necessary truth, so its impossibility requires proof.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi compare the proposition of ru'ya to in his second proof?
A) The existence of the world
B) The truth that one is half of two
C) The necessity of God's existence
D) The rejection of anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that ru'ya is not a necessary truth, so its impossibility cannot be claimed without proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief about Allah that you hold as 'obvious' without ever seeking its proof? What is it, and why do you assume it?
?
Are you willing to subject your beliefs about what Allah can or cannot do to rational scrutiny, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
If the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife requires proof, what does that say about the importance of knowledge in your faith? Are you content with hearsay or do you seek to know?

Third Proof and Conclusion: The Contradiction of Mu'tazila on Wahm
Razi's third proof and summary refuting Mu'tazila on wahm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi exposes the Mu'tazila's inconsistent reliance on wahm and concludes that denial of ru'ya must be based on proof, not necessity.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila accept wahm when it suits them and reject it when it doesn't - a contradiction.

In his third proof, Imam Razi attacks the Mu'tazila's use of wahm (estimation) as a basis for denying ru'ya. He shows that if wahm is reliable, it could lead to anthropomorphism which they reject; if unreliable, they cannot rely on it. He then summarizes their inconsistency and concludes that denial of ru'ya requires rational proof, not a claim of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof and the concluding summary. Notice the conditional structure.
This is a longer passage; take your time to follow the logic.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi presents two possibilities: if wahm's judgment is accepted in divine matters, or if it is not. Understand each branch and its consequence.
The first branch leads to affirming a transcendent essence (false for Mu'tazila); the second branch undermines reliance on wahm.
3
Analyze the summary
The summary (وبالجملة) highlights the Mu'tazila's contradiction: they reject wahm when it leads to anthropomorphism but accept it when denying ru'ya.
This inconsistency is the key point.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that denial of ru'ya must be based on proof, not on a claim of necessity. He also explains why he introduced this argument: to preempt the Mu'tazila's last resort of claiming necessity.
This shows his strategic approach to the debate.

Correction: Razi shows their position is contradictory: they accept and reject wahm selectively.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's third proof, what is contradictory about the Mu'tazila's use of wahm?
A) They reject wahm entirely in all matters.
B) They accept wahm in affirming direction but reject it in denying ru'ya.
C) They reject wahm in affirming direction but accept it in denying ru'ya.
D) They have no evidence for their position.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's reliance on wahm is contradictory; denial of ru'ya requires rational proof, not claims of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you accepted an argument because it supported your position, but rejected the same type of argument when it led to a conclusion you disliked. How does Razi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's inconsistency challenge you to be more intellectually honest? What will you do the next time you catch yourself doing this?
?
The Mu'tazila fell back on 'necessity' when their reasoning failed. In your own life, where do you rely on comfortable assumptions rather than examining evidence? Are you willing to search for proof even when it might unsettle you? Commit to one area where you will seek deeper understanding.
?
If the judgment of your own mind (like wahm) is unreliable in some matters, how carefully do you test your beliefs? This lesson warns against selective reasoning. Will you surrender your biases to the truth, even if it means admitting you were wrong? What step will you take today?

The Ash'arite Proof from Existence for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Imam Razi presents the majority Ash'arite proof that existence is the cause for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite argument that existence itself, not origination, is the basis for the possibility of seeing Allah, and the reasoning behind excluding non-existence from the cause.

Core Idea
Existence alone, not origination, makes vision possible.

Imam al-Razi presents the classical Ash'arite proof for the possibility of seeing Allah based on the common attribute of existence. He first lays out the argument as formulated by the majority of Ash'arite theologians, then adds his own position that he cannot sustain it. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure
Notice that this is a proof by commonality: it observes a shared property (visibility) among different kinds of things and seeks a common cause.
The proof is presented by the majority, not necessarily endorsed by Razi.
2
Identify the two candidates
The common factor between substances and colors is either origination (huduth) or existence (wujud). These are the only possibilities.
3
Reject origination
Origination includes non-existence, which cannot be part of a cause. Therefore, it cannot be the cause of visibility.
Non-existence is pure negation and cannot produce an effect.
4
Conclude with existence
Since existence remains, and it is shared by all beings including Allah, His existence is a valid cause for the possibility of seeing Him.
Wherever the cause (existence) is found, the judgment (visibility) follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في حكاية ما قيل في هذه المسألة من الدلائل العقلية وذكر المباحث فيها اعلم أن جمهور الأصحاب عولوا في إثبات أنه تعالى يصح أن يرى على دليل الوجود، وأما نحن فعاجزون عن تمشيه ونحن نذكر ذلك الدليل ثم نوجه عليه ما عندنا من الاعتراضات. علة، قالوا : ثبت أن الجوهر يصح أن يرى، واللون يصح أن يرى، والجواهر والألوان تشتركان في صحة الرؤية، وهذه الصحة حكم حادث، فلا بد لها من علة، والحكم المشترك يجب تعليله بعلة مشتركة لامتناع الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة، والمشترك بين الجواهر والأعراض إما الحدوث وإما الوجود، لا جائز أن تكون علة هذه الصحة الحدوث ؛ لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود حاصل، وعدم سابق، والعدم لا يجوز أن يكون جزءًا من المقتضي، وإذا سقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الوجود والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب، فإذن وجود الله تعالى علة صالحة لصحة رؤيته، وإذا حصلت العلة حصل الحكم لا محالة، فوجب القول بصحة رؤيته هذا حاصل الكلام في هذا الباب.
Chapter Two: Narrating what has been said on this issue of intellectual proofs and mentioning the investigations therein. Know that the majority of the companions relied in proving that He, the Most High, can be seen on the proof of existence. As for us, we are unable to sustain it, and we will mention that proof, then present against it the objections we have. They said: It is established that the substance (jawhar) can be seen, and the color can be seen. Substances and colors share in the validity of vision (sihhat al-ru'ya). This validity is an originated (hadith) judgment, so it must have a cause. The common judgment must be attributed to a common cause, because equivalent judgments cannot be due to different causes. The common factor between substances and accidents is either origination (huduth) or existence (wujud). It is not possible that the cause of this validity is origination, because origination is an expression of attained existence and preceding non-existence, and non-existence cannot be part of the determinant. When non-existence falls from consideration, nothing remains except existence, and existence is common between the witnessed and the absent. Therefore, the existence of Allah the Most High is a valid cause for the validity of seeing Him. When the cause is present, the judgment necessarily occurs, so it is necessary to affirm the validity of seeing Him. This is the sum of discourse on this topic.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the classical Ash'arite proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, based on the attribute of existence. Imam al-Razi begins the second chapter by stating that the majority of Ash'arite scholars relied on the 'proof of existence' (dalil al-wujud) for affirming that Allah can be seen. He admits that he himself is unable to sustain this proof but will present it and then raise objections. The proof proceeds as follows: It is known that substances (like atoms) and colors can be seen. The 'validity of vision' (sihhat al-ru'ya) is a common property shared by these different kinds of entities. This validity is an originated attribute (hukm hadith), meaning it comes into being when the object exists. Therefore, it must have a cause. Since different kinds of objects share the same judgment (visibility), the cause must be something common to them. The common factor is either origination (huduth) or existence (wujud). Origination cannot be the cause because origination includes both existence and preceding non-existence, and non-existence cannot be a part of a cause (since non-existence is pure negation). Therefore, non-existence is excluded from consideration. What remains is existence. Existence is shared by all objects in the witnessed world and also by Allah (the unseen). Therefore, Allah's existence is a valid cause for the possibility of seeing Him. Wherever the cause (existence) is found, the judgment (possibility of vision) must also be present. Hence, it is necessary to affirm that Allah can be seen. The author notes that this is the gist of the argument.

Key Terms
صحة الرؤية
علة
حدوث
وجود
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing something requires it to be in a specific direction or location.
Correction: The Ash'arite proof shows that visibility is caused by existence alone, not by spatial properties.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'arite proof reject origination (huduth) as the cause for visibility?
A) Because origination does not apply to Allah.
B) Because origination includes non-existence, which cannot be a cause.
C) Because origination is not common to all visible things.
D) Because origination is an accidental attribute.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah is logically grounded in His existence, as existence is the common cause of visibility for all beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's very existence makes seeing Him possible, does that change how you long to see Him? In what specific moments today can you remind yourself that His existence—not your merit—makes that vision attainable?
?
The proof removes non-existence from the cause. When you feel distant from Allah, is it because you focus on your own spiritual 'non-existence'? What concrete step can you take today to shift your focus to His ever-present existence?
?
Every visible thing shares existence with Allah. Choose one thing you will see today and let it serve as a proof that seeing the Creator is possible. What will that be, and how will you use it to increase your longing for the vision of Allah?

The First Objection: Validity as Non-Existent
Imam Razi presents the first objection: validity of vision is a non-existent judgment, thus uncausable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector claims that the validity of vision is a non-existent judgment and why this precludes causation.

Core Idea
Validity is a privative concept, so it cannot be caused.

Imam al-Razi presents the first objection to the Ash'arite proof that vision of Allah is possible. The objector argues that 'validity' (sihha) itself is a non-existent (privative) judgment, not a positive attribute. Since the validity of vision is a specific kind of validity, it too is non-existent and therefore cannot be the effect of a cause. This passage lays out two arguments to show that validity is not an affirmative state, setting the stage for a key debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an initial claim, followed by two proofs, then a conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two proofs
The objector gives two reasons why validity is non-existent: (1) from proofs about bodily origination, (2) from a reductio about the world's pre-eternity. Locate each.
The second proof is a conditional argument: if validity were existent, then...
3
Understand the conclusion
The objector concludes that since validity is not established, the validity of vision is also not established, and thus cannot be caused. Ask yourself: why does non-existence preclude causation?
Causation requires two existent things; pure negation cannot be a cause or effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول : صحة الرؤية حكم عدمي، والحكم العدمي لا يجوز تعليله وإنما قلنا : إن صحة الرؤية حكم عدمي لأن الصحة حكم عدمي، وإذا كانت الصحة أمرًا عدميًا كانت صحة الرؤية أمرًا عدميًا.
وإنما قلنا : الصحة أمر عدمي لوجهين :
الأول : الدلائل الكثيرة المذكورة في مسألة حدوث الأجسام على أن الصحة والإمكان يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة
الثاني : أن الصحة لو كانت صفة موجودة فلا شك أن العالم قبل وجوده صحيح الوجود، وكانت تلك الصحة صفة موجودة أيضاً، فيستدعي موصوفًا موجودًا، وذلك يوجب القول بقدم العالم، وهو محال فثبت أن الصحة ليست ثابتة، ولا حالة ثبوتية البتة، وإنما قلنا : إن الصحة لما لم تكن حكمًا ثابتا امتنع كون صحة الرؤية حالة ثابتة وذلك لأن صحة الرؤية صحة مخصوصة بكيفية مخصوصة، ولما كان أصل الصحة غير ثابتة امتنع أن تكون كيفيتها وصفتها ثابتة لامتناع قيام الثابت بالنفي المحض، فثبت أن صحة الرؤية ليست صفة ثابتة ولا حكمًا ثابتا ، وإذا ثبت هذا امتنع تعليل هذه الصحة ؛ لأن التعليل عبارة عن تأثير أمر في أمر، والعدم نفي محض وسلب صرف فيمتنع أن يكون علة ومعلولا.
The first question: The validity of vision (sihhat al-ru'ya) is a non-existent ('adami) judgment, and a non-existent judgment cannot be caused. We only say that the validity of vision is a non-existent judgment because validity (sihha) is a non-existent judgment. If validity is a non-existent matter, then the validity of vision is a non-existent matter. We only say that validity is a non-existent matter for two reasons: The first: The many proofs mentioned in the issue of the origination of bodies that validity and possibility cannot be existent attributes. The second: If validity were an existent attribute, then without doubt the world before its existence had validity of existence (sahih al-wujud), and that validity would also be an existent attribute, requiring an existent subject; that would necessitate the pre-eternity of the world, which is impossible. So it is established that validity is not established, nor any affirmative state whatsoever. We say: Since validity is not an established judgment, it is impossible for the validity of vision to be an established state, because the validity of vision is a specific validity with a specific modality, and since the root of validity is not established, its modality and attribute cannot be established, because it is impossible for the established to subsist in pure negation. So it is established that the validity of vision is neither an established attribute nor an established judgment. When this is established, it is impossible to cause this validity, because causation is the influence of one thing on another, and non-existence is pure negation and sheer privation, so it is impossible for it to be cause or effect.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the Ash'arite proof, arguing that the 'validity of vision' (sihhat al-ru'ya) is a non-existent (non-affirmative) judgment and therefore cannot be an effect of a cause. The objector begins by asserting that validity is a 'adami' (privative) judgment, and such judgments cannot be caused. The reasoning is that 'validity' (sihha) itself is non-existent, and since the validity of vision is a specific kind of validity, it shares that non-existence. Two proofs are given for the claim that validity is non-existent: First, the arguments from the issue of the origination of bodies demonstrate that validity and possibility cannot be existent attributes. Second, if validity were existent, then the world before its existence would have had 'validity of existence', which would be an existent attribute requiring an existent subject; that would imply the pre-eternity of the world (since the subject would be eternal), which is impossible. Therefore, validity is not an affirmative state (hala thubutiyya). Since the root (asl) of validity is not established, its specific modality (kayfiyya) also cannot be established—because one cannot have a positive attribute (thabit) subsisting in pure negation. Thus, the validity of vision is not an established attribute or judgment. Since it is not established, it cannot be caused, because causation involves the influence of one thing on another, and non-existence is pure negation, which cannot be a cause or effect.

عدم
تعليل
صفة موجودة
حكم عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may assume that 'validity' (sihha) is a positive, existent attribute like other attributes.
Correction: The objection clarifies that validity is a privative (non-existent) judgment, akin to possibility (imkan), which is not an affirmative state.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why would the world's pre-eternity follow if validity were an existent attribute?
A) Because the world before existence would have an existent attribute requiring an eternal subject.
B) Because validity is a necessary attribute of the world.
C) Because the world is eternal by nature.
D) Because existence and validity are the same.

Key Takeaway
The validity of vision is a non-existent judgment, and non-existent judgments cannot be caused, because causation requires two existing things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your belief in the possibility of seeing Allah might rest on a privative concept? How does this argument challenge you to examine the foundations of your hope?
?
If non-existence cannot be caused, what does that imply for your own spiritual states—like the 'absence' of sin or the 'void' of doubt? Are you treating these as positive realities that can be changed by your actions alone?
?
This objection forces you to think rigorously about cause and effect. When you feel distant from Allah, do you assume that distance is a 'thing' that can be removed? How might this passage reshape your understanding of spiritual transformation?

Second Objection: Not All Judgments Require a Cause
Imam Razi presents the second objection arguing that not every judgment requires a cause, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection challenges the principle that every judgment must have a cause, using examples of non-existent judgments and shared power judgments.

Core Idea
Not every judgment needs a cause—some are inherent to their subject.

After the first objection, Imam Razi presents a deeper challenge. The objector grants that the validity of vision is a judgment but questions why all judgments must be caused. This lesson unpacks the two key counterexamples: knowability of non-existents and the shared judgment of powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the objector's argument come through before you analyze.
The passage is incomplete; it breaks off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the main claim being challenged
Notice that the objector first makes a conditional concession ('Granted that...'), then attacks the principle that every judgment must have a cause.
The principle is from the earlier Ash'arite proof.
3
Understand the first example: knowability of non-existents
The objector says judgments like 'being knowable' apply to non-existent things, yet they are not caused because non-existence cannot be an effect.
This shows the principle is not universal.
4
Understand the second example: powers and creation
The objector argues that powers in the created world share the judgment of being unable to create a body, but this judgment cannot be attributed to being powers (else God's power would also be unable) nor to being originated (as origination is not a valid cause per the Ash'arites).
This further undermines the need for a cause for every shared judgment.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If some judgments do not require causes, what does that mean for the Ash'arite proof about the validity of seeing Allah?
This objection aims to block the proof's second premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : هب أن صحة الرؤية حكم ثابت فلم قلتم : إن كل حكم فإنه يجب تعليله، والدليل عليه اتفاق المتكلمين على أن من الأحكام ما يعلل، ومنها ما لا يعلل، ولذلك فإن صحة المعلومية والمذكورية والمخبرية لا تعلل ؛ لأن هذه الأحكام ثابتة المعدومات، والعدم لا يصلح للعلية - على ما قررتموه في دليلكم - وأيضا : القُدَرُ في الشاهد لا تصلح لخلق الجسم فهي مشتركة في هذا الحكم، ولا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بكونها قُدَرًا، وإلا لزم أن لا تصلح القدرة القديمة لخلق الجسم ولا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بكونها قدرًا حادثة ؛ لأنكم في دليلكم أن الحدوث لا يصلح
The second question: Granted that the validity of vision is an established judgment, why do you say that every judgment must be caused? The evidence against it is the consensus of the theologians (mutakallimun) that among judgments some are caused and some are not. For this reason, the validity of being known (ma'lumiyya), being mentioned (madhkuriyya), and being informed about (mukhbaariyya) are not caused, because these judgments are established for non-existents, and non-existence is not fit for causality—according to what you established in your proof. Also, powers (qudar) in the witnessed world are not fit to create a body, yet they share in this judgment (of not creating a body). This judgment cannot be attributed to them being powers, for then it would follow that the eternal power (al-qudra al-qadima) would not be fit to create a body. Nor can this judgment be attributed to them being originated powers, because in your proof you [argued] that origination is not fit...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the Ash'arite proof. The objector makes a conditional concession: even if we grant that the validity of vision is an established (positive) judgment, why must every judgment be caused? The objector argues that there is consensus among theologians that some judgments are caused and some are not. For example, the judgments of being knowable (ma'lumiyya), being mentionable (madhkuriyya), and being informable (mukhbaariyya) apply to non-existent things (ma'dumat), and these judgments are not caused because non-existence cannot be an effect (or cause). This undermines the claim that every originated judgment requires a cause. Additionally, the objector brings another example: in the physical world, powers (qudar, i.e., abilities) are not capable of creating a body, so they share the judgment of being unable to create. This common judgment cannot be attributed to them being powers per se, because then it would imply that God's eternal power would also be unable to create a body, which is false. Nor can it be attributed to them being originated powers, because the Ash'arites themselves argue that origination (huduth) is not a valid cause (as seen in earlier proofs). Therefore, the principle that every shared judgment must have a shared cause is not universally true. The objection cuts off mid-sentence at the page break, but the argument is clear.

قدرة
علة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all judgments require causes, but the objection shows that some judgments (like knowability) are inherent to the subject and not caused.
Correction: The objector demonstrates that judgments applying to non-existents and shared power judgments do not require a cause, challenging the universality of the principle.

Checkpoint
What specific counterexample does the objector give to show that not all judgments need a cause?
A) The judgment of being created.
B) The judgment of being visible.
C) The judgment of being knowable for non-existents.
D) The judgment of being powerful.

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that the principle 'every judgment must have a cause' is not universally true, as evidenced by judgments applying to non-existents and shared power judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The First Objection: Vision's Possibility as a Non-Existent Judgment
First objection: vision's possibility as a non-existent judgment and uncausable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector argues that the possibility of seeing Allah is a non-existent judgment and how this challenges the causation of that possibility.

Core Idea
If possibility itself is not a real attribute, then the possibility of vision cannot be caused.

In this lesson, we examine the first objection raised against the proof that Allah's existence is a sufficient cause for the possibility of seeing Him. The objector contends that 'possibility' (sihha) is not a real, existing attribute but rather a non-existent judgment (hukm 'adami). This distinction is crucial because only existing things can be causes or effects, so if possibility is non-existent, it cannot be caused by anything.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
The objector states that the possibility of vision is a non-existent judgment. Grasp this as the foundational premise.
Notice that the claim is about the nature of possibility itself.
2
Follow the first proof
The objector cites proofs from the temporal origination of bodies that possibility cannot be an existing attribute.
These proofs are referenced but not detailed here.
3
Follow the second proof
If possibility were existing, then before the world existed, its possibility would require an existing subject, leading to the pre-eternity of the world, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Connect to vision's possibility
Since general possibility is non-existent, the specific possibility of vision is also non-existent. Therefore, it cannot be caused.
Causation involves influence between existing things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول: السؤال الأول: صحة الرؤية حكم عدمي، والحكم العدمي لا يجوز تعليله وإنما قلنا: إن صحة الرؤية حكم عدمي لأن الصحة حكم عدمي، وإذا كانت الصحة أمرًا عدميًا كانت صحة الرؤية أمرًا عدميًا. وإنما قلنا: الصحة أمر عدمي لوجهين: الأول: الدلائل الكثيرة المذكورة في مسألة حدوث الأجسام على أن الصحة والإمكان يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة. الثاني: أن الصحة لو كانت صفة موجودة فلا شك أن العالم قبل وجوده صحيح الوجود، وكانت تلك الصحة صفة موجودة أيضاً، فيستدعي موصوفًا موجودًا، وذلك يوجب القول بقدم العالم، وهو محال فثبت أن الصحة ليس ثابتة، ولا حالة ثبوتية البتة، وإنما قلنا: إن الصحة لما لم تكن حكمًا ثابتا امتنع كون صحة الرؤية حالة ثابتة وذلك لأن صحة الرؤية صحة مخصوصة بكيفية مخصوصة، ولما كان أصل الصحة غير ثابتة امتنع أن تكون كيفيتها وصفتها ثابتة لامتناع قيام الثابت بالنفي المحض، فثبت أن صحة الرؤية ليست صفة ثابتة ولا حكمًا ثابتا، وإذا ثبت هذا امتنع تعليل هذه الصحة؛ لأن التعليل عبارة عن تأثير أمر في أمر، والعدم نفي محض وسلب صرف فيمتنع أن يكون علة ومعلولا.
And the objector may say: The first question: The possibility of vision is a non-existent judgment, and a non-existent judgment cannot be caused. And we only say: the possibility of vision is a non-existent judgment because possibility is a non-existent judgment. And if possibility is a non-existent thing, then the possibility of vision is a non-existent thing. And we only say: possibility is a non-existent thing for two reasons: First: the many proofs mentioned in the question of the temporal origination of bodies that possibility and capacity cannot be an existing attribute. Second: if possibility were an existing attribute, then undoubtedly the world before its existence was possible of existence, and that possibility was also an existing attribute, so it would require an existing subject, and that would necessitate the pre-eternity of the world, which is impossible. So it is established that possibility is not a fixed thing, nor a fixed state at all. And we only say: since possibility is not a fixed judgment, it is impossible for the possibility of vision to be a fixed state, because the possibility of vision is a specific possibility with a specific modality. And since the origin of possibility is not fixed, it is impossible for its modality and attribute to be fixed, due to the impossibility of the fixed subsisting in pure negation. So it is established that the possibility of vision is neither a fixed attribute nor a fixed judgment. And when this is established, it is impossible to cause this possibility, because causation is the effect of one thing on another, and non-existence is pure negation and absolute privation, so it is impossible for it to be a cause or an effect.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the previous proof that God's existence is a sufficient cause for the possibility of seeing Him. The objector argues that the possibility (sihha) of vision is a non-existent judgment (hukm 'adami), and a non-existent judgment cannot be caused (ta'lil). The objector first establishes that sihha itself is non-existent by two proofs: (1) many proofs in the topic of the temporal origination of bodies show that possibility and capacity cannot be existing attributes; (2) if possibility were existing, then before the world existed, its possibility would have been an existing attribute requiring an existing subject, which would imply the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam), which is false. Therefore, sihha is not a fixed thing. Since the general possibility is non-existent, the specific possibility of vision is also non-existent. A non-existent thing cannot be a cause or effect, so the argument that God's existence causes vision's possibility fails. The objector concludes that causation involves influence between existing things, and non-existence is pure negation.

Key Terms
صحة الرؤية (sihhat al-ru'ya)
حكم عدمي (hukm 'adami)
تعليل (ta'lil)
العدم (al-'adam)
صفة موجودة (sifa mawjida)
قدم العالم (qidam al-'alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (sihha) is a real positive attribute that exists in the mind or externally.
Correction: The objector argues that possibility is a non-existent judgment (hukm 'adami), not an existing attribute, based on proofs from kalam.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector claim that the possibility of vision (sihhat al-ru'ya) is a non-existent judgment?
A) Because vision itself is impossible.
B) Because possibility (sihha) is proven to be non-existent by proofs about bodies' origination and the pre-eternity of the world.
C) Because God cannot be seen.
D) Because the objector denies causation.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that since possibility is non-existent, the specific possibility of vision cannot be caused, thus challenging the proof that God's existence causes that possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility is not a real attribute, what is the foundation of your hope to see Allah in the afterlife? Is your hope built on a mere abstract possibility or on the concrete promise of Allah? Examine your heart: Does your longing to see Allah rest on logical argument or on the certainty of His promise?
?
The objection argues that non-existence cannot be caused. How does this challenge your assumption that God's power can bring about anything, even logically impossible things? Are you willing to submit to the limits of rational theology, or do you insist on a God who can do everything, even contradictions? What does this reveal about your conception of God?
?
The proof against possibility being an existing attribute uses a reductio that would lead to pre-eternity of the world, which is impossible. Reflect: Do you ever base your beliefs on what seems possible rather than on what is actually revealed? Are you guilty of letting your own sense of possibility define your theology rather than the Qur'an and Sunnah? How can you align your thinking with the teachings of Ahl al-Sunnah?

Objection: Not All Judgments Need a Cause
Second objection: not all judgments require a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why not all judgments require a cause, as shown by two counterexamples from the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Not every judgment needs a cause—even the mutakallimun agree.

This objection challenges the claim that every judgment must be caused (ta'lil). The objector presents two examples from agreed-upon principles: judgments about non-existents and shared judgments among powers. By analyzing these, we see that causation is not universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the objector structures the argument: granting the premise, then presenting counterexamples.
The objector starts with 'Suppose that...' (هب أن).
2
Identify the two examples
Find the two examples given: (1) judgments about non-existents (معدومات) like being known, mentioned, reported; (2) the shared judgment of powers (قدر) not creating bodies.
Each example uses the opponent's own principles.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why can't these judgments be caused? For non-existents, non-existence is not a cause. For powers, being a power or being originated fails as a cause.
This shows that the opponent's claim that all judgments need causes is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: هب أن صحة الرؤية حكم ثابت فلم قلتم: إن كل حكم فإنه يجب تعليله، والدليل عليه اتفاق المتكلمين على أن من الأحكام ما يعلل، ومنها ما لا يعلل، ولذلك فإن صحة المعلومية والمذكورية والمخبرية لا تعلل؛ لأن هذه الأحكام ثابتة للمعدومات، والعدم لا يصلح للعلية - على ما قررتموه في دليلكم - وأيضا: القدر في الشاهد لا تصلح لخلق الجسم فهي مشتركة في هذا الحكم، ولا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بكونها قدرًا، وإلا لزم أن لا تصلح القدرة القديمة لخلق الجسم ولا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بكونها قدرًا حادثة؛ لأنكم في دليلكم أن الحدوث لا يصلح أن يكون علة، ولا أن يكون جزء علة، فثبت أن هذا الحكم المشترك فيه بين القدرة غير معلل بشيء أصلا.
The second question: Suppose that the possibility of vision is a fixed judgment; why do you say that every judgment must be caused? The proof of this is the agreement of the mutakallimun that some judgments are caused and some are not. For that reason, the possibility of being known, mentioned, and reported is not caused, because these judgments are fixed for non-existents, and non-existence is not suitable for causality—according to what you established in your proof. Also: powers (qudar) in the witnessed world are not suitable for creating a body; they share this judgment. And it is impossible to cause this judgment by their being powers, otherwise the eternal power would also be unsuitable for creating a body. And it is impossible to cause this judgment by their being originated powers, because you in your proof [say] that temporal origination (huduth) is not suitable to be a cause nor a part of a cause. So it is established that this shared judgment among powers is not caused by anything at all.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which even if we grant that the possibility of vision is a fixed judgment, the opponent's claim that every judgment must be caused (ta'lil) is false. The objector gives two examples: (1) The possibility of being known, mentioned, or reported applies to non-existent things (ma'dumat), and these judgments are not caused because non-existence cannot be a cause—this agrees with the opponent's own principle that non-existence cannot be a cause. (2) In the witnessed world, all powers (qudar) share the judgment that they are not capable of creating a body. This shared judgment cannot be caused by the fact that they are powers, otherwise the eternal power (qudra qadima) would also be unable to create a body, which is false. It cannot be caused by their being originated (haditha), because the opponent holds that huduth cannot be a cause or part of a cause. Thus this shared judgment has no cause at all, proving that not all judgments require a cause.

Key Terms
تعليل (ta'lil)
حكم مشترك (hukm mushtarak)
قدر (qudra)
المعدومات (al-ma'dumat)
حدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All judgments must have a cause.
Correction: Some judgments, like those about non-existents or shared attributes, are not caused, as the mutakallimun agree.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, which of the following is an example of an uncaused judgment?
A) The possibility of vision.
B) The possibility of being known, mentioned, or reported for non-existents.
C) The creation of the body.
D) The eternal power creating a body.

Key Takeaway
Not all judgments require a cause; the examples of non-existents and shared powers demonstrate that some judgments are inherently uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that everything in your life must have a clear cause or reason, how does this lesson’s exposure of uncaused judgments challenge your need for control? Will you submit to the reality that some of God’s decrees simply have no explanation you can grasp?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God that you think is fully rational and causally necessary. Could it be that this belief, like the judgments on non-existents, is simply true without a cause? How does that possibility humble your intellect?
?
The shared inability of created powers to create a body is an uncaused judgment. Are there areas of your life where you keep striving to achieve something that only God can do? Will you finally accept your limitation and rely on His power alone?

Third Objection: Possibility of Vision Differs by Type
Third objection: possibility of vision differs by type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of seeing an object is not a single uniform judgment but differs by type, and how the objector refutes the claim of a single cause for vision.

Core Idea
The possibility of vision is not one-size-fits-all; it varies by the type of thing seen.

The third objection in Razi's argument about the beatific vision pushes deeper into the nature of possibility. The opponent conceded that possibility is a judgment, but the objector now denies that this judgment is the same for substances and accidents. This lesson unpacks the subtle distinction between genus and species and shows why different types of visibility require different causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice that the objector begins by conceding a point before launching the real challenge.
2
Understand the proof from non-interchangeability
The objector proves that the possibility of seeing blackness differs from seeing whiteness because they cannot be swapped: a substance can't be seen as blackness, and blackness can't be seen as a substance.
This is a key observation based on concrete examples.
3
Examine the anticipated counterargument
The objector considers the reply that all vision possibilities share the genus 'possibility of vision.' See how the objector acknowledges this but then counters.
The opponent tries to unify the judgment at the genus level.
4
Analyze the refutation using analogy
The objector uses the example of motion and rest, knowledge and power: they share a genus but have different causes. This shows that even if vision possibilities share a genus, they can have different causes.
This is the decisive point that dismantles the opponent's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: سلمنا أن صحة الرؤية حكم ثابت وإن كان حكمًا عدميًا، لكن الحكم العدمي يجب تعليله، لكن لا نسلم أن صحة الرؤية حكم مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض، وذلك لأن صحة كون السواد مرتبًا مخالفة لصحة كون البياض مرئياً، والدليل عليه أنه يمتنع قيام كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين مقام الأخرى، بدليل أن الجوهر يمتنع أن يرى سوادا، والسواد يمتنع أن يرى جوهرًا، ولو تساوت الصحتان لقامت كل واحدة منهما مقام الأخرى. لا يقال: هب ان هاتين الصحتين نوعان مختلفان في النوعية، لكنهما مشتركان في جنس واحد وهو كونه صحة الرؤية وهذا الحكم واحد بحسب الوحدة والجنسية، وحينئذ يعود التقريب. لأنا نقول: إنه لا نزاع في أن الأحكام المختلفة بحسب النوعية وإن كانت متحدة في الجنس فإنه يجوز تعليلها بعلل مختلفة في الماهية والدليل عليه أن المتحركية والساكنية والعالمية والقادرية وإن كانت مختلفة بحسب الوحدة النوعية لكنها متحدة بحسب الوحدة الجنسية، فإنها بأسرها صفات وأحوال، ثم إنها مع ذلك تعلل بعلل مختلفة في الماهية والحقيقة، فثبت أن هذا غير ممتنع.
The third question: We grant that the possibility of vision is a fixed judgment even if it is a non-existent judgment, but a non-existent judgment must be caused. However, we do not concede that the possibility of vision is a shared judgment between substance and accident. That is because the possibility of blackness being seen differs from the possibility of whiteness being seen. The proof of this is that it is impossible for each of these two possibilities to take the place of the other, evidenced by the fact that a substance cannot be seen as blackness, and blackness cannot be seen as a substance. And if the two possibilities were equivalent, each would take the place of the other. It cannot be said: Suppose these two possibilities are two different species, but they share a single genus, namely being the possibility of vision, and this judgment is one in terms of unity and genus, and then the argument returns. For we say: There is no dispute that judgments differing by species, even if they are united in genus, may be caused by causes different in essence. The proof of this is that motion and rest, knowledge and power, although they differ in terms of specific unity, are united in terms of generic unity, for they are all attributes and states. Then with that, they are caused by causes different in essence and reality. So it is established that this is not impossible.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which concedes that the possibility of vision is a fixed judgment but denies that it is a single shared judgment across all objects (substances and accidents). The objector argues that the possibility of seeing blackness is different from the possibility of seeing whiteness, because a substance cannot be seen as blackness, and blackness cannot be seen as a substance. If they were equivalent, they would be interchangeable. The objection anticipates a rejoinder: that they are different species but share the genus 'possibility of vision', so the genus is common. The objector refutes this by noting that different species within the same genus (e.g., motion and rest, knowledge and power) are caused by different causes in essence. Therefore, even if the possibility of vision is one genus, it can have different causes for different species, so the opponent cannot argue for a single cause for all vision possibilities.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
صحة الرؤية (sihhat al-ru'ya)
جنس (jins)
نوع (naw')
علة ('illa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of vision is a single uniform judgment that applies to all objects equally.
Correction: The objector argues that the possibility differs by type (species) and that different species can have different causes even if they share a genus.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector claim that the possibility of seeing blackness differs from the possibility of seeing whiteness?
A) Because blackness and whiteness are opposite colors.
B) Because a substance cannot be seen as blackness, and blackness cannot be seen as a substance.
C) Because vision is impossible for accidents.
D) Because the objector denies any shared genus.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing an object is not a single judgment but varies by type; objects of different species can have different causes even if they share a genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that the possibility of seeing blackness differs from seeing whiteness, consider: do you take for granted the differences in how you perceive the world? In what ways do you fail to recognize that each thing God created has its own unique reality and cause? How might this awareness change your gratitude for the blessing of sight?
?
The objector argues that judgments differing by species can have different causes. Have you ever assumed that all people are the same in their spiritual potential or that everyone's path to God is identical? In what ways have you judged others based on a generic category rather than recognizing the specific cause behind their state? How can this lesson help you see the unique divine wisdom in each individual's journey?
?
The examples of motion and rest, knowledge and power show that attributes of the same genus can have different essences. Consider your own attributes: your knowledge, your strength, your emotions. Are you trying to reduce them to one uniform cause, or do you acknowledge that each comes from Allah in a specific way? How can you better recognize the distinct signs of Allah in the diversity of your own experiences?

Similar Judgments May Have Different Causes
Fourth objection with two complete proofs and a third cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the argument for a single cause of vision: similar judgments can arise from different causes, as demonstrated by examples from difference and knowability.

Core Idea
Similar judgments do not require a single cause; different causes can produce the same effect.

Imam Razi now presents a fourth objection that directly attacks the assumption that similar judgments must have a single cause. The objector concedes that the possibilities of seeing a substance and an accident are similar, but asks: why can't they be caused by two different causes? Three proofs are offered, the first two of which are complete in this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening question carefully. It concedes similarity but denies the necessity of a single cause.
2
Analyze the first proof
Study the first proof: different things share equal concomitants, like the concept of difference itself.
This shows that shared properties do not imply a shared cause.
3
Analyze the second proof
Examine the second proof: blackness and whiteness have the same judgment (being knowable) but caused by their specific essences.
4
Notice the incomplete third proof
The third proof is cut off. It begins to discuss two essences sharing one aspect and differing in another.
The full objection may have been completed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: هب أن صحة رؤية الجوهر وصحة رؤية العرض حكمان متماثلان لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل الحكمين المتماثلين بعلتين مختلفتين؟ والذي يدل عليه وجوه: الأول: أن الأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لوازم متساوية، والدليل عليه أن المختلفين يشتركان في صحة الاختلاف فإنه كما أن هذا مخالف لذلك فذاك مخالف لهذا، فمسمى الاختلاف مشترك فيه وكذا القول في الضدين. الثاني: وهو أن السواد بخصوص كونه سوادًا يصح أن يكون معلوما، وكذا البياض بخصوص كونه بياضًا يصح أن يكون معلوماً، وهذه أحكام متساوية معللة بخصوصيات هذه الماهيات، مختلفة. الثالث: وهو أن الماهيتين إذا اشتركتا من وجه اختلفا من وجه آخر، ولا شك
The fourth question: Suppose that the possibility of seeing a substance and the possibility of seeing an accident are two similar judgments. Why is it not permissible to cause two similar judgments by two different causes? What indicates this are aspects: First: Different things are not prevented from sharing equal concomitants. The proof of this is that two different things share the validity of difference; just as this is different from that, so that is different from this. So the meaning of difference is shared, and likewise in the case of opposites. Second: Blackness, specifically by being blackness, is valid to be known, and similarly whiteness, specifically by being whiteness, is valid to be known. These are equal judgments caused by the specificities of these different essences. Third: Two essences, if they share from one aspect, they differ from another aspect, and there is no doubt...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection, which concedes even further that the possibilities are similar (mutamathilan) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. The objector provides three proofs (wujuh) in support. (1) Different things can share equal concomitants (lawazim mutasawiya); for example, two different things both share the property of being different from each other—the concept of difference is common. This shows that similarity of a judgment does not require a single cause. (2) Blackness and whiteness are both knowable, and this equal judgment (being knowable) is caused by their specific essences (khawass al-mahiyyat). Different causes produce the same judgment. (3) Starts mentioning that two essences share one aspect and differ in another, but the text is cut off. The objection is incomplete but clearly argues against the necessity of a single cause for similar judgments.

Key Terms
حكمان متماثلان
علتان مختلفتان
لازم
ماهية
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two judgments are similar, they must have the same cause.
Correction: The objector shows that similarity does not necessitate a single cause; different causes can produce similar judgments, as seen in the examples of difference and knowability.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof that similar judgments can have different causes?
A) Different things share the concept of difference.
B) Blackness and whiteness are both knowable due to their specific essences.
C) Two essences share one aspect and differ in another.
D) The objector denies that the judgments are similar.

Key Takeaway
Two similar judgments can be caused by different causes, as shown by the examples of difference and knowability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe two people performing the same outward action, do you instinctively assume their inner motives are identical? How might this lesson challenge you to stop and consider that different causes can produce similar effects—even in human behavior? What would it take for you to pause before judging someone's heart?
?
Think of a specific moment when you harshly judged someone because their action looked similar to a wrong action you knew from someone else. In light of this passage, how might you re-evaluate that judgment? What would it cost you to admit you may have been wrong?
?
The passage uses blackness and whiteness—both knowable but by different specificities. Can you identify an area in your own life where you have been trying to achieve a good outcome (like patience or generosity) but imitating the external form without addressing your unique inner cause? How can you instead seek the specific cause that fits your own essence?

The Fifth Objection: Possibility as a Common Attribute?
Razi responds to an objection about possibility as a common attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the concept of possibility (imkān) dialectically to challenge the claim that only temporality and existence are common attributes between substance and accident.

Core Idea
Possibility is mentioned to refute exclusivity, not to prove a cause.

This lesson explores the fifth objection in Imam Razi's Al-Arba'een. The opponent argues that possibility (imkān) may be another attribute common to substance and accident, alongside temporality and existence. Razi responds by clarifying his dialectical purpose—he invokes possibility only to refute the claim of exclusivity—and then refutes three objections that possibility cannot serve as a cause for visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the objector's statement, Razi's initial reply, three objections, and Razi's refutations.
Pay attention to the dialectical flow.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector asserts that possibility is a common attribute besides temporality and existence. He challenges the proof of exclusivity.
This sets up the debate.
3
Understand Razi's initial response
Razi states that he only mentioned possibility to refute the claim of exclusivity, not to establish it as a cause for visibility.
This is crucial for avoiding misunderstanding.
4
Examine the three objections and refutations
The objector gives three reasons why possibility cannot be a cause for visibility. Razi refutes each in turn. Follow the logical progression.
Notice how Razi turns the objector's arguments against them.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Consider why Razi uses a dialectical approach: he does not need to prove possibility is a cause; he only needs to show the opponent's exclusivity claim is unsupported.
This reflects a common kalām methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : هب أنه لا بد لهذا الحكم المشترك من علة مشتركة فيها، فلم قلتم : إنه لا مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض إلا الحدوث والوجود؟ وما الدليل على هذا الحصر ؟ وعدم العلم بالشيء لا يدل على عدم الشيء، والسبر والبحث لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف، ثم نقول: ههنا مشترك آخر ، وهو كون الشيء ممكن الوجود لذاته. لا يقال : الإمكان لا يصلح علة للرؤية، ويدل عليه وجوه. الأول : أن الإمكان عدمي، فلا يصلح للعلية. الثاني: أن الإمكان قائم في المعدومات، ولا يصح رؤيتها. الثالث : لو كان الإمكان علة للرؤية لزم صحة رؤية جميع الممكنات والخصم لا يقول به. لأنا نقول نحن إنما ذكرنا الإمكان للقدح في قولكم: إنه لا مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض إلا الحدوث والوجود، لا لبيان أن نجعله علة لصحة الرؤية. وأيضًا: فنحن نبحث عن الوجوه التي ذكرتم. أما الأول : فنقول : صحة الرؤية عبارة عن علة إمكان الرؤية، فإن كان الإمكان عدما كانت صحة الرؤية أيضًا عدمية، ولا يبعد تعليل حكم عدمي بعلة عدمية. وأما الثاني: فلم لا يجوز أن تكون صحة علة الرؤية للمجموع الحاصل من الإمكان والوجود ؟ وأما الثالث : فهو أن الخصم أخطأ في قوله : لا يمكن رؤية بعض الممكنات، ولكن لم يحصل لكم من هذا البرهان صحة رؤية الله تعالى.
The fifth question: Suppose that this common judgment must have a common cause; so why do you say that there is nothing common between substance and accident except temporality and existence? What is the proof of this exclusivity? Lack of knowledge of something does not indicate the non-existence of that thing, and investigation and research only yield weak conjecture. Then we say: There is another common thing here, which is the thing's being possible of existence in itself. It cannot be said: Possibility is not suitable as a cause for visibility, and there are proofs for this. First: Possibility is non-existent, so it is not suitable for causation. Second: Possibility subsists in non-existent things, and it is not valid to see them. Third: If possibility were the cause of visibility, it would necessitate the validity of seeing all possible things, but the opponent does not accept that. We say: We only mentioned possibility to refute your statement that there is nothing common between substance and accident except temporality and existence, not to show that we make it a cause for the validity of visibility. Moreover, we examine the points you mentioned. As for the first: We say the validity of visibility is the cause of the possibility of visibility; if possibility is non-existent, then the validity of visibility is also non-existent, and it is not unlikely to explain a non-existent judgment by a non-existent cause. As for the second: Why is it not permissible that the validity of visibility is for the composite resulting from possibility and existence? As for the third: The opponent erred in saying that one cannot see some possible things, but from this proof you do not obtain the validity of seeing God Almighty.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This is the fifth objection (السؤال الخامس). The objector challenges the claim that the only common attributes between substance and accident are temporality (ḥudūth) and existence (wujūd). He proposes possibility (imkān) as another common attribute. Although possibility might be objected to as a cause for visibility, the author clarifies that his purpose is not to make possibility a cause but merely to refute the exclusivity. He then refutes three objections against possibility: (1) possibility is non-existent but visibility is also a non-existent quality; (2) possibility could be combined with existence; (3) the opponent's claim that not all possibles are visible is mistaken, and this argument does not prove God's visibility.

Correction: Razi explicitly states he mentions possibility only to refute the claim of exclusivity, not to establish it as a cause.

?

?
The opponent's argument lacked proof of exclusivity. Where in your own life have you assumed something is exclusive without evidence? How will you change that assumption starting now?

Is Temporality Reducible to Existence? Imam Razi's Sixth Objection
Imam Razi argues that temporality is an attribute of existence, not a composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that temporality is a distinct attribute of existence, not reducible to existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
Temporality is an attribute of existence, not a composite of non-existence and existence.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection that attempts to collapse temporality into existence itself. The objector claims that since temporality is composed of non-existence and existence, dropping non-existence leaves only existence. Razi refutes this by clarifying the definition of temporality and showing that non-existence, being the opposite of existence, cannot be an attribute of it. This discussion is crucial for understanding the nature of contingent beings and their dependence on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's counter-definition
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the objector's claim that temporality is a composite and his reduction argument. Then Razi's response rejecting that definition.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بل الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الحاضر المسبوق بذلك العدم السابق'.
2
Analyze Razi's proof
Razi proves that temporality is an attribute (ṣifa) and quality (kayfiyya) of existence, while non-existence is its opposite. An opposite cannot be an attribute. Follow the logic.
This step is key to understanding why temporality is irreducible.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters for divine attributes: if temporality were just existence, then God's existence would entail temporality, which is impossible. Temporality is a mark of contingent beings.
Connect back to the broader theme of causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس : هب أنه لا مشترك إلا الحدوث والوجود فلم لا يجوز أن تكون العلة الحدوث؟ قوله : الحدوث ماهية مركبة من العدم السابق والوجود الحاضر، فإذا سقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الوجود الحاضر. نقول : لا نسلم أن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الحاضر والعدم السابق، بل الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الحاضر المسبوق بذلك العدم السابق، فلم قلتم : بأن كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم نفس العدم؟ والدليل عليه أن الحدوث صفة للوجود وكيفية له، والعدم نقيض الوجود ومناف له ونقيض الشيء لا يكون صفة له، ولا كيفية له.
The sixth question: Suppose there is nothing common except temporality and existence, so why is it not permissible that the cause be temporality? He says: Temporality is an essence composed of prior non-existence and present existence; when non-existence is dropped from consideration, nothing remains but present existence. We say: We do not grant that temporality is present existence and prior non-existence, rather temporality is present existence preceded by that prior non-existence. So why do you say that its being preceded by non-existence is itself non-existence? The proof of this is that temporality is an attribute of existence and a quality of it, while non-existence is the opposite of existence and contrary to it, and the opposite of a thing cannot be an attribute of it nor a quality of it.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This is the sixth objection (السؤال السادس). The objector asks why temporality (ḥudūth) itself cannot be the cause, given that it is composed of non-existence and existence. The target of the objection argues that if non-existence is removed, only existence remains, so temporality is reducible to existence. The author responds by rejecting this definition of temporality. He insists that temporality is not the mere composite but rather present existence preceded by prior non-existence. He proves that temporality is an attribute (ṣifa) and quality (kayfiyya) of existence, while non-existence is its opposite. An opposite cannot be an attribute of a thing. Therefore, temporality is a distinct attribute and not reducible to existence.

Correction: Temporality is an attribute of existence preceded by non-existence, not a composition; non-existence cannot be an attribute as it is the opposite of existence.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that temporality is an attribute of existence and non-existence is its opposite
C) By denying that non-existence exists
D) By claiming that temporality is a separate substance

Key Takeaway
Temporality is a real attribute of existence, not reducible to non-existence or existence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that temporality is not merely a combination of non-existence and existence, but an attribute that qualifies your very being. How does this reshape your understanding of your own mortality? Is your life a mere series of moments bookended by non-existence, or does time define who you are before God? Pause and ask yourself: Do I live as if each moment is a gift that qualifies my existence, or do I treat time as a resource to be spent without thought?
?
The proof shows that an opposite cannot be an attribute. In your spiritual life, have you ever tried to combine opposites—such as claiming love for God while harboring hatred for His creation? How might this lesson expose the inconsistency? Commit to examining one relationship today where you might be mixing opposite intentions.
?
Imam Razi insists that temporality is a real quality, not reducible to something else. Is there an aspect of your faith that you have reduced to mere components—like prayer as a checklist rather than an attribute of your relationship with God? What would change if you viewed that practice as a quality that permeates your existence with Him?
