Razi's Refutation of the Exclusive Common Cause Claim
Razi presents the fifth objection and responds with a multi-layered argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that only temporality and existence can be common causes for visibility, and how he defends possibility as a legitimate commonality.

Core Idea
Razi uses the opponent's own method to argue that possibility is a valid shared attribute.

In this passage, Imam Razi continues his defense of the Ash'ari position on the visibility of God. The Mu'tazila argue that visibility requires a common cause, and they claim only temporality and existence are shared by substance and accident. Razi challenges this exclusivity by pointing out that possibility (imkan) is also common, and he systematically dismantles their objections. This lesson walks through that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get the overall flow of the objection and response.
Notice the dialogical style: opponent claim, Razi counter, then three objections and responses.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Razi's initial counter
The opponent asserts that only temporality (ḥudūth) and existence (wujūd) are common between substance and accident. Razi counters by asking for proof of this exclusivity and offers possibility (imkān) as another commonality.
3
Examine the three objections against possibility
The opponent raises three objections: possibility is non-existent, it subsists in non-existents, and if it were a cause, all possibles would be visible. Read each objection carefully.
These objections test whether possibility can be a cause for vision.
4
Analyze Razi's three responses
Razi responds: he is not making possibility a cause, only using it to challenge the list; the validity of vision is non-existent, so a non-existent cause is fine; the cause could be the composite of possibility and existence; and the opponent's denial of visibility of some possibles does not disprove God's visibility.
Understand the strategic nuance: Razi is not committing to possibility as the actual cause.
5
Consolidate the argument's logic
Trace the dialectical flow: the opponent's exclusivity claim is refuted by showing another common attribute. Even if possibility fails as a cause, it suffices to break the opponent's premise.
This is a key argumentative move in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر
السؤال الخامس :
هب أنه لا بد لهذا الحكم المشترك من علة مشتركة فيها، فلم قلتم : إنه لا مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض إلا الحدوث والوجود؟ وما الدليل على هذا الحصر ؟ وعدم العلم بالشيء لا يدل على عدم الشيء، والسبر والبحث لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف، ثم نقول: ههنا مشترك آخر ، وهو كون الشيء ممكن الوجود لذاته.
لا يقال : الإمكان لا يصلح علة للرؤية، ويدل عليه وجوه.
الأول : أن الإمكان عدمي، فلا يصلح للعلية. الثاني: أن الإمكان قائم في المعدومات، ولا يصح. الثالث : لو كان الإمكان علة للرؤية لزم صحة رؤية جميع الممكنات والخصم لا يقول به.
لأنا نقول نحن إنما ذكرنا الإمكان للقدح في قولكم: إنه لا مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض إلا الحدوث والوجود، لا لبيان أن نجعله علة لصحة الرؤية. وأيضًا: فنحن نبحث عن الوجوه التي ذكرتم. أما الأول : فنقول : صحة الرؤية عبارة عن علة إمكان الرؤية، فإن كان الإمكان عدما كانت صحة الرؤية أيضًا عدمية، ولا يبعد تعليل حكم عدمي بعلة عدمية. وأما الثاني: فلم لا يجوز أن تكون صحة علة الرؤية للمجموع الحاصل من الإمكان والوجود ؟
وأما الثالث : فهو أن الخصم أخطأ في قوله : لا يمكن رؤية بعض الممكنات، ولكن لم يحصل لكم من هذا البرهان صحة رؤية الله تعالى.
The Nineteenth Issue. The Fifth Question: Grant that this common judgment must have a common cause, so why do you say that there is nothing common between substance and accident except temporality and existence? And what is the proof of this exclusivity? And lack of knowledge of something does not indicate the non-existence of something, and investigation and examination yield only weak conjecture. Then we say: There is another commonality here, which is that the thing is possible in existence in itself. It should not be said: Possibility is not suitable as a cause for vision, and there are indications for that. First: That possibility is non-existent, so it is not suitable for causality. Second: That possibility subsists in non-existent things, and it is not valid. Third: If possibility were a cause for vision, then the validity of seeing all possible things would be necessary, and the opponent does not say that. Because we say: We only mentioned possibility to challenge your statement that there is nothing common between substance and accident except temporality and existence, not to show that we make it a cause for the validity of vision. Also, we examine the aspects you mentioned. As for the first: We say: The validity of vision is an expression of the cause of the possibility of vision. If possibility is non-existent, then the validity of vision is also non-existent, and there is no improbability in explaining a non-existent judgment by a non-existent cause. As for the second: Why is it not permissible for the validity of the cause of vision to be for the composite of possibility and existence? As for the third: It is that the opponent erred in his statement that it is not possible to see some possible things, but you have not obtained from this proof the validity of seeing God Almighty.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) argues that for a common judgment (visibility) there must be a common cause. They claim the only commonalities between substance and accident are temporality and existence. Razi counters by pointing out that there is another commonality: possibility (imkan). The opponent anticipates objections against possibility being a cause: (1) possibility is non-existent, so cannot cause; (2) possibility inheres in non-existents, which are not subjects of vision; (3) if possibility were the cause, all possibles would be visible, which the opponent denies. Razi replies that he is not positing possibility as the cause, but only using it to refute the opponent's exclusive list. He then systematically answers the three objections: (1) the validity of vision is a non-existent attribute, so a non-existent cause suffices; (2) the cause might be the composite of possibility and existence; (3) the opponent is wrong that some possibles cannot be seen, but even if they were right, that does not prove God's visibility.

Correction: Razi only uses possibility to challenge the opponent's exclusive list, not as a positive cause for vision.

Key Takeaway
Razi skillfully refutes the Mu'tazilite exclusivity claim by introducing possibility as a shared attribute and neutralizing each objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen Razi challenge a deeply held assumption of his opponent. Is there a belief about God or His attributes that you have held without questioning, simply because it was taught to you? What would it take for you to honestly examine that belief today, and what might you discover?
?
Razi points out that lack of knowledge does not prove non-existence. When was the last time you assumed something about your own spiritual state (e.g., that you have no hidden pride or that your deeds are accepted) simply because you were unaware of evidence to the contrary? Will you ask Allah to reveal what you are blind to?
?
The opponent accused possibility of being insufficient, but Razi turned the tables. Have you ever felt that a small, 'insufficient' act of worship or obedience is not worth offering? What if, like possibility, it becomes the very thing that opens the door to seeing Allah's mercy? Will you commit to a small act of devotion today, trusting that power is not in the act but in Allah's acceptance?

Razi's Refutation: Temporality as an Attribute of Existence
Razi refutes the redefinition of temporality as a composite of existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that temporality is an attribute of existence and cannot be reduced to a composite including non-existence.

Core Idea
Temporality is an attribute of existence, not a mixture of existence and non-existence.

In this sixth objection, the opponent suggests that temporality could be the cause of visibility because it is the only commonality besides existence. They define temporality as a composite of non-existence and existence, arguing the cause reverts to existence. Razi rejects this definition and provides a precise alternative: temporality is present existence characterized by being preceded by non-existence. He then proves that non-existence cannot be an attribute of existence because it is its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and opponent's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opponent's definition: temporality as composite of non-existence and existence.
The opponent wants to reduce the cause to existence alone.
2
Identify Razi's redefinition
Razi says temporality is not a composite but 'present existence preceded by non-existence'. The 'being preceded' is key.
This distinguishes temporality from mere existence.
3
Understand the proof
Razi argues that non-existence is the opposite of existence, so it cannot be an attribute of existence. Attributes must be compatible with their subject.
Thus, temporality cannot include non-existence as a component.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for the cause of visibility. Temporality remains a distinct attribute, not reducible to existence.
The cause of visibility involves the attribute of being preceded, not existence alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس :
هب أنه لا مشترك إلا الحدوث والوجود فلم لا يجوز أن تكون العلة الحدوث؟ . قوله : الحدوث ماهية مركبة من العدم السابق والوجود الحاضر، فإذا سقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الوجود الحاضر.
قلنا : لا نسلم أن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الحاضر والعدم السابق، بل الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الحاضر المسبوق بذلك العدم السابق، فلم قلتم : بأن كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم نفس العدم؟ والدليل عليه أن الحدوث صفة للوجود وكيفية له،
والعدم نقيض الوجود ومناف له ونقيض الشيء لا يكون صفة له، ولا كيفية له.
The Sixth Question: Grant that there is no commonality except temporality and existence; so why is it not permissible for the cause to be temporality? His statement: Temporality is a composite of prior non-existence and present existence. So when non-existence falls from the degree of consideration, nothing remains but present existence. We say: We do not accept that temporality is an expression of present existence and prior non-existence. Rather, temporality is an expression of present existence that is preceded by that prior non-existence. So why do you say that its being preceded by non-existence is itself non-existence? And the proof of it is that temporality is an attribute of existence and a modality of it, and non-existence is the negation of existence and its opposite, and the opposite of a thing is not an attribute of it nor a modality of it.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a direct objection-and-answer. The opponent suggests that since temporality is the only commonality besides existence, it could be the cause of visibility. They define temporality as composite of prior non-existence and present existence, arguing that when non-existence is disregarded, only existence remains, so the cause reduces to existence. Razi rejects this definition: temporality is not the mere conjunction of existence and non-existence, but rather present existence characterized by being preceded by non-existence. The 'being preceded' is not the same as non-existence itself. He proves that non-existence cannot be an attribute of existence because it is the opposite; attributes must be compatible with their subject.

Key Terms
علة
حدوث
وجود
عدم
صفة
كيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality is a simple property or a composite of non-existence and existence.
Correction: Temporality is a relational attribute of existence: it is existence preceded by non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the definition of temporality as a composite of non-existence and existence?
A) Because temporality is an attribute of existence, not a composite
B) Because non-existence is not part of existence
C) Because temporality means existence preceded by non-existence
D) Because existence and non-existence cannot coexist

Key Takeaway
Temporality is an attribute of existence characterized by being preceded by non-existence, and non-existence itself cannot be an attribute of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you truly acknowledge that it was preceded by absolute non-existence? Or do you live as if you have always been and will always be? Let this lesson remind you that your existence is a gift, not a given. How will you cultivate gratitude for this gift today?
?
Razi warns against conflating opposites: non-existence cannot be an attribute of existence. In your own life, are there ways you try to mix incompatible things—like seeking spiritual growth while holding onto harmful habits? What 'composite' in your character needs to be redefined as a pure attribute of faith?
?
The opponent in this passage reduces temporality to a mere composite, stripping it of its true meaning. Are there areas in your understanding of Allah's attributes where you have subconsciously reduced them to human-like composites? Take time to repent from any anthropomorphic thoughts and affirm His transcendence.

The Tension in al-Ash'ari's View of Existence
Razi questions how al-Ash'ari can claim existence is common given his view that existence is essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal contradiction Razi exposes in al-Ash'ari's equation of existence with essence.

Core Idea
If existence is essence, then distinct essences imply distinct existences.

Razi now turns a previous argument on its head. He accepts for argument's sake that existence is the cause of visibility, but then challenges al-Ash'ari's claim that existence is a common description shared by God and contingent beings. The problem arises from al-Ash'ari's own view that existence is identical to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the conditional acceptance and the surprise Razi expresses.
The word 'هن' may be a scribal variant for 'هب' (grant).
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's position
Razi quotes al-Ash'ari's view: existence of a thing is its essence (dhāt) and reality (ḥaqīqa). Understand this equivalence.
3
Trace the argument
Follow how Razi derives the contradiction: since realities differ, existences must differ, so existence cannot be a single common description.
This is a reductio ad absurdum within al-Ash'ari's own system.
4
Reflect on the tension
Consider why this objection is significant for the broader debate on divine attributes and the nature of existence.
It challenges the very foundation of al-Ash'ari's theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأربعين في أصول الدين
السؤال السابع : هن أن العلة لهذه الصحة هي الوجود ولكن لم قلتم : إن الوجود أمر مشترك فيه بين الواجب والممكن ؟ والعجب أن عند أبي الحسن الأشعري وجود الشيء ذاته و حقيقته، فعلى هذا لما كانت الحقائق مختلفة في حقائقها وجب أن تكون مختلفة وجوداتها، ومع هذا القول كيف يمكنه أن يقول : الوجود وصف مشترك فيه ؟
The Book of Forty in the Principles of Religion. The Seventh Question: Grant that the cause of this validity is existence, but why do you say that existence is something common between the Necessary and the possible? And it is strange that according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the existence of a thing is its essence and reality. So on this basis, since realities are different in their realities, their existences must be different. And with this statement, how can he say that existence is a common description?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by Razi against al-Ash'ari's position. Razi accepts for argument's sake that existence is the cause of visibility, but questions how al-Ash'ari can maintain that existence is a common attribute between God (the Necessary) and contingent beings. The problem is that al-Ash'ari holds that existence is identical to the essence (dhāt) of a thing. Since essences are irreducibly distinct (e.g., the essence of God is different from the essence of a body), their existences must also be distinct. Thus saying 'existence' is a single common description shared by all existents contradicts the view that existence is essence.

The Eighth Objection: Receptive Substrate and Absence of Obstacles
Razi argues that cause alone is insufficient; substrate and absence of obstacles are required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere presence of a cause is insufficient for an effect, and why the opponent must also prove that the divine essence is receptive to visibility and that no obstacle prevents it.

Core Idea
A cause needs a receptive substrate and no obstacles to produce its effect.

In this passage, Razi challenges the assumption that if existence is the cause of visibility, then God must be visible. He introduces a crucial principle in causality: for any cause to produce its effect, the substrate must be receptive and obstacles must be absent. Through the example of life, he shows that a cause can exist without its typical effects if these conditions are not met. This objection places the burden of proof back on the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: a condition, an example, and a conclusion.
Pay attention to the logical flow: Razi first states the principle, then illustrates with life, then applies to the visibility argument.
2
Analyze the example of life
Consider how life is a cause of ignorance, desire, etc., yet these effects are absent in God. Ask yourself why.
Razi offers two possibilities: the divine essence is not receptive, or there are attributes that prevent these effects.
3
Apply the principle to visibility
Understand that even if existence is the cause of visibility, the same conditions apply: the substrate (God's essence) must be receptive and no obstacle present. The opponent has not proven these.
This shifts the burden of proof back to the opponent to show that God's essence is receptive and no obstacle exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثامن :
، هب أن الوجود علة لصحة الرؤية ولكن كما يعتبر في حصول الحكم حصون العلة المؤثرة يعتبر أيضًا حصول المحل القابل، ويعتبر فيه عدم المانع، ألا ترى أن الحياة علة الجهل والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة ثم الحياة حاصلة في ذات الله. وصحة هذه الأشياء غير حاصلة، إما لأن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الأحكام، أو لأنه قامت بتلك الذات صفات مانعة من تحقق هذه الأشياء. فثبت بهذا أن مع حصول العلة لا يتحقق الحكم إلا إذا ثبت أن المحل قابل. والمانع زائل، فلم قلتم إن خصوصية ذات الله تعالى قابلة لهذه الصحة ؟ ولم قلتم : إنه يوجد هناك ما يكون مانعا هذه الصحة ؟
The Eighth Question: Grant that existence is a cause for the validity of vision, but just as the conditions of the effective cause are considered in obtaining the judgment, so also the obtaining of the receptive substrate is considered, and the absence of the obstacle is considered. Do you not see that life is a cause of ignorance, desire, aversion, pain, and pleasure? Then life is present in the essence of God, and the validity of these things is not present, either because that specific essence is not receptive to these judgments, or because there are attributes in that essence that prevent the realization of these things. So it is established by this that with the presence of the cause, the judgment is not realized unless it is established that the substrate is receptive and the obstacle is removed. So why do you say that the specificity of the essence of God Almighty is receptive to this validity? And why do you say that there is something there that is an obstacle to this validity?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection by Razi against the claim that existence alone suffices for visibility. He introduces the principle that for any cause to produce its effect, three conditions are needed: (1) the cause must be present and effective, (2) the substrate must be receptive (qābil), and (3) there must be no obstacle (māni'). He illustrates with the example of life: life is present in God, but life's typical effects (ignorance, desire, etc.) do not occur in God because either the divine essence is not receptive to them or there are attributes that prevent them. Therefore, even if existence is the cause of visibility, it does not follow that God is visible unless it is shown that His essence is receptive and that no obstacle exists. The opponent has not proven these conditions.

ذات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the cause is present, the effect must follow regardless of other conditions.
Correction: A cause requires a receptive substrate and the absence of obstacles; otherwise, the effect may not occur.

Checkpoint

B) That the substrate must be receptive and obstacles absent
C) That life always produces the same effects
D) That God lacks life

Key Takeaway
The presence of a cause does not guarantee its effect; the substrate must be receptive and obstacles must be absent.

This lesson demands that you prove not just the existence of a cause, but also the receptivity of the subject and absence of obstacles. The Ninth Objection: Analogy with the Tactile Faculty
Razi uses the analogy of touch to argue that if existence is cause for vision, it should also allow God to be touched.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses an analogy with the sense of touch to argue against the claim that existence is the cause of perception.

Core Idea
If existence caused perception, God would be tangible – an absurdity.

Razi presents the ninth objection to the argument that existence is the cause of vision. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: applying the same logic to the sense of touch leads to the conclusion that God can be touched and tasted, which is absurd. This passage demonstrates the method of parallel reasoning to expose a flawed premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi sets up the analogy with touch.
The passage is a single objection with a clear argument.
2
Identify the two parts of the analogy
First, Razi argues that touch perceives substances (by dimensions) and accidents (by heat/cold). Then he applies the opponent's reasoning to show the absurd outcome.
3
Understand the reductio
The conclusion that God would be touched and tasted is absurd, so the premise that existence is the cause must be false. Recognize this as a refutation by parallel reasoning.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع :
من أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للجواهر والأعراض : أما أنها مدركة للجواهر فلأنا باللمس نميز الطويل والعريض والعميق، كما ببصرنا ندرك التفرقة بين هذه الأحوال، ولما دل ذلك على كون البصر مدركًا للجسم وجب أن يدل ذلك على كون اللمس مدركا للجسم، وأما أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للأعراض، فلأنا ندرك التفرقة بين الحار والبارد، وإذا ثبت أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للجواهر والأعراض اطرد الدليل الذي ذكرتم بتمامه، فيلزم أن تكون علة صحة اللمس هي الوجود والله تعالى موجود، فوجب أن يصح أن يكون ملموسًا مذوقا، والتزامه في غاية البعد.
The Ninth Question: That the tactile faculty perceives substances and accidents. As for its perceiving substances, we distinguish by touch length, width, and depth, just as by our sight we perceive the differentiation between these states. And since that indicates that sight perceives the body, it must indicate that touch perceives the body. As for the tactile faculty perceiving accidents, we perceive the differentiation between hot and cold. And if it is established that the tactile faculty perceives substances and accidents, then the proof you mentioned applies completely. So it would follow that the cause of the validity of touch is existence, and God Almighty exists, so it must be valid that He be touched and tasted, and that implication is extremely remote.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection that uses a reductio ad absurdum. Razi argues that the same reasoning the opponent uses for sight can be applied to touch: touch perceives both substances (by distinguishing dimensions) and accidents (by distinguishing hot and cold). If the cause of the validity of perception is existence, then existence should also be the cause for touch. Since God exists, He would be tangible and even tasteable. This conclusion is absurd, so the premise that existence is the cause of perception must be false. The argument is a parallel case to the one the opponent made for sight, thereby reducing their position to an unacceptable consequence.

جوهر
عرض
رؤية
لمس
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is seriously proposing that God can be touched or tasted.
Correction: Razi is using reductio ad absurdum: he shows that the opponent's logic leads to an absurd conclusion, which refutes their premise.

Checkpoint

A) That God can be seen
B) That God can be touched and tasted
C) That touch perceives only accidents
D) That existence is not a cause

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the analogy of touch to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to an absurd consequence, thereby refuting the premise that existence is the cause of perception.

Two Interpretations of Existence as Cause of Vision
Razi distinguishes two meanings of 'existence is the cause of vision' and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the claim that 'existence is the cause of vision' and why the first meaning is necessarily false.

Core Idea
Existence as cause of vision can mean seeing existence alone, or seeing essence — only the second is plausible.

In this tenth objection, Razi continues his critique of the Ash'ari argument for the vision of God. He takes the opponent's central claim — that existence is the cause of the validity of vision — and shows that it is ambiguous. By distinguishing two interpretations, he prepares the ground for a decisive refutation. Let us examine how he deconstructs the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage is incomplete but the core distinction is present.
2
Identify the two interpretations

3
Understand why the first interpretation is false
Razi states the first interpretation is 'absolutely false' (batil qatan). Why? Because if only existence per se is seen, then the visible object reduces to mere existence, not a specific thing. Think about what that would imply.
The full explanation of the second interpretation is cut off, but the refutation of the first is clear.

Seventh Objection: Existence Cannot Be Shared if Essences Differ
Razi objects that existence cannot be a shared cause for visibility if essences differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal inconsistency Ash'ari faces regarding the premise that existence is a shared attribute between God and contingent beings.

Core Idea
If existence equals essence, then distinct essences mean distinct existences.

Razi presents the seventh objection against the Ash'ari proof that existence is the cause for the validity of seeing God. Even if we grant that existence is the cause, the objector asks on what basis existence is considered a shared attribute. The objection then exposes a contradiction within Ash'ari's own metaphysical framework, where existence is identical to essence. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: the objection begins with a supposition (هب أن) and then presents a logical problem.
The word 'عجب' signals a contradiction.
2
Understand Ash'ari's identity thesis
According to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the existence of a thing is identical to its essence (ذاته) and reality (حقيقته). This means there is no distinction between what a thing is and that it is.
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine.
3
Trace the logical consequence
If essences are different, then existences must be different. So existence cannot be a single shared quality across all beings.
This directly undermines the claim that existence is a shared cause.
4
Reflect on the objection's force
The objection does not only deny the shared nature but also shows an internal inconsistency in Ash'ari's system. Ask yourself: can Ash'ari consistently hold both positions?
This is a sophisticated critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع : هب أن العلة لهذه الصحة هي الوجود ولكن لم قلتم : إن الوجود أمر مشترك فيه بين الواجب والممكن ؟ والعجب أن عند أبي الحسن الأشعري وجود الشيء ذاته وحقيقته، فعلى هذا لما كانت الحقائق مختلفة في حقائقها وجب أن تكون مختلفة وجوداتها، ومع هذا القول كيف يمكنه أن يقول : الوجود وصف مشترك فيه ؟

And the wonder is that according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the existence of a thing is its essence and reality. So on this basis, since realities differ in their realities, their existences must differ. And with this statement, how can he say that existence is a shared attribute?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection against the proof that existence is the cause for the validity of seeing God. The objector asks: even if we grant that existence is the cause, why do you claim that existence is a shared attribute between God and contingent beings? The objector points out an internal inconsistency in Ash'ari's own position: if, as al-Ash'ari holds, the existence of a thing is identical to its essence, then since essences are distinct, existences must also be distinct. Therefore existence cannot be a shared universal attribute. The objection thus challenges the premise that existence is one and the same in all existents.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The objection actually concedes the cause for argument and focuses on the premise that existence is a shared attribute.

C) Existence is a shared attribute only among contingent beings.
D) The objection denies that existence is a cause at all.

Key Takeaway
Razi's seventh objection reveals a potential inconsistency within Ash'ari's own system: if existence is essence, then distinct essences preclude a shared existence.

Ninth Objection: Touch Perceives Substance and Accident
Razi uses the sense of touch to argue that if existence is the cause, God could be tangible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses an analogy between sight and touch to expose an absurd consequence of the proof that existence is the cause for perception.

Core Idea
If existence makes God seeable, then He would also be tangible and tasteable.

In the ninth objection, Razi extends the earlier causal reasoning from sight to the sense of touch. The objector argues that if the validity of perception is caused by existence, then the same must hold for all senses. By showing this leads to the unacceptable conclusion that God could be touched and tasted, the objection forces a reconsideration of the original proof. Let's examine how this analogy works step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the structure: it first asserts that touch perceives both substances and accidents, then draws the parallel to sight.
Look for the key phrase 'فيلزم أن تكون علة صحة اللمس هي الوجود'.
2
Identify the two parts of the claim
The objection is built on two sub-claims: touch perceives substances (dimensions) and touch perceives accidents (heat/cold). Identify the evidence given for each.
For substances, the evidence is distinguishing length, width, depth by touch.
3
Understand the logical deduction
Once the two sub-claims are established, the objection applies the same causal rule: the cause is existence, so God must be tangible and tasteable.
The objection concludes that this consequence is 'extremely remote' (في غاية البعد).
4
Connect to the larger debate
Reflect on why this objection is problematic for the original argument. If the opponent cannot accept that God is tangible/tasteable, then their own proof must be flawed.
The objection does not disprove the original proof directly but shows an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع : أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للجواهر والأعراض : أما أنها مدركة للجواهر فلأنا باللمس نميز الطويل والعريض والعميق، كما ببصرنا ندرك التفرقة بين هذه الأحوال، ولما دل ذلك على كون البصر مدركًا للجسم وجب أن يدل ذلك على كون اللمس مدركا للجسم، وأما أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للأعراض، فلأنا ندرك التفرقة بين الحار والبارد، وإذا ثبت أن القوة اللامسة مدركة للجواهر والأعراض اطرد الدليل الذي ذكرتم بتمامه، فيلزم أن تكون علة صحة اللمس هي الوجود والله تعالى موجود، فوجب أن يصح أن يكون ملموسًا مذوقا، والتزامه في غاية البعد.
The ninth question: That the sense of touch perceives substances and accidents. As for its perception of substances, it is because by touch we distinguish the long, the wide, and the deep, just as by our sight we perceive the distinction between these states. And since that indicates that sight perceives the body, it must indicate that touch perceives the body. And as for the sense of touch perceiving accidents, it is because we perceive the distinction between hot and cold. And if it is established that the sense of touch perceives substances and accidents, then the proof you have mentioned applies completely, so it would follow that the cause of the validity of touch is existence, and God Almighty exists, so it must be valid that He be tangible and tasteable, and that is extremely remote.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection, which uses an analogy from the sense of touch to argue ad absurdum. The objector begins by asserting that the sense of touch perceives both substances (the three dimensions) and accidents (heat, cold, etc.). Evidence for perceiving substances comes from differentiating length, width, and depth by touch, similar to vision. Evidence for perceiving accidents comes from distinguishing hot and cold. Once it is established that touch perceives both substance and accident, the same causal reasoning used for sight can be applied: the cause for the validity of touch would be existence. Since God exists, He must be tangible and even tasteable. This conclusion is clearly unacceptable, thus the original proof must be flawed. The objection shows that the argument from existence leads to absurd consequences if applied consistently to other senses.

Key Terms
قوة لامسة (sense of touch)
جواهر (substances)
أعراض (accidents)
لمس (touch)
ملموس (tangible)
مذوق (tasteable)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection only applies to the sense of sight.
Correction: The objection intentionally generalizes to touch and taste to show the absurdity of the original causal reasoning, demonstrating that if existence were the cause for sight, it would have to be the cause for all senses.

Checkpoint
What absurd conclusion does the ninth objection derive from the proof?
A) That God cannot be seen.
B) That existence is not a cause.
C) That God would be tangible and tasteable by the same logic.
D) That touch is not a valid sense.

Key Takeaway
The ninth objection shows that if existence is the cause for perception, it would absurdly entail that God is tangible and tasteable, revealing a flaw in the original argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is true for created beings, it must also apply to God? What specific assumption in your own understanding of God might be similarly flawed? Will you commit to questioning it today?
?
The objector says the conclusion that God would be tangible and tasteable is 'extremely remote'. How does this remind you of the limits of your own reasoning about God? In what area of your faith do you need to humbly accept that God is beyond your senses?
?
If the argument for seeing God is invalid, how does that affect your longing to see Him? Does it make you trust more in God's wisdom rather than your own logic? What step will you take to cultivate that trust?

Two Interpretations of 'Existence as Cause'
Razi presents two meanings of the claim 'existence is the cause' and shows both are problematic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'existence is the cause of visibility' leads to two equally problematic interpretations, and how each undermines the proof for seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The claim that existence is the cause of visibility is ambiguous and leads to either skepticism or a broken proof.

Razi's tenth objection targets a key argument of those who deny the vision of Allah by attacking the ambiguous phrase 'existence is the cause.' He shows that this phrase can be understood in two ways, and both lead to unacceptable consequences. This lesson unpacks those interpretations and their flaws.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and note the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two interpretations labeled 'أما الأول' and 'أما الثاني'.
The objector is showing that the phrase is ambiguous.
2
Analyze the first interpretation and its skeptical consequence
Understand that if only existence is seen, and existence is shared by all existents, then all things would appear identical, making differentiation impossible. This leads to skepticism.
The term 'السفسطة' refers to sophistry/skepticism about knowledge of differences.
3
Analyze the second interpretation and its consequence
Under the second interpretation, the cause is existence plus quiddity (e.g., blackness). But then the cause is not a single shared thing, so the original proof that a shared attribute is the cause collapses.
The proof that existence is the cause was meant to be a single shared attribute.
4
Understand the conclusion: either way, the proof fails
Razi concludes that if the first interpretation is taken, skepticism follows; if the second, the proof falls entirely. Thus the original argument is unsound.
This is a classic 'either way' refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال العاشر : قولكم الوجود علة لصحة الرؤية يحتمل وجهين : أحدهما : أن يكون المراد منه أن يكون الوجود علة لصحة أن يرى الموجود فقط. والثاني: أن يكون المراد منه أن يكون الوجود علة لصحة أن ترى الماهية. أما الأول : فباطل قطعاً، وذلك لأن المرئي إذا كان هو الوجود فقط والوجود أمر مشترك فيه بين كل الموجودات كان المرئي لحس البصر أمرًا مشتركًا فيه بين كل الموجودات، فوجب أن لا يكون اختلاف المختلفات مدركًا بالبصر، وهذا دخول في السفسطة. وأما الثاني : فإنه يقتضي أن لا تكون علة صحة رؤية السواد هي الوجود فقط، بل أن تكون علة تلك الصحة هي مجموع كونه سوادًا، وكونه موجوداً، وعلة رؤية البياض هي مجموع كونه بياضاً، وكونه موجوداً، وعلى هذا التقدير يبطل القول بأن علة صحة الرؤية أمر مشترك فيه. والحاصل أنا إن حملنا الكلام على الوجه الأول كانت السفسطة لازمة، وإن حملناه على الوجه الثاني يسقط الدليل بالكلية.
The tenth question: Your statement that existence is the cause for the validity of seeing admits two interpretations: One is that what is meant is that existence is the cause for the validity that the existent is seen only. The second is that what is meant is that existence is the cause for the validity that the quiddity is seen. As for the first, it is absolutely false, because if what is seen is only existence, and existence is a thing shared among all existents, then what is seen by the sense of sight would be a thing shared among all existents, so it would be necessary that the differences of different things are not perceived by sight, and this is entry into skepticism. As for the second, it entails that the cause for the validity of seeing blackness is not existence alone, but the cause of that validity is the sum of its being blackness and its being existent, and the cause of seeing whiteness is the sum of its being whiteness and its being existent. On this assumption, the statement that the cause of the validity of seeing is a shared thing is invalid. The conclusion is that if we interpret the statement in the first way, skepticism is necessary, and if we interpret it in the second way, the proof falls entirely.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth objection, which examines the ambiguous statement that 'existence is the cause for the validity of seeing.' The objector distinguishes two possible meanings. The first interpretation: the cause is existence alone, so only existence is seen. But existence is common to all existents, so all things would appear identical to sight, making perception of differences impossible — a form of skepticism. The second interpretation: the cause is existence plus the specific quiddity (e.g., blackness for black). Then the cause is not a single shared thing but a compound, undermining the original proof that a shared attribute (existence) is the cause. Thus, either way, the proof fails.

Key Terms
وجهان (two aspects)
الماهية (quiddity)
المشترك (shared)
السفسطة (sophistry/skepticism)
السواد (blackness)
البياض (whiteness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the claim 'existence is the cause' is straightforward and has a clear meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that it is ambiguous and both possible interpretations lead to problematic consequences, so the claim is not sound.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of interpreting 'existence is the cause' as meaning existence alone?
A) The proof becomes stronger.
B) It leads to skepticism because all seen objects would appear identical.
C) It implies that quiddities are seen.
D) It requires a receptive subject.

Key Takeaway
The claim that existence is the cause of visibility is ambiguous; under any interpretation, the argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a statement about Allah or faith without examining its possible meanings, assuming it was clear? After this lesson, how will you approach ambiguous claims in theological arguments?
?
The first interpretation leads to complete skepticism about sensory differences. Are there areas in your daily life where you hold onto a belief that, if logically extended, would lead to absurd consequences? Will you honestly reconsider that belief today?
?
Razi's objection shows that even a seemingly strong proof can collapse under careful analysis. What is one belief you currently hold that you are afraid to examine critically because it might fall apart? Will you commit to studying it with the same scrutiny starting this week?

Al-Ka'bi's Metaphorical Interpretation of Musa's Request
Al-Ka'bi's first objection interpreting Moses' request metaphorically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's objection that Musa's request for vision was a metaphor for seeking necessary knowledge of the Creator.

Core Idea
Vision is a metaphor for clear knowledge, not literal sight.

Al-Ka'bi, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian, challenges the assumption that Musa's request implies the possibility of seeing Allah. He argues that the word 'ru'ya' (vision) is commonly used metaphorically to mean clear, necessary knowledge. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for interpreting the Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read al-Ka'bi's argument carefully. He claims that 'Show me' means 'manifest states that produce necessary knowledge of the Creator's existence.'
Notice the key term 'ilm daruri (necessary knowledge) and the claim of a well-known metaphor (majaz mashhur).
2
Understand the metaphor claim
Al-Ka'bi argues that applying the term 'vision' to knowledge is a common metaphorical usage in Arabic. This would mean Musa was not asking for literal sight.
3
Evaluate the objection's force
Consider how this interpretation undermines the proof that seeing Allah is possible. If 'ru'ya' can mean knowledge, then the request does not indicate that Allah can be seen.
This objection challenges the very premise of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم عليه اعتراضات السؤال الأول : قال الكعبي : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قوله : آرني أن يظهر الله أحوالا تفيد العلم الضروري بوجود الصانع ؟ وإطلاق لفظ الرؤية على العلم الضروري الجلي مجاز مشهور. السؤال الثاني : قال أبو علي وأبو هاشم: إن موسى ال إنما سأل الرؤية لا لنفسه بل لقومه، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى : ولَن نُّؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً [البَقَرَة : ٥٥] ثم أن موسى أضاف ذلك السؤال إلى نفسه حتى يكون ذلك السؤال أولى بالإجابة، ولما منعه الله تعالى كان ذلك أقوى في الدلالة على منع الغير. السؤال الثالث: قال أبو الهذيل يحتمل أن يقال إن موسى ال كان عالما بالدلائل العقلية أنه تمتنع رؤية الله تعالى، فسأل الله الرؤية حتى يرى الدلائل السمعية من الرؤية، فتصير الدلائل السمعية والعقلية متعاضدة متوافقة، وكثرة الدلائل توجب زيادة الطمأنينة وقوة اليقين وزوال الشك، ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى في القرآن من ذكر الدلائل الدالة على التوحيد والصفات. السؤال الرابع : قال بعضهم : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن موسى الله ما كان ذلك الوقت عالمًا بامتناع الرؤية على الله تعالى؟ وهذا وإن كان مستبعداً، إلا أنه غير مستحيل، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن كل صفة من صفات الله تعالى لا يتوقف على معرفتها العلم بصحة النبوة، ثم لم لم يبعد أن لا تكون تلك الصفة معلومة للأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، وكونه تعالى بحيث يمتنع رؤيته لا يتوقف على العلم ،به والعلم بصحة النبوة، فعلى هذا لا يمتنع أن تكون هذه الصفة غير معلومة لموسى الة. الثاني : أن المشهور من أهل السنة أنهم يجوزون المعاصي على الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - حال نبوتهم، فإذا جوزوا ذلك فلم لا يجوز هذا الجهل. الثالث : مذهب أهل السنة أنه يحسن من الله تعالى الأشياء، وإذا كان كذلك لم يبعد أن يقال : إن الله تعالى ما أمره بمعرفة هذه الصفة، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يكن ذلك عبنا في حق موسى ال
And they have objections against it. The first question: Al-Ka'bi said: Why is it not possible that the meaning of His statement 'Show me' is that Allah should manifest states (ahwal) that yield necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) of the existence of the Maker? And applying the term vision (ru'ya) to clear necessary knowledge is a well-known metaphor (majaz mashhur). The second question: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim said: Musa (AS) only asked for the vision not for himself but for his people, and the proof for it is His statement: 'We will never believe you until we see Allah plainly' [Al-Baqara: 55]. Then Musa attributed that question to himself so that the question would be more deserving of an answer, and when Allah prevented him, that was stronger in indicating the prevention for others. The third question: Abu al-Hudhayl said: It is possible to say that Musa (AS) knew by rational proofs that seeing Allah is impossible, so he asked Allah for the vision so that he could see the transmitted proofs (al-dala'il al-sam'iyya) of the vision, so that the transmitted and rational proofs would become mutually supportive and harmonious, and the abundance of proofs increases tranquility, strength of certainty, and removal of doubt; and for this reason Allah multiplied in the Quran the proofs indicating tawhid and attributes. The fourth question: Some said: Why is it not possible to say that Musa (AS) was not at that time aware of the impossibility of seeing Allah? And this, although improbable, is not impossible, and it is indicated by several aspects: First, that each attribute of Allah's attributes does not depend for its knowledge on the knowledge of the soundness of prophethood, and it is not far that that attribute should not be known to the prophets (peace be upon them); and His being such that seeing Him is impossible does not depend on knowledge of it and knowledge of the soundness of prophethood; so on this, it is not impossible that this attribute was not known to Musa (AS). Second, that the well-known view from Ahl al-Sunna is that they permit sins (ma'asi) on prophets - peace be upon them - during their prophethood; so if they permit that, why not permit this ignorance? Third, the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that things are good (hasan) from Allah ta'ala; and if that is so, it is not far to say that Allah ta'ala did not command him to know this attribute; and on this assumption, that was not a defect in Musa (AS).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents four objections raised by various theologians against the proof that Musa's request indicates the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection, from al-Ka'bi, interprets the request as a metaphor for seeking necessary knowledge of the existence of the Creator. The second, from Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, claims Musa asked on behalf of his people, not himself, as indicated by the verse where his people demanded to see Allah. The third, from Abu al-Hudhayl, suggests Musa already knew the impossibility but wanted to witness the transmitted proofs alongside rational ones for increased certainty. The fourth objection, from unnamed scholars, posits that Musa may not have known that seeing Allah is impossible; they support this by arguing that knowledge of this attribute is not necessary for prophethood, and by appealing to the Sunni view that prophets can commit minor sins and that Allah is not obligated to inform them of everything.

Key Terms
الكعبي
العلم الضروري
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Musa's request must be taken literally, and any metaphorical reading is forced.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi shows that metaphorical usage of 'ru'ya' for knowledge is well-established in Arabic, so a literal reading is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, what does Musa's request 'Show me' actually mean?
A) A literal request to see Allah with the eyes
B) A request for necessary knowledge of the Creator's existence
C) A request for physical miracles
D) A request for companionship

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's objection shows that the word 'vision' in Musa's request can be interpreted metaphorically as a request for knowledge, not literal sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Rabb arini anthur ilayk' (7:143), do you instinctively assume Musa was asking to see Allah with his physical eyes? Or do you allow for the possibility that he sought a deeper, intellectual vision? What does your instinct reveal about your approach to understanding Allah's attributes? This week, before interpreting any verse about Allah, pause and ask: 'Could there be a metaphorical meaning I am missing?'
?
Al-Ka'bi's argument demands 'necessary knowledge' (ilm daruri) as the basis for certainty about Allah's existence. Do you possess such certainty in your own heart, or do you rely on inherited belief without personal verification? What specific steps can you take today to transform your knowledge of Allah from hearsay to direct, undeniable certainty?
?
Imagine someone told you that your deepest conviction about Allah was based on a misinterpretation of a single word. Would you be open to rethinking your position, or would you cling to it out of habit? Why? This lesson challenges you to humbly reexamine even the most familiar verses—are you willing to do that, whatever the cost?

Abu Ali and Abu Hashim: Musa Asked on Behalf of His People
Second objection: Musa's request was on behalf of his people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Musa's request was made on behalf of his people, not for himself, using the Quranic verse as evidence.

Core Idea
Musa asked for vision to intercede for his people, not for himself.

Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, leading Mu'tazili figures, propose that Musa's request was motivated by his people's demand to see Allah. They cite the Quranic verse where the Israelites say, 'We will never believe you until we see Allah plainly.' Musa then repeated their request as his own to increase the likelihood of acceptance, and when denied, it served as a stronger lesson for others.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the second objection. Note that it claims Musa did not ask for himself but for his people, and it provides a proof from the Quran.
Look at the citation of Al-Baqara 2:55.
2
Understand the rationale
Why would Musa attribute the question to himself? The objection says this made the request more 'deserving of an answer' and the denial stronger for others.
3
Implications for the proof
If Musa asked on behalf of others, his personal belief about the possibility of seeing Allah is not at stake. Thus, the request cannot be used as evidence that he believed vision is possible.
This objection undermines the proof's reliance on Musa's intent.

Abu al-Hudhayl: Musa Sought Transmitted Proofs for Certainty
Third objection: Musa sought transmitted proofs for increased certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl's objection that Musa, already knowing the impossibility of vision, asked to see transmitted proofs for stronger certainty.

Core Idea
Musa's request aimed at harmonizing rational and transmitted proofs, not literal vision.

Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, a Mu'tazili theologian, offers a sophisticated psychological interpretation. He suggests that Musa was already convinced by rational arguments that seeing Allah is impossible. Yet he asked for vision not to see Allah, but to witness the transmitted proofs (the very verses that mention vision) so that rational and scriptural evidence would align, increasing his tranquility and certainty. This objection reframes the request as a search for epistemic harmony.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read Abu al-Hudhayl's argument. He says Musa was already aware of rational proofs for impossibility, but asked to see the transmitted proofs of vision.
Note the phrase 'yara al-dala'il al-sam'iyya min al-ru'ya' (see the transmitted proofs from the vision).
2
Understand the purpose
Why would Musa want to see transmitted proofs? The objection says to make rational and transmitted proofs mutually supportive (muta'addida mutawafiq) and to increase tranquility, certainty, and remove doubt.
3
Consider the broader implication
Abu al-Hudhayl parallels this with the Quran's multiplicity of proofs for tawhid. This objection suggests Musa's request was pedagogical, not literal.

ولهم عليه اعتراضات السؤال الأول : قال الكعبي : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قوله : آرني أن يظهر الله أحوالا تفيد العلم الضروري بوجود الصانع ؟ وإطلاق لفظ الرؤية على العلم الضروري الجلي مجاز مشهور. السؤال الثاني : قال أبو علي وأبو هاشم: إن موسى ال إنما سأل الرؤية لا لنفسه بل لقومه، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى : ولَن نُّؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً [البَقَرَة : ٥٥] ثم أن موسى أضاف ذلك السؤال إلى نفسه حتى يكون ذلك السؤال أولى بالإجابة، ولما منعه الله تعالى كان ذلك أقوى في الدلالة على منع الغير. السؤال الثالث: قال أبو الهذيل يحتمل أن يقال إن موسى ال كان عالما بالدلائل العقلية أنه تمتنع رؤية الله تعالى، فسأل الله الرؤية حتى يرى الدلائل السمعية من الرؤية، فتصير الدلائل السمعية والعقلية متعاضدة متوافقة، وكثرة الدلائل توجب زيادة الطمأنينة وقوة اليقين وزوال الشك، ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى في القرآن من ذكر الدلائل الدالة على التوحيد والصفات. السؤال الرابع : قال بعضهم : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن موسى الله ما كان ذلك الوقت عالمًا بامتناع الرؤية على الله تعالى؟ وهذا وإن كان مستبعداً، إلا أنه غير مستحيل، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن كل صفة من صفات الله تعالى لا يتوقف على معرفتها العلم بصحة النبوة، ثم لم لم يبعد أن لا تكون تلك الصفة معلومة للأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، وكونه تعالى بحيث يمتنع رؤيته لا يتوقف على العلم ،به والعلم بصحة النبوة، فعلى هذا لا يمتنع أن تكون هذه الصفة غير معلومة لموسى الة. الثاني : أن المشهور من أهل السنة أنهم يجوزون المعاصي على الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - حال نبوتهم، فإذا جوزوا ذلك فلم لا يجوز هذا الجهل. الثالث : مذهب أهل السنة أنه يحسن من الله تعالى الأشياء، وإذا كان كذلك لم يبعد أن يقال : إن الله تعالى ما أمره بمعرفة هذه الصفة، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يكن ذلك عبنا في حق موسى ال
And they have objections against it. The first question: Al-Ka'bi said: Why is it not possible that the meaning of His statement 'Show me' is that Allah should manifest states (ahwal) that yield necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) of the existence of the Maker? And applying the term vision (ru'ya) to clear necessary knowledge is a well-known metaphor (majaz mashhur). The second question: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim said: Musa (AS) only asked for the vision not for himself but for his people, and the proof for it is His statement: 'We will never believe you until we see Allah plainly' [Al-Baqara: 55]. Then Musa attributed that question to himself so that the question would be more deserving of an answer, and when Allah prevented him, that was stronger in indicating the prevention for others. The third question: Abu al-Hudhayl said: It is possible to say that Musa (AS) knew by rational proofs that seeing Allah is impossible, so he asked Allah for the vision so that he could see the transmitted proofs (al-dala'il al-sam'iyya) of the vision, so that the transmitted and rational proofs would become mutually supportive and harmonious, and the abundance of proofs increases tranquility, strength of certainty, and removal of doubt; and for this reason Allah multiplied in the Quran the proofs indicating tawhid and attributes. The fourth question: Some said: Why is it not possible to say that Musa (AS) was not at that time aware of the impossibility of seeing Allah? And this, although improbable, is not impossible, and it is indicated by several aspects: First, that each attribute of Allah's attributes does not depend for its knowledge on the knowledge of the soundness of prophethood, and it is not far that that attribute should not be known to the prophets (peace be upon them); and His being such that seeing Him is impossible does not depend on knowledge of it and knowledge of the soundness of prophethood; so on this, it is not impossible that this attribute was not known to Musa (AS). Second, that the well-known view from Ahl al-Sunna is that they permit sins (ma'asi) on prophets - peace be upon them - during their prophethood; so if they permit that, why not permit this ignorance? Third, the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that things are good (hasan) from Allah ta'ala; and if that is so, it is not far to say that Allah ta'ala did not command him to know this attribute; and on this assumption, that was not a defect in Musa (AS).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents four objections raised by various theologians against the proof that Musa's request indicates the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection, from al-Ka'bi, interprets the request as a metaphor for seeking necessary knowledge of the existence of the Creator. The second, from Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, claims Musa asked on behalf of his people, not himself, as indicated by the verse where his people demanded to see Allah. The third, from Abu al-Hudhayl, suggests Musa already knew the impossibility but wanted to witness the transmitted proofs alongside rational ones for increased certainty. The fourth objection, from unnamed scholars, posits that Musa may not have known that seeing Allah is impossible; they support this by arguing that knowledge of this attribute is not necessary for prophethood, and by appealing to the Sunni view that prophets can commit minor sins and that Allah is not obligated to inform them of everything.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل
الدلائل السمعية
الدلائل العقلية
يقين
طمأنينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Musa doubted the rational proofs, so he asked to see Allah.
Correction: Abu al-Hudhayl says Musa was already certain of the impossibility; he sought additional transmitted proofs for stronger certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Hudhayl, why did Musa ask to see Allah despite knowing it is impossible?
A) He was not sure about the rational proofs
B) He wanted to see the transmitted proofs of vision to strengthen his certainty
C) He wanted to test Allah's power
D) He was forced by his people

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's objection reinterprets Musa's request as a desire to see transmitted proofs, increasing certainty through the harmony of reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief with rational certainty, do you still seek external evidence to confirm it? Where in your life have you neglected to seek transmitted proofs, relying only on reason? What would it cost you today to seek that harmony and remove doubt?
?
Musa, a prophet with knowledge, still desired more proofs. What does that say about your own spiritual complacency? Are you willing to ask Allah to show you signs that align your intellect with revelation until your heart finds tranquility?
?
The lesson says abundance of proofs brings tranquility. What one area of your faith lacks this tranquility? Will you take one tangible step today—reading a verse, reflecting on creation, or consulting a scholar—to add a proof that strengthens your certainty and removes doubt?

Objection: Musa Might Have Been Ignorant of the Impossibility
Fourth objection: possible ignorance of Musa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Musa may not have known that seeing Allah is impossible, supported by arguments from prophetic knowledge and Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Musa's ignorance of the impossibility is improbable but not impossible, defended by Sunni principles.

The fourth objection, attributed to unnamed scholars, takes a different tack: perhaps Musa simply did not know that seeing Allah is impossible. While this seems unlikely, the objectors argue it is not rationally impossible. They support this with three arguments: first, knowing this attribute is not necessary for prophethood; second, Sunni theology allows for minor sins (which could include ignorance) by prophets; third, Allah may not have commanded Musa to know this attribute, so his ignorance is not blameworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the fourth objection. It says: why not say Musa was not aware of the impossibility at that time? Then it provides three supporting arguments.
Note the three arguments: (a) knowledge of this attribute is not prerequisite for prophethood, (b) Sunni allowance of minor sins includes ignorance, (c) Allah may not have obligated Musa to know this.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument says that knowing each divine attribute does not depend on knowing prophethood, so a prophet might not know a particular attribute.
3
Analyze the second and third arguments
The second cites Sunni permission of sins on prophets, so why not this ignorance? The third says since Allah can do as He wills, He might not have commanded Musa to know this attribute, making ignorance no defect.
4
Implications
If Musa could have been ignorant, then his request does not indicate belief in possibility, as he may have asked out of ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the First Objection: Musa's Direct Request
Razi gives two arguments to refute the first objection about Musa's request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that Musa's request for vision was metaphorical for seeking knowledge.

Core Idea
Razi uses two arguments to show the request was literal, not metaphorical.

The first objection claimed that Musa's request 'Show me' was actually a request for a proof of existence, not literal vision. Razi offers two decisive arguments that demonstrate the request was for direct vision, not a metaphorical proof. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first objection
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the two aspects (وجهان) Razi presents.
The first aspect focuses on the context of direct speech.
2
Analyze the first argument
Razi argues that since Musa was speaking directly to Allah without an intermediary, it would be absurd to ask for a proof of existence. Ask yourself: why?
3
Analyze the second argument
Razi points to the exact phrasing 'Look at You' and contrasts it with what would have been said if the intention was a proof. This linguistic precision is key.
Al-Ka'bi's interpretation is rejected on linguistic grounds.
4
Summarize the refutation
Combine both arguments to see why the first objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن موسى كان يتكلم مع الله تعالى في هذا الوقت بلا واسطة وفي مثل هذا الوقت يبعد أن يقول : يا إلهي أظهر لي دليلًا أعرف به وجودك. الثاني : أنه قال : أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] ولو كان المراد ما ذكره الكعبي لكان يقول : أنظر إلى دليلك. وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن أولئك الذين كانوا يطلبون الرؤية إما يقال: إنهم كانوا من المؤمنين، أو من الكفار ، فإن كانوا من المؤمنين كانوا لا محالة يقبلون قول موسى في أن هذا السؤال غير جائز، وما كان موسى محتاجا إلى إضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه وإن كانوا من الكفار ، فهم لا يصدقونه في أن الله تعالى منع العباد من سؤال الرؤية، وعلى التقديرين فإضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه عبث. الثاني : أن هذا السؤال لو كان محالا لمنعهم لمنعهم عنه ؛ الا ترى أن القوم لما قالوا له : واجْعَل لَّنَا إِلَهَا كَمَا لَمْ الله الله [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] منعهم من هذا الكلام، وقال: إنكم قَوْم تَجْهَلُونَ [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] .
And the answer: As for the first question, its answer is from two aspects: First: Musa was speaking with Allah ta'ala at this time without an intermediary, and at such a time it is unlikely that he would say: 'O my God, show me a proof by which I know Your existence.' Second: He said: 'Look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]; and if the intended meaning were what al-Ka'bi mentioned, he would have said: 'Look at Your proof.' And as for the second question, its answer is from two aspects: First: Those who were asking for the vision, either it is said that they were believers or disbelievers. If they were believers, they would inevitably accept Musa's statement that this request is not permissible, and Musa did not need to attribute this request to himself. And if they were disbelievers, they would not believe him that Allah forbade the servants from asking for vision. On both assumptions, attributing this request to himself is futile. Second: That if this request were impossible for them, he would have prevented them from it. Do you not see that when the people said to him: 'Make for us a god as they have gods' [Al-A'raf: 138], he prevented them from this speech and said: 'Indeed you are a people behaving ignorantly' [Al-A'raf: 138].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides the author's refutation of the first two objections. For the first objection (that the request was metaphorical for seeking knowledge), Razi gives two arguments. First, since Musa was speaking directly to Allah without an intermediary, it is unlikely he would ask for a proof of existence; that would be inappropriate in such a direct conversation. Second, the wording 'Look at You' is specific; if the intention were to see a proof, he would have said 'Look at Your proof.' For the second objection (that Musa asked on behalf of his people), Razi again gives two arguments. First, whether the people were believers or disbelievers, the attribution to himself is futile; believers would have accepted his prohibition, and disbelievers would not believe his claim that Allah forbade it. Second, if the request were impossible, Musa would have prohibited them directly, just as he did when they asked for a physical idol; he rebuked them and called them ignorant. Thus, he would not have asked for something impermissible.

Key Terms
الجواب
الوجهان
بلا واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa's request 'Show me' was a metaphorical request for a proof of Allah's existence.
Correction: Razi shows that the context of direct speech and the exact wording indicate it was a literal request for vision, not a metaphorical one.

Checkpoint
In refuting the first objection, what does Razi argue Musa would have said if he meant to ask for a proof?
A) 'Show me Your proof'
B) 'Look at Your proof'
C) 'Guide me to certainty'
D) 'Increase my knowledge'

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the metaphorical interpretation of Musa's request by citing the context of direct speech and the precise wording 'Look at You.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Musa (AS), in his closeness to Allah, dared to ask to see Him. In your own worship, have you ever dared to ask Allah for a direct experience of His presence—or do you keep your requests safe and comfortable? What holds you back from such a bold plea?
?
Razi argues that when you are already in direct conversation with Allah, asking for a proof of His existence is inappropriate. Think of a moment when you felt close to Allah. Did you still seek intellectual proofs and signs, or did you surrender to the intimacy? Which do you rely on more: proofs or presence?
?
The difference between 'look at You' and 'look at your proof' changes everything. Are you quick to reinterpret clear Qur'anic verses to fit your own understanding, or do you submit to the literal wording, even when it challenges you? What does that reveal about your heart's posture toward Allah's speech?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Attribution to the People
Razi refutes the second objection using a case analysis and a historical precedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that Musa attributed the request for vision to his own desire rather than the people's.

Core Idea
Razi shows that whether the people were believers or disbelievers, attributing the request to himself was futile.

The second objection argued that Musa's phrase 'Show me' was a request made on behalf of his people, not himself. Razi dismantles this by considering both possibilities for the people's faith and by citing a precedent where Musa directly prohibited a similar request. Let's see his two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second objection
Read the Arabic and English. Note the two aspects Razi presents.
2
Understand the first argument
Razi divides the people into believers or disbelievers. If believers, they would accept his word; if disbelievers, they wouldn't believe his claim. Either way, attributing to himself is futile.
This is an exhaustive case analysis.
3
Understand the second argument
Razi gives a historical precedent: when the Israelites asked for an idol, Musa directly prohibited them. If the request for vision were impermissible, he would have done the same, but he didn't. So it must be permissible.
The idol example shows Musa's typical response.
4
Conclude the refutation
Both arguments together show that Musa's request was genuine, not a concession to the people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن موسى كان يتكلم مع الله تعالى في هذا الوقت بلا واسطة وفي مثل هذا الوقت يبعد أن يقول : يا إلهي أظهر لي دليلًا أعرف به وجودك. الثاني : أنه قال : أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] ولو كان المراد ما ذكره الكعبي لكان يقول : أنظر إلى دليلك. وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن أولئك الذين كانوا يطلبون الرؤية إما يقال: إنهم كانوا من المؤمنين، أو من الكفار ، فإن كانوا من المؤمنين كانوا لا محالة يقبلون قول موسى في أن هذا السؤال غير جائز، وما كان موسى محتاجا إلى إضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه وإن كانوا من الكفار ، فهم لا يصدقونه في أن الله تعالى منع العباد من سؤال الرؤية، وعلى التقديرين فإضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه عبث. الثاني : أن هذا السؤال لو كان محالا لمنعهم لمنعهم عنه ؛ الا ترى أن القوم لما قالوا له : واجْعَل لَّنَا إِلَهَا كَمَا لَمْ الله الله [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] منعهم من هذا الكلام، وقال: إنكم قَوْم تَجْهَلُونَ [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] .
And the answer: As for the first question, its answer is from two aspects: First: Musa was speaking with Allah ta'ala at this time without an intermediary, and at such a time it is unlikely that he would say: 'O my God, show me a proof by which I know Your existence.' Second: He said: 'Look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]; and if the intended meaning were what al-Ka'bi mentioned, he would have said: 'Look at Your proof.' And as for the second question, its answer is from two aspects: First: Those who were asking for the vision, either it is said that they were believers or disbelievers. If they were believers, they would inevitably accept Musa's statement that this request is not permissible, and Musa did not need to attribute this request to himself. And if they were disbelievers, they would not believe him that Allah forbade the servants from asking for vision. On both assumptions, attributing this request to himself is futile. Second: That if this request were impossible for them, he would have prevented them from it. Do you not see that when the people said to him: 'Make for us a god as they have gods' [Al-A'raf: 138], he prevented them from this speech and said: 'Indeed you are a people behaving ignorantly' [Al-A'raf: 138].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides the author's refutation of the first two objections. For the first objection (that the request was metaphorical for seeking knowledge), Razi gives two arguments. First, since Musa was speaking directly to Allah without an intermediary, it is unlikely he would ask for a proof of existence; that would be inappropriate in such a direct conversation. Second, the wording 'Look at You' is specific; if the intention were to see a proof, he would have said 'Look at Your proof.' For the second objection (that Musa asked on behalf of his people), Razi again gives two arguments. First, whether the people were believers or disbelievers, the attribution to himself is futile; believers would have accepted his prohibition, and disbelievers would not believe his claim that Allah forbade it. Second, if the request were impossible, Musa would have prohibited them directly, just as he did when they asked for a physical idol; he rebuked them and called them ignorant. Thus, he would not have asked for something impermissible.

Key Terms
عبث
منعهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's argument is weak because it assumes the people are only two types, but it actually covers all possibilities exhaustively.
Correction: The binary division (believers/disbelievers) is exhaustive (every person is either a believer or not), so the argument is logically sound.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi give to show that Musa would have prohibited an impermissible request?
A) The people asked for a calf
B) The people asked for an idol
C) The people asked for water
D) The people asked for food

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the second objection by showing that attributing the request to the people is futile and that Musa's lack of prohibition indicates the request was permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for a sin by blaming others or circumstances, are you not like those who attribute their request to the people? Will you honestly examine your heart and stop hiding behind attributions, taking full responsibility before Allah?
?
Razi shows that attributing a request to others is futile. In what situations have you used 'but everyone else does it' to justify a wrong action? Are you willing to repent and seek Allah's forgiveness for placing your accountability on others?
