Refuting the Third Objection: Musa's Request
Razi presents a dichotomy to refute Abu al-Hudhayl's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's objection by showing that both possible states of Musa's knowledge lead to unacceptable outcomes.

Core Idea
Both doubt and certainty lead to impossibility for a prophet.

Razi addresses the third objection, which claims that Musa's request to see Allah is incompatible with knowing it is impossible. Razi dismantles this by presenting a stark choice: either Musa was doubtful, entailing ignorance, or he was certain, entailing irrationality. Both options are unbefitting a prophet. This short but powerful argument closes the door on the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Let Razi's rhetorical structure sink in.
Notice the clear 'either...or' structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Razi presents two possibilities for Musa's state of knowledge: doubt or certainty. Find exactly how he phrases each.
3
Understand why each fails
For doubt: leads to ignorance of Allah, unbecoming a prophet. For certainty: should ask for proof, not the impossible thing.
Consider why asking for the impossible is irrational.
4
Appreciate the refutation's strength
Razi does not need to prove which state Musa was in. He shows both lead to contradiction, so the objection fails regardless.
This is a classic dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه أنه إما أن يقال إنه كان شاكًا في الامتناع والجواز، أو يقال : إنه كان قاطعا بالامتناع، فإن قطعنا أنه كان شاكا في الجواز لزم كونه جاهلا بالله تعالى، وهذا لا يليق بالأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - وإن قلنا : إنه كان قاطعا بامتناع الرؤية على الله تعالى كان الأدب أن يقول : رب زدني دليلًا على امتناع الرؤية فأما أن يسأل الرؤية مع العلم بامتناعها فهذا لا يليق بالعقلاء.
And as for the third question, its answer is that it is either said that he was doubtful about the impossibility and permissibility, or said that he was certain of the impossibility. If we decide that he was doubtful about the permissibility, it would entail his ignorance of Allah ta'ala, and this is not fitting for the prophets - may the blessings and peace of Allah be upon them. And if we say that he was certain of the impossibility of seeing Allah ta'ala, then the proper conduct would be for him to say: 'My Lord, increase me in proof of the impossibility of vision.' But as for asking for the vision while knowing its impossibility, this is not fitting for rational people.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection from Abu al-Hudhayl, who claimed that Musa asked despite knowing impossibility to combine proofs. Razi presents a dichotomy: either Musa was doubtful about the permissibility of seeing Allah, or he was certain it is impossible. If he was doubtful, that means he was ignorant of Allah's attribute, which is unbecoming of a prophet. If he was certain it is impossible, then his proper conduct would have been to ask for an increase in proof of impossibility, not to ask for the impossible thing itself. Asking for something known to be impossible is irrational and unbefitting a rational person, let alone a prophet. Thus, both alternatives lead to an unacceptable conclusion.

Key Terms
شاك
قاطع
الامتناع
الجواز
جاهل
الأدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is arguing that Musa could not have been doubtful, but he shows both options are problematic.
Correction: Razi does not reject doubt alone; he shows that both doubt and certainty lead to contradictions, so the objection fails regardless.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what would be the proper conduct for Musa if he were certain that seeing Allah is impossible?
A) To ask for a proof of impossibility
B) To ask for a miracle
C) To remain silent
D) To ask for forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that whether Musa was doubtful or certain, his request is incompatible with prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you asked Allah for something while deep down knowing it might not be best for you? How does Musa's example of asking for increase in proof rather than the object itself challenge your own du'as?
?
Are you more often 'certain' or 'doubtful' in your religious matters? Which of these two states leads you to behave more humbly and seek increase in knowledge, and which leads you to be complacent?
?
Musa's request, though seemingly improper, was met with mercy and a lesson. What request have you made that you now realize was inappropriate, and how can you turn that into a request for 'increase in proof' rather than demanding the thing itself?

Answering the Objection of Musa's Request
Razi answers the objection that Musa should have asked for a proof instead of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two-fold response to the objection that Prophet Musa should have asked for a proof instead of vision.

Core Idea
Musa's request for vision was appropriate because he was already in direct communion with Allah.

Imam Razi now addresses a critical objection: if seeing Allah is impossible, why did Prophet Musa ask for it? The objection, attributed to al-Ka'bi, suggests Musa should have asked for a proof. Razi provides two concise answers that defend the permissibility of the request while refuting the objection. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the structure.
2
Understand the first answer
Razi argues that Musa was speaking directly to Allah without intermediary, so asking for a proof would be improbable.
Consider why direct communion makes a proof request inappropriate.
3
Understand the second answer
Razi points to the Quranic wording 'Look upon You' and argues that if Musa meant a proof, he would have said 'Look upon Your proof.'
Check the Quranic verse 7:143 to confirm the wording.
4
Reflect on the combined refutation
Notice how both answers together show that the objection misinterprets Musa's intent and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الأول فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن موسى كان يتكلم مع الله تعالى في هذا الوقت بلا واسطة وفي مثل هذا الوقت يبعد أن يقول : يا إلهي أظهر لي دليلًا أعرف به وجودك. الثاني : أنه قال : أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] ولو كان المراد ما ذكره الكعبي لكان يقول : أنظر إلى دليلك.
As for the first question, its answer is from two aspects: First: That Moses was speaking with Allah Almighty at that time without an intermediary, and at such a time it is improbable that he would say: 'O my God, show me a proof by which I may know Your existence.' Second: That he said: 'Look upon You' [7:143]. And if the intended meaning was what al-Ka'bi mentioned, he would have said: 'Look upon Your proof.'
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first objection (السؤال الأول) against the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The objection likely claimed that Moses should have asked for a rational proof for Allah's existence rather than for vision. Razi gives two answers. First, at that moment Moses was speaking directly to Allah without any intermediary, so it would be inappropriate for him to ask for a proof—since he was already in direct communion. Second, Moses' actual words were 'Look upon You' (انظر إليك), not 'Look upon Your proof.' If the objector's interpretation (attributed to al-Ka'bi) were correct, Moses would have said 'Look upon Your proof,' but he did not. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms

الرؤية
موسى
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa's request was for a rational proof rather than for vision itself.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that both the context (direct speech) and the wording (انظر إليك) confirm Musa asked for vision.

Checkpoint

B) Because Musa was speaking directly to Allah without an intermediary.
C) Because al-Ka'bi prohibited such a request.
D) Because Musa already knew Allah's existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi's answer shows that the context and wording of Musa's request confirm he asked for vision, not a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Musa could have asked for a proof, but he instead asked to see Allah. If you stood before Allah today, what would your request reveal about your heart’s deepest longing?
?
Are you more attached to signs and evidence from Allah than to His very presence? How can you shift from seeking proofs to seeking Him directly?

Razi's Refutation: Who Sought the Vision?
Razi refutes the objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two-fold refutation of the objection that Moses attributed the request for vision to himself rather than to the people.

Core Idea
Moses' attribution of the request was strategic, not factual.

In this passage, Razi addresses a critical objection: why did Moses attribute the request for seeing Allah to himself when it was the people who asked? He offers two compelling arguments that reveal the futility of the objection and highlight the consistency of the Qur'anic narrative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the two numbered aspects (الأول, الثاني).
2
Understand the first aspect
Razi argues based on the status of the requesters: believers or disbelievers. Work through both cases and see why attribution to Moses is futile in either scenario.
Notice the logical conditional: if believers → they would accept; if disbelievers → they would not believe.
3
Understand the second aspect
Razi gives a parallel example from the Qur'an: when the Israelites asked for an idol, Moses rebuked them directly. Consider why this supports his argument.
This shows that if the request were truly impossible, Moses would have forbidden it, not attributed it to himself.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Ask yourself: How does Razi's two-fold answer defeat the objection? What does this teach us about understanding the Qur'anic narrative?
The objection is shown to be logically flawed regardless of the requesters' identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه من وجهين : الأول : أن أولئك الذين كانوا يطلبون الرؤية إما يقال: إنهم كانوا من المؤمنين، أو من الكفار ، فإن كانوا من المؤمنين كانوا لا محالة يقبلون قول موسى في أن هذا السؤال غير جائز، وما كان موسى محتاجا إلى إضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه وإن كانوا من الكفار ، فهم لا يصدقونه في أن الله تعالى منع العباد من سؤال الرؤية، وعلى التقديرين فإضافة هذا السؤال إلى نفسه عبث. الثاني : أن هذا السؤال لو كان محالا لمنعهم عنه ؛ الا ترى أن القوم لما قالوا له : واجْعَل لَّنَا إِلَهَا كَمَا لَمْ الله الله [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] منعهم من هذا الكلام، وقال: إنكم قَوْم تَجْهَلُونَ [الأعراف: ۱۳۸] .
As for the second question, its answer is from two aspects: First: Those who were requesting the vision—it is said: either they were believers or disbelievers. If they were believers, they would inevitably accept Moses' statement that this request is impermissible, and Moses had no need to attribute this request to himself. If they were disbelievers, they would not believe him that Allah prohibited servants from requesting vision. In both cases, attributing this request to himself is futile. Second: This request, if it were impossible, he would have forbidden them from it. Do you not see that when the people said to him: 'Make for us a god as they have gods' [7:138], he forbade them from this speech and said: 'Indeed, you are a people ignorant' [7:138].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which probably claimed that Moses attributed the request for vision to himself when in fact it was the people who asked. Razi gives two responses. First, he analyses the status of the people: if they were believers, they would have accepted Moses' guidance and not insisted, so Moses had no need to take the blame. If they were disbelievers, they would not believe Moses anyway, so taking the blame is pointless. Second, Razi cites the parallel of the Israelites asking for a physical idol—Moses immediately rebuked them and called them ignorant. Similarly, if requesting vision were impossible, Moses would have forbade the people directly instead of attributing the request to himself. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
السؤال الثاني
المؤمنون
الكفار
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses attributed the request to himself because he was the one who actually asked.
Correction: Razi shows that this interpretation is unnecessary; the attribution was either strategic or the requesters were not believers, and Moses' response in a similar case (idol request) was to rebuke directly, not to self-attribute.

Checkpoint

A) Because disbelievers would accept his authority.
B) Because disbelievers would not believe him that Allah forbade it.
C) Because Moses was afraid of them.
D) Because the request was not from them.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that Moses' attribution was a rhetorical device, not a historical claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a strong desire for something that Allah has clearly prohibited, do you honestly ask yourself whether that desire comes from a place of faith or disbelief? Or do you try to justify it and look for loopholes? Razi’s argument shows that even believers should accept the command. Are you truly ready to submit, or do you cling to your own wishes? Commit today to one specific area where you will stop rationalizing and start obeying.
?
Moses immediately rebuked the request for an idol and called it ignorance. When you see something forbidden in your own heart—like envy, pride, or lust—do you rebuke it instantly, or do you entertain it and debate it? This lesson forces you to ask: Are you treating your own sinful desires as a serious prohibition, or as a matter for discussion? Name one sinful thought you have been entertaining and decide now to cut it off with a firm 'this is ignorance'.
?
Razi’s analysis shows that attributing the request to himself was a rhetorical choice. Are there areas in your life where you attribute your faults or mistakes to others or to circumstances, rather than taking responsibility? The truth is, your heart knows where the blame lies. What would it mean for you to stop that deflection and honestly say, 'I am the one who chose this'? Pray for the courage to be truthful with yourself and with Allah.

The Second Proof: The Syllogism of Ru'ya
Razi builds a syllogism for the permissibility of ru'ya using a Quranic condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi constructs a syllogism to prove that seeing Allah is possible, using a conditional from the Quran.

Core Idea
Ru'ya is permissible because it is contingent upon a possible condition.

Razi presents his second proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah by constructing a clear syllogism. He links the vision of Allah to a contingent condition—the stability of the mountain—and then argues that whatever is contingent upon a permissible condition is itself permissible. This passage lays out the reasoning step by step, grounding the argument in Quran 7:143.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi states the conclusion first: ru'ya is permissible. Then identify the two premises that support it.
2
Understand the premises
The first premise: ru'ya is contingent upon a permissible condition (the mountain's stability). The second premise: anything contingent upon a permissible condition is itself permissible. Examine how Razi justifies each premise.
The justification for the first premise comes from Quran 7:143; for the second, from a logical argument about divine truthfulness.
3
Reflect on the 'fine point'
Razi calls his justification of the second premise a 'fine point' (nukta hasana). Consider why this reasoning is significant—it ties the possibility of ru'ya to the impossibility of lying in Allah's speech.
This strengthens the argument by grounding it in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز والمعلق على الشرط الجائز جائز ، فرؤية الله تعالى جائزة. وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط جائز لأن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل جائز فيلزم أن تكون رؤية الله تعالى جائزة، وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل لقوله تعالى : فإنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَكي [الأعراف : ١٤٣] وإنما قلنا إن استقرار الجبل جائز لأن الجبل جسم، وكل جسم فإنه يمكن أن يكون ساكنا وإنما قلنا إن المعلق على الجائز جائز لأن بتقدير وقوع ذلك الشرط الجائز إن لم يحصل المشروط لزم الكذب في كلام الله تعالى وإن حصل كان الجواز قبله حاصلا، وهذه نكتة حسنة. (فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز ، قوله : لأنها معلقة على استقرار الجبل، وإنه جائز، قلنا : بل هي معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كون الجبل متحركا، واستقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا محال. وإنما قلنا : إن الرؤية معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا لأنه لو كان معلقا على استقراره لا حال كونه متحركا فاستقراره في غير حال حركته يكون واقعا حاصلاً لا محالة ؛ لأن الجسم كلما لم يكن متحركًا كان ساكنا لا محالة، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون شرط وقوع الرؤية حاصلًا، فكان يجب أن تحصل الرؤية، وحيث لم تحصل الرؤية علمنا أن الشرط لم يحصل، وإنما يصح أن يقال : لم يحصل الشرط إذا قلنا : إن الشرط هو استقراره حال حركته وإذا كان ذلك كان هذا الشرط محالا، فثبت أن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط محال فلم يلزم القول بجواز رؤية الله تعالى. والجواب أن الشرط هو استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل هذا من حيث هذا المفهوم أمر جائز الوجود، فثبت أن الرؤية معلقة على شرط جائز الوجود. وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : دل دليل منفصل على أنه وجد في ذلك الوقت
The second proof: The vision of Allah (ru'ya) is contingent upon a permissible condition, and what is contingent upon a permissible condition is permissible, so the vision of Allah is permissible. We say that the vision of Allah is contingent upon a permissible condition because the vision of Allah is contingent upon the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain is permissible; therefore, it follows that the vision of Allah is permissible. We say that the vision of Allah is contingent upon the stability of the mountain due to His statement: 'If it remains in its place, then you will see Me' [7:143]. We say that the stability of the mountain is permissible because the mountain is a body, and every body can be stationary. We say that what is contingent upon the permissible is permissible because, if that permissible condition occurs, if the conditioned does not occur, then lying in the speech of Allah would follow; and if it occurs, then permission was obtained before it. This is a fine point. If it is said: We do not agree that the vision of Allah is contingent upon a permissible condition—because it is contingent upon the stability of the mountain, and that is permissible—[then] we say: Rather, it is contingent upon the stability of the mountain at the time when the mountain is moving, and the stability of the mountain while it is moving is impossible. We say that the vision is contingent upon the stability of the mountain while it is moving because if it were contingent upon its stability not while it is moving, then its stability at a time other than its motion would occur necessarily, since whenever a body is not moving it is stationary necessarily. On this assumption, the condition for the occurrence of vision would be fulfilled, so vision must occur. Since vision did not occur, we know that the condition was not fulfilled. It is only valid to say that the condition was not fulfilled if we say the condition is its stability during its motion. If that is so, then this condition is impossible. So it is established that the vision of Allah is contingent upon an impossible condition, so the statement of the permissibility of vision of Allah does not follow. The answer is that the condition is the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain from the perspective of this concept is a possible existence. So it is established that the vision is contingent upon a condition that is possible of existence. The utmost that can be said is that a separate evidence indicates that it occurred at that time...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Razi constructs a syllogism: the vision of Allah is conditional upon a possible condition (the mountain's stability); whatever is conditional upon a possible condition is itself possible; therefore ru'ya is possible. He derives the condition from Quran 7:143. The opponent objects that the condition is actually the mountain's stability while it is moving, which is impossible, so the condition is impossible, not possible. Razi responds by clarifying that the condition is simply 'stability of the mountain' as a concept, which is possible in itself. The specific circumstance (at the time of Moses) may have prevented its fulfillment due to a separate evidence, but that does not negate the conceptual possibility. The passage ends mid-sentence, concluding that separate evidence may indicate that it occurred at that time (probably referring to the mountain's stability). This is a classic kalam argument about conditional possibility.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية
الرؤية
الشرط الجائز
استقرار الجبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the condition must actually be fulfilled for the possibility of ru'ya to be established.
Correction: Razi argues that possibility is based on the intrinsic permissibility of the condition, not its actual occurrence. The condition being possible in itself suffices.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the stability of the mountain considered a permissible condition (sharṭ jāʾiz)?
A) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
B) Because the mountain is a body and every body can be stationary.
C) Because the mountain was stable during Moses' encounter.
D) Because Allah's speech cannot be false.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that seeing Allah is possible because it is contingent upon a permissible condition, and whatever is contingent upon a permissible condition is itself permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing Allah is rationally possible, does your heart truly yearn for that vision, or are you content with a distant faith? What would you change in your worship today to prepare for that encounter?
?
Allah's truthfulness is the foundation of this proof: He cannot lie. When you speak, do you mirror that attribute, or do you allow falsehoods, even small ones, to slip? What one specific habit of truthfulness can you commit to this week?
?
The mountain's stability is just a possibility—yet it grounds the hope of seeing Allah. What impossible-seeming condition in your life might actually be possible through relying on Allah's promise? Will you take one step of trust today?

Objection and Answer: The Condition of Stability
Razi addresses an objection that the condition is impossible and provides a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to Razi's proof and his response that the condition is conceptually possible even if not realized in a specific instance.

Core Idea
The possibility of ru'ya does not require the actual fulfillment of the condition.

Razi now addresses a key objection. The opponent argues that the condition in the Quran is actually the mountain's stability while it is moving, which is impossible, so the condition is impossible, not permissible. Razi responds by distinguishing between the conceptual possibility of stability and its specific occurrence in the event of Moses. This dialectical exchange sharpens the proof and clarifies the nature of conditional possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection and the counter-reasoning. The opponent argues that the condition is impossible because it is the mountain's stability while it is moving.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the condition were simply 'stability' (not while moving), then at some time the mountain would be stable, and ru'ya would have occurred. Since it did not occur, the condition must be stability during motion, which is impossible.
This is a logical move that forces the condition to be impossible.
3
Analyze Razi's response
Razi replies that the condition is simply the stability of the mountain as a concept, which is possible in itself. The fact that it did not happen at that time is explained by a separate evidence, but that does not negate its conceptual possibility.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that the separate evidence may show that the condition was not realized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز والمعلق على الشرط الجائز جائز ، فرؤية الله تعالى جائزة. وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط جائز لأن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل جائز فيلزم أن تكون رؤية الله تعالى جائزة، وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل لقوله تعالى : فإنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَكي [الأعراف : ١٤٣] وإنما قلنا إن استقرار الجبل جائز لأن الجبل جسم، وكل جسم فإنه يمكن أن يكون ساكنا وإنما قلنا إن المعلق على الجائز جائز لأن بتقدير وقوع ذلك الشرط الجائز إن لم يحصل المشروط لزم الكذب في كلام الله تعالى وإن حصل كان الجواز قبله حاصلا، وهذه نكتة حسنة. (فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز ، قوله : لأنها معلقة على استقرار الجبل، وإنه جائز، قلنا : بل هي معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كون الجبل متحركا، واستقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا محال. وإنما قلنا : إن الرؤية معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا لأنه لو كان معلقا على استقراره لا حال كونه متحركا فاستقراره في غير حال حركته يكون واقعا حاصلاً لا محالة ؛ لأن الجسم كلما لم يكن متحركًا كان ساكنا لا محالة، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون شرط وقوع الرؤية حاصلًا، فكان يجب أن تحصل الرؤية، وحيث لم تحصل الرؤية علمنا أن الشرط لم يحصل، وإنما يصح أن يقال : لم يحصل الشرط إذا قلنا : إن الشرط هو استقراره حال حركته وإذا كان ذلك كان هذا الشرط محالا، فثبت أن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط محال فلم يلزم القول بجواز رؤية الله تعالى. والجواب أن الشرط هو استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل هذا من حيث هذا المفهوم أمر جائز الوجود، فثبت أن الرؤية معلقة على شرط جائز الوجود. وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : دل دليل منفصل على أنه وجد في ذلك الوقت
The second proof: The vision of Allah (ru'ya) is contingent upon a permissible condition, and what is contingent upon a permissible condition is permissible, so the vision of Allah is permissible. We say that the vision of Allah is contingent upon a permissible condition because the vision of Allah is contingent upon the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain is permissible; therefore, it follows that the vision of Allah is permissible. We say that the vision of Allah is contingent upon the stability of the mountain due to His statement: 'If it remains in its place, then you will see Me' [7:143]. We say that the stability of the mountain is permissible because the mountain is a body, and every body can be stationary. We say that what is contingent upon the permissible is permissible because, if that permissible condition occurs, if the conditioned does not occur, then lying in the speech of Allah would follow; and if it occurs, then permission was obtained before it. This is a fine point. If it is said: We do not agree that the vision of Allah is contingent upon a permissible condition—because it is contingent upon the stability of the mountain, and that is permissible—[then] we say: Rather, it is contingent upon the stability of the mountain at the time when the mountain is moving, and the stability of the mountain while it is moving is impossible. We say that the vision is contingent upon the stability of the mountain while it is moving because if it were contingent upon its stability not while it is moving, then its stability at a time other than its motion would occur necessarily, since whenever a body is not moving it is stationary necessarily. On this assumption, the condition for the occurrence of vision would be fulfilled, so vision must occur. Since vision did not occur, we know that the condition was not fulfilled. It is only valid to say that the condition was not fulfilled if we say the condition is its stability during its motion. If that is so, then this condition is impossible. So it is established that the vision of Allah is contingent upon an impossible condition, so the statement of the permissibility of vision of Allah does not follow. The answer is that the condition is the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain from the perspective of this concept is a possible existence. So it is established that the vision is contingent upon a condition that is possible of existence. The utmost that can be said is that a separate evidence indicates that it occurred at that time...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Razi constructs a syllogism: the vision of Allah is conditional upon a possible condition (the mountain's stability); whatever is conditional upon a possible condition is itself possible; therefore ru'ya is possible. He derives the condition from Quran 7:143. The opponent objects that the condition is actually the mountain's stability while it is moving, which is impossible, so the condition is impossible, not possible. Razi responds by clarifying that the condition is simply 'stability of the mountain' as a concept, which is possible in itself. The specific circumstance (at the time of Moses) may have prevented its fulfillment due to a separate evidence, but that does not negate the conceptual possibility. The passage ends mid-sentence, concluding that separate evidence may indicate that it occurred at that time (probably referring to the mountain's stability). This is a classic kalam argument about conditional possibility.

Misconception: That the impossibility of the condition in a specific instance (Moses) makes it impossible in general.
Correction: Razi distinguishes between the conceptual possibility of stability (which is permissible) and its non-occurrence in that particular instance, which is due to a separate reason.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, why is the condition for ru'ya considered impossible?
A) Because the mountain cannot be stable at all.
B) Because the condition is the mountain's stability while it is moving, which is impossible.
C) Because ru'ya is impossible in itself.

Answer to the Fourth Objection: Knowledge of Prophets vs. Mu'tazila
Razi refutes an objection by showing the implication would elevate Mu'tazila above Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the consensus on prophetic knowledge superiority to refute the Mu'tazila objection against the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
If the Mu'tazila knew better than Moses, consensus would break.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a clever objection from the Mu'tazila. He does not directly refute their argument but shows that accepting it leads to an absurd conclusion: that ordinary Mu'tazila would have greater knowledge of God than Prophet Moses. By appealing to the consensus of the community, Razi decisively closes this line of objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the premise
Locate the consensus (ijma') that Razi invokes. What does the community agree upon?
The premise is that prophets have the most complete knowledge of God.
3
Follow the implication
Razi sets up a conditional: if the Mu'tazila know the impossibility of vision and Moses did not, then each Mu'tazili would surpass Moses in knowledge of God. Understand why this is a problem.
4
Understand the refutation
The conclusion is absurd because it contradicts the consensus that prophets are superior. Therefore, the fourth objection fails. See how this argument protects the doctrine of the possibility of seeing God.
Razi does not need to prove Moses knew or did not know; the hypothetical suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع فجوابه: أن الأمة مجمعة على أن علم الأنبياء والرسل - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - بذات الله تعالى وصفاته أتم وأكمل من علم كل واحد آحاد الأمة، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لما كان العلم بامتناع الرؤية حاصلا لكل واحد من آحاد المعتزلة [قلو؟] لم يكن حاصلا لموسى عليه السلام لكان كل واحد من آحاد المعتزلة أعرف بذات الله تعالى وبصفاته من موسى عليه السلام ولما كان هذا باطلا بالإجماع سقط هذا السؤال.
As for the fourth question, its answer is: The community (umma) is unanimous that the knowledge of the prophets and messengers—blessings and peace of God be upon them—of the Essence of God Most High and His attributes is more complete and perfect than the knowledge of any individual of the community. And when this is established, we say: Since the knowledge of the impossibility of vision is attained by every individual of the Mu'tazila, if it were not attained by Moses, peace be upon him, then every individual of the Mu'tazila would be more knowledgeable of the Essence of God Most High and His attributes than Moses, peace be upon him. And since this is false by consensus, this question falls away.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the possibility of seeing God. Razi begins by stating the agreed-upon premise that the prophets have the most complete knowledge of God. He then applies it: if every Mu'tazili knows that seeing God is impossible, but Moses—a prophet—did not know this, then each Mu'tazili would have greater knowledge of God than Moses. This contradicts the consensus that prophets are superior in knowledge. Therefore, the objection that something is wrong with the argument for vision fails. The argument hinges on the impossibility of ordinary believers surpassing a prophet in knowledge of God.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
الإجماع (al-ijma')
العلم (al-'ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi claims that Moses actually knew that seeing God is impossible.
Correction: Razi does not assert whether Moses knew or not; he uses a conditional to show the absurd consequence if he did not.

Checkpoint

A) The prophets are infallible.
B) The prophets' knowledge of God is superior to that of any ordinary believer.
C) Seeing God is impossible for all rational beings.
D) The Mu'tazila are the most knowledgeable sect.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Mu'tazila objection by showing that accepting it would absurdly elevate ordinary Mu'tazila above Prophet Moses in knowledge of God.

Second Proof: The Conditional Argument for Seeing God
Razi presents a proof from the conditional statement in the Quran: if the mountain stands still, you will see Me.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for the possibility of seeing God, which uses a conditional statement from the Quran and a logical principle about possible conditions.

Core Idea
If a condition is possible, what depends on it is possible.

Razi presents a second argument for the possibility of seeing God, this time based on a conditional verse in the Quran. The argument is logical: if something is conditioned on a possible event, then that thing itself is possible. By analyzing the verse where God says to Moses 'if the mountain stands still, you will see Me,' Razi shows that seeing God is possible because the condition is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a clear understanding of Razi's argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise 1, premise 2, conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises of the conditional syllogism
The proof has two premises: (1) Seeing God is conditioned on a possible condition (the mountain's stillness). (2) Whatever is conditioned on a possible condition is possible. Find these in the Arabic text.
Razi explicitly states both premises and then defends them.
3
Understand why the condition is possible
Razi argues the mountain is a body, and any body can be at rest, so the condition is possible. Consider why this reasoning holds.
This is based on natural possibility, not logical necessity.
4
Appreciate the subtle logical point
Razi explains why 'what is conditioned on the possible is possible' using a subtle point: if the condition occurs and the conditioned does not, God's speech would be false—impossible. So the conditioned must occur, establishing its possibility beforehand.
This subtlety (nukta hasana) strengthens the major premise.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
Compare this to other proofs for the possibility of seeing God. Notice how Razi uses a Quranic verse as a springboard for a logical argument.
This proof relies on the linguistic form of the conditional, not on the historical occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز والمعلق على الشرط الجائز جائز ، فرؤية الله تعالى جائزة. وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط جائز لأن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل جائز فيلزم أن تكون رؤية الله تعالى جائزة، وإنما قلنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على استقرار الجبل لقوله تعالى : فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَانِي [الأعراف : ١٤٣] وإنما قلنا إن استقرار الجبل جائز لأن الجبل جسم، وكل جسم فإنه يمكن أن يكون ساكنا وإنما قلنا إن المعلق على الجائز جائز لأن بتقدير وقوع ذلك الشرط الجائز إن لم يحصل المشروط لزم الكذب في كلام الله تعالى وإن حصل كان الجواز قبله حاصلا، وهذه نكتة حسنة.
The second proof: Seeing God Most High is conditioned upon a possible condition, and what is conditioned upon a possible condition is possible, so seeing God Most High is possible. We only say that seeing God is conditioned upon a possible condition because seeing God Most High is conditioned upon the mountain's standing still, and the mountain's standing still is possible, so it follows that seeing God Most High is possible. We only say that seeing God Most High is conditioned upon the mountain's standing still due to His statement, the Most High: 'And if it stands still in its place, then you will see Me' [al-A'raf 7:143]. And we only say that the mountain's standing still is possible because the mountain is a body, and every body can be at rest. And we only say that what is conditioned upon the possible is possible because upon the supposition of the occurrence of that possible condition, if the conditioned does not occur, it would necessitate lying in the speech of God Most High; and if it does occur, the possibility was already there before it. And this is a nice subtle point.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (hujja) for the possibility of seeing God. Razi structures it as a conditional syllogism: (1) Seeing God (al-ru'ya) is conditioned upon a possible condition (shart ja'iz). (2) Anything conditioned upon a possible condition is itself possible. Therefore, seeing God is possible. He then justifies each premise. Premise 1: The condition is the mountain's standing still, as stated in the Quran (7:143). Premise 2: The mountain's standing still is possible because the mountain is a body, and all bodies can be at rest. The major premise (what is conditioned on the possible is possible) is defended by a subtle point (nukta hasana): if the condition occurs and the conditioned does not, God's promise would be false—impossible for God. So vision must happen, and its possibility is established beforehand. This subtlety is a key logical move.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
الشرط الجائز (al-shart al-ja'iz)
المعلق (al-mu'allaq)
استقرار الجبل (istiqrar al-jabal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the mountain actually stood still and Moses saw God.
Correction: Razi only uses the linguistic form of the conditional verse. He does not claim the condition occurred; he only argues that because the condition is possible, the conditioned (seeing God) is also possible. The proof is logical, not historical.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, why is the mountain's standing still considered a possible condition?
A) Because the mountain is a body and bodies can be at rest.
B) Because God is able to make it stand still.
C) Because Moses specifically requested this condition.
D) Because it is explicitly stated in the Torah.

Key Takeaway
Razi's conditional proof shows that seeing God is possible because it is conditioned on a possible event, and God's truthfulness ensures the conditioned must occur when the condition does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof rests on God's absolute truthfulness: if He says, 'If this, then that,' it must happen exactly as promised. In your own life, when you read God's promises in the Quran—like forgiveness for the repentant, provision for the trusting—do you treat them as absolutely certain, or do you let doubts creep in because you haven't seen the outcome yet? Identify one promise you find hardest to rely on, and decide today to act as if it were already fulfilled.
?
Razi makes a 'subtle point' that if the possible condition occurs and God does not bring about what He promised, His speech would be false. This shows the weight of God's words. Now consider your own words: when you make promises to God (e.g., 'I will pray regularly,' 'I will stop a sin'), do you take them as seriously as God takes His promises? Or do you make excuses when you fail? What would it look like for you to honor a commitment to God this week, even at personal cost?
?
The argument says that something dependent on a possible event is itself possible. In your spiritual life, your connection with God often depends on your circumstances: when life is stable, you worship more easily; when it's chaotic, you drift. What if you allowed your worship to be 'possible' under all conditions—not dependent on your mood or situation? What is one small, consistent act of devotion you could start today that is not conditioned on anything external?

The Condition Is Possible: Answering the Objection
Razi answers the objection by affirming the condition is possible and citing an external impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the possibility of the condition (the mountain's rest) against the objection that it is impossible, and how he introduces the concept of an external impediment.

Core Idea
A condition can be possible yet unfulfilled due to an impediment.

In the previous lesson, Razi presented a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah based on the verse about the mountain. Now an objector tries to undermine that proof by arguing that the condition (the mountain's rest) is actually impossible. This lesson walks through the objection and Razi's crisp reply, showing how the condition remains possible and why an external impediment does not affect the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector re-interprets the condition: not just rest, but rest while the mountain is moving.
The objector's reasoning is subtle: if the condition were simply rest, it would have been fulfilled before and vision would have occurred.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector argues: if the condition were the mountain's rest (without qualification), then at any time the mountain is not moving, it is at rest, so the condition would be met and vision should occur. Since it did not, the condition must be more specific: rest during motion, which is impossible.
This makes the condition impossible, thus the proof fails.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi replies that the condition is simply the mountain's standing still (istiqrar al-jabal), which is a possible concept. The non-occurrence of vision is due to an external impediment (man' s), not an impossible condition.
Razi distinguishes between the condition (possible) and an impediment that prevents its fulfillment.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the difference between a condition (shart) and an impediment (mani'). A condition can be possible in itself but prevented by an external factor. This preserves the strength of the original proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة على شرط جائز ، قوله : لأنها معلقة على استقرار الجبل، وإنه جائز، قلنا : بل هي معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كون الجبل متحركا، واستقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا محال. وإنما قلنا : إن الرؤية معلقة على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا لأنه لو كان معلقا على استقراره لا حال كونه متحركا فاستقراره في غير حال حركته يكون واقعا حاصلاً لا محالة ؛ لأن الجسم كلما لم يكن متحركًا كان ساكنا لا محالة، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون شرط وقوع الرؤية حاصلًا، فكان يجب أن تحصل الرؤية، وحيث لم تحصل الرؤية علمنا أن الشرط لم يحصل، وإنما يصح أن يقال : لم يحصل الشرط إذا قلنا : إن الشرط هو استقراره حال حركته وإذا كان ذلك كان هذا الشرط محالا، فثبت أن رؤية الله معلقة على شرط محال فلم يلزم القول بجواز رؤية الله تعالى. والجواب أن الشرط هو استقرار الجبل، واستقرار الجبل هذا من حيث هذا المفهوم أمر جائز الوجود، فثبت أن الرؤية معلقة على شرط جائز الوجود. وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : دل دليل منفصل على أنه وجد في ذلك الوقت مانع إلا أن الذي دل اللفظ على كونه شرطا للرؤية أمر جائز ، فكان المقصود حاصلًا.
If it is said: We do not accept that seeing God Most High is conditioned upon a possible condition, his statement: because it is conditioned upon the mountain's standing still, and it is possible, (we reply): Rather, it is conditioned upon the mountain's standing still while it is moving, and the standing still of the mountain while it is moving is impossible. And we only say that seeing is conditioned upon the mountain's standing still while it is moving because if it were conditioned upon its standing still not while it is moving, then its standing still at other than its state of motion would inevitably occur and be realized; because the body, whenever it is not moving, is necessarily at rest. And on this estimation, the condition for the occurrence of seeing would be realized, so seeing would necessarily occur. And since seeing did not occur, we know that the condition did not occur. And it is only correct to say that the condition did not occur if we say that the condition is its standing still while it is moving; and when that is the case, this condition is impossible. So it is established that seeing God is conditioned upon an impossible condition, and therefore the statement of the permissibility of seeing God Most High does not follow. The answer: The condition is the standing still of the mountain, and the standing still of the mountain, in terms of this concept, is a possible existent. So it is established that seeing is conditioned upon a possible condition. And the most that can be said in this regard is that an external proof indicated that there was an impediment at that time; but what the wording indicates as a condition for seeing is a possible matter, so the intended point is achieved.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector tries to defeat the second proof by interpreting the condition as impossible. He claims: the condition is actually 'the mountain's standing still while it is moving', which is impossible. Why add 'while moving'? Because if the condition were simply 'standing still', then at any moment the mountain is not moving, it would be resting, and vision should have occurred then—but it didn't. So the condition must be more specific: rest during motion, which is impossible. Therefore, the proof fails because it rests on an impossible condition. Razi answers that the condition is simply the mountain's standing still (istiqrar al-jabal) without any temporal qualification. That concept is possible in itself. The fact that there was an external impediment (man' s) at the time (such as God's will not to show Himself) does not change the linguistic condition being possible. The proof holds because the condition in the verse is something possible.

Key Terms
الشرط المحال (al-shart al-muhal)
المانع (al-mani')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a condition is not fulfilled, it must be impossible.
Correction: A condition can be possible in itself but prevented by an external impediment (mani').

Checkpoint
How does the objector try to make the condition impossible?
A) By claiming the mountain cannot exist in the afterlife.
B) By specifying the condition as 'standing still while moving'.
C) By arguing that the verse was abrogated.
D) By saying the condition is that the mountain is seen.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the second proof by showing that the condition (the mountain's rest) is possible, and non-occurrence is due to an external impediment, not an impossible condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because a condition from Allah was not fulfilled in your life, it must be impossible? Are you willing to consider that there may be an impediment you do not know, and that the condition itself remains possible?
?
How does Razi's distinction between the condition itself and external impediments affect your trust in Allah's promises? Will you now wait patiently, knowing that what is possible may be delayed by a wise impediment?
?
When you face a delay in an answer to dua, does your heart default to thinking the condition is impossible? What would it mean to believe the condition is possible while submitting to Allah's wisdom in withholding, and how can you cultivate that trust today?

Distinguishing Nazar from Ru'ya: Qualities of Shazr and Intensity
Razi argues nazar is distinct from ru'ya via unique descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses linguistic and poetic evidence to argue that nazar (looking) is distinct from ru'ya (vision) by highlighting qualities unique to nazar.

Core Idea
Shazr and intensity are qualities of looking, not vision.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'een that distinguishes nazar from ru'ya. Razi points out that people describe nazar with specific physical qualities such as shazr (squinting) and intensity, which cannot apply to ru'ya. By analyzing these descriptions, we can understand why the two terms are not synonymous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source and its literal English translation to grasp Razi's argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi claims that nazar and ru'ya are different because one can describe nazar with qualities that cannot apply to ru'ya. Identify these qualities from the text.
Look for 'shazr' and 'shidda wa salaba' (intensity and hardness).
3
Examine the supporting evidence
Razi provides two poetic examples. Understand how each example illustrates a quality of nazar that is not applicable to ru'ya.
The first poem mentions shazr; the second mentions intensity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in the context of Islamic theology, especially regarding the vision of Allah (ru'ya Allah).
This argument reinforces that ru'ya is a different kind of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : أنهم يصفون النظر بما لا يمكن أن توصف به الرؤية، وذلك يوجب التباين بينهما، فإنه يوصف النظر بكونه شزرًا. قال الشاعر : ولا خيــر بــالــبــغــضــاء والـنـظـر ظر الشـ و معلوم أن الشزر كيفية في موضع العين وتحريكها. ومنها أنهم يصفون النظر بالشدة والصلابة، وأنشد ابن قتيبة : يتقارضون إذا التقوا في موطن نظرًا يزيل مواطن الإقدام قال ابن قتيبة يكاد يزيلها من شدة وصلابة والمراد من تلك الشدة والاعتماد على الحدقة في تقليبها وتحريكها.
The eleventh argument: They describe 'nazar' (looking) with what cannot be used to describe 'ru'ya' (vision), and that necessitates difference between them, for 'nazar' is described as being 'shazr' (squinting). The poet said: 'There is no good in hatred and looking askance.' And it is known that 'shazr' is a quality in the location of the eye and its movement. And among them is that they describe 'nazar' with intensity and hardness, and Ibn Qutayba recited: 'They exchange, when they meet in a place, a look that displaces the positions of courage.' Ibn Qutayba said: 'It almost displaces them from intensity and hardness, and the intended meaning of that intensity is the reliance on the pupil in its turning and movement.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh argument in a series proving that 'nazar' (looking) is not the same as 'ru'ya' (vision). The argument is that people describe 'nazar' with qualities that cannot apply to 'ru'ya'. Specifically, 'nazar' can be described as 'shazr' (squinting or looking askance), which involves a particular positioning and movement of the eye. The author cites a poetic example illustrating this usage. Additionally, 'nazar' is described with intensity and hardness, as shown in another poem cited by Ibn Qutayba, where a look is so intense it displaces courage. The commentary explains that this intensity refers to the effort and pressure on the pupil during turning and movement. Since 'ru'ya' cannot be described with these physical movements and intensities, the two terms must be distinct.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
الشزر (al-shazr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nazar and ru'ya are interchangeable synonyms.
Correction: This argument shows that nazar has specific physical descriptions that ru'ya lacks, thus they are distinct terms.

Checkpoint
What quality does Razi use to distinguish nazar from ru'ya in the eleventh argument?
A) Intensity and hardness
B) Shazr (squinting)
C) Both A and B
D) Duration

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that nazar and ru'ya are not synonymous because nazar can be described with physical qualities like shazr and intensity, which ru'ya cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that nazar can be described with intensity and hardness, have you ever considered that your own looking carries such weight? How does this challenge the way you regard your glances at others? Will you now check your heart before you look?
?
This passage shows that ru'ya is distinct from nazar because it lacks physical qualities. In your life, do you conflate looking with seeing? Can you recall a moment when you looked at someone but failed to truly see them? What would change if you sought ru'ya instead of just nazar?
?
Razi quotes poetry to prove his point about shazr. How does your heart respond knowing that even your sidelong glances are described as 'nazar' with quality? Does this make you more mindful of how you look at things, especially those you dislike? Will you repent for any looks that were harsh or squinting in hatred?

Poem Proof: Looking vs. Seeing
Razi cites a poem to distinguish looking from seeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a poetic example demonstrates that the act of looking (nazar) can occur without seeing (ru'ya), thereby distinguishing them as separate concepts in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Looking is not seeing; you can look without perceiving.

Imam al-Razi presents a unique argument using a poem to illustrate a key distinction. The poet describes standing and looking at a house, but his tears prevent him from seeing. This shows that the act of looking (nazar) is independent of the act of seeing (ru'ya), a crucial nuance in discussions of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic proof
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the poet's description of his state.
The poem is cited by Razi as the fourteenth argument.
2
Identify the key acts
The poet uses two verbs: 'anẓur' (I look) and 'ubṣir' (I see). Find each occurrence.
3
Observe the contrast
The poet looks continuously, but seeing is intermittent. When his eyes are full of tears, he is veiled and cannot see. When they clear, he sees.
This demonstrates that looking does not guarantee seeing.
4
Understand the theological point
Razi uses this to argue that nazar and ru'ya are distinct. This matters for debates about whether Allah can be seen.
The argument is that nazar is a mental act, ru'ya is perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشرة: ما أنشد بعضهم : 0 وقفت كأني من وراء زجاجة إلى الدار من فرط الصبابة أنظر فعيناي طورًا تغرقان من البكا فأغشي وطورًا يحسران فأبصر
The fourteenth argument: What some of them recited: 'I stood as if from behind a glass towards the house from excessive longing, looking (anẓur). My eyes sometimes drown in tears so I am veiled (ughshā), and sometimes they are exhausted so I see (ubṣir).'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is the fourteenth argument, which presents a poetic proof. The poet describes himself standing and looking (anẓur) at a house due to longing. He then notes that his eyes sometimes fill with tears, so he is veiled and cannot see (ughshā), and at other times they are exhausted and then he sees (ubṣir). The key point is that he describes himself as 'looking' (anẓur) even when he is not seeing. The act of looking (nazar) is continuous, while seeing (ibṣār) occurs intermittently. This demonstrates that looking (nazar) is not the same as seeing (ru'ya).

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الإبصار (al-ibṣār)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Looking (nazar) necessarily implies seeing (ru'ya).
Correction: The poem demonstrates that one can look without seeing; nazar and ru'ya are distinct acts.

Checkpoint
In the poem cited in the fourteenth argument, what happens to the poet's eyes when they are filled with tears?
A) They see clearly
B) They are veiled (ughshā)
C) They turn red
D) They become exhausted

Key Takeaway
Poetry can serve as theological evidence; but more importantly, the act of looking (nazar) is distinct from the act of seeing (ru'ya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you 'looked' at a sign of Allah in creation—like a sunset or a newborn—but did not truly 'see' His power and mercy. What tears (distractions, preoccupations) veiled your heart? Will you intentionally remove them tomorrow?
?
The poet’s eyes were veiled by tears of longing. What deep longing in your life has actually become a veil? How can you turn that longing into a means of true vision (ibṣār) of what Allah wants you to see?
?
Looking at the Quran is not the same as seeing its truths. In which area of your faith have you been 'looking' for years but never truly 'seeing'? What specific step will you take tonight to move from anẓur to ubṣir?

Fifteenth Argument: Seeking Reward for Looking
Razi cites a poet seeking reward for looking, which would be irrational if looking were seeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a poetic example to prove that looking (nazar) is distinct from seeing (ru'ya).

Core Idea
You can seek reward for looking, but not for seeing—proving they differ.

Razi presents a clever linguistic argument from poetry: a poet asks his beloved to repay him for looking at her. If looking were the same as seeing, seeking repayment would be absurd, as seeing is its own pleasure. This highlights the distinction between nazar (effortful looking) and ru'ya (actual vision).

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and explanation
Read the Arabic poem and its literal translation. Notice how the poet asks for recompense specifically for being a 'looker' (nāẓir).
The poem is from Abu Ali al-Farisi's work 'Al-Hujja'.
2
Identify the key inference
Focus on Abu Ali's comment: if nazar meant ru'ya, the poet would be seeking reward for pleasure, which is irrational.
This inference rests on the assumption that seeing is inherently beneficial.
3
Connect to the theological debate
Understand why this matters: in kalam, proving nazar ≠ ru'ya supports the Ash'ari view that vision of Allah is possible even without physical sight.
This argument undermines the Mu'tazilite equation of nazar with ru'ya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشرة : ما أنشده أبو علي الفارسي في كتاب الحجة : فيا مي هل تجزي بكائي بمثله مرارًا وأنفاسي عليك الزوافر وإني متى أشرف من الجنب الذي به أنت من بين الجوانب ناظر قال أبو علي الفارسي يطلب منها الجزاء على كونه ناظرا إليها، ولو كان النظر عبارة عن الرؤية لها لكان قد طلب الجزاء على منفعته ولذاته وحصول غرضه، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
The fifteenth argument: What Abu Ali al-Farisi recited in the book 'Al-Hujja': 'O May, will you repay my weeping with its like? My breaths are many, sighs over you. And I, whenever I look out from the side where you are among the sides, I am a looker (nāẓir).' Abu Ali al-Farisi said: He seeks from her recompense for his being a looker (nāẓir) at her. If 'nazar' were an expression for seeing (ru'ya) her, then he would have sought recompense for his benefit, pleasures, and attainment of his aim, and that no rational person would say.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is the fifteenth argument, cited from Abu Ali al-Farisi's work 'Al-Hujja'. The poet asks his beloved May to repay his weeping and sighs, stating that whenever he looks at her, he is a looker (nāẓir). The key point is that he asks for recompense for the act of looking. But if looking (nazar) were the same as seeing (ru'ya), then seeing her would be a pleasurable experience that benefits him. Seeking repayment for a benefit is irrational; no sane person would demand compensation for a pleasant experience. Therefore, looking must be a burdensome or effortful act distinct from the pleasure of seeing. Thus, nazar is not ru'ya.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
الجزاء (al-jazā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) are synonymous in classical Arabic.
Correction: This argument shows that nazar can be a burdensome act deserving compensation, while ru'ya is a pleasurable result. They are not identical.

Checkpoint
Why would it be irrational to seek recompense for seeing (ru'ya) according to the fifteenth argument?
A) Seeing is a burdensome act
B) Seeing is a benefit and pleasure
C) Seeing is involuntary
D) Seeing is rare

Key Takeaway
Because one can rationally seek reward for looking but not for seeing, looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) are distinct acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, are you seeking reward for the effort you put in (like looking) or for the pleasure of seeing Allah? Which of these truly motivates your heart? Resolve today to shift from seeking reward to seeking His vision.
?
The poet sought recompense for looking at his beloved. Do you treat your relationship with Allah as a transaction of effort and reward, or do you long for the pure joy of seeing Him? Ask yourself: Do I want His reward more than I want Him?
?
If looking and seeing are distinct, how often do you confuse the mere appearance of piety (looking) with genuine faith (seeing)? Commit to one action this week that moves you from looking like a believer to truly seeing Allah in your life.

Seventeenth Argument: Nazar Is Not Ru'ya
Razi uses a poem to argue nazar ≠ ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a poetic example proves that nazar (looking) is different from ru'ya (vision).

Core Idea
Looking can continue after sight is impossible — nazar and ru'ya are distinct.

Imam Razi presents his seventeenth argument using a line of poetry. A sorrowful lover casts a glance (nazar) after the riders have already passed a mile away, a time when vision (ru'ya) cannot occur. This example shows that nazar does not require ru'ya, so they cannot be the same. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow. Notice the poetic line and Razi's conclusion.
2
Identify the key elements
Find where the poet affirms looking and when it occurs. Then note that vision is absent at that moment.
3
Follow the logical argument
Razi argues: looking is affirmed when vision is not possible, so looking must be different from vision. Trace this reasoning step by step.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this distinction matters for theology — it prevents confusing intellectual consideration (nazar) with seeing Allah (ru'ya).
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the argument's structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة عشرة: أنشد بعضهم : ونظرة ذي شجن وامق إذا ما الركائب جاوزن میلا أثبت النظر بعد مجاوزة الركائب ميلاً، ومعلوم أن الرؤية غير حاصلة في هذا الوقت، ولما أثبت النظر حال عدم الرؤية ثبت أن النظر غير الرؤية.
The seventeenth argument: Some of them recited: 'And a glance (naẓra) of a sorrowful, ardent lover, when the riders passed a mile.' He affirmed looking (nazar) after the riders had passed a mile, and it is known that vision (ru'ya) is not occurring at that time. Since he affirmed looking in the absence of vision, it is established that looking is not vision.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is the seventeenth argument, again using a poetic example. The poet describes a look (naẓra) of a sorrowful lover after the riders have already passed a mile away. At that distance, vision (ru'ya) is impossible; one cannot see the riders. Yet the poet still uses the word 'looking' (nazar) for that act. Since he affirms looking at a time when seeing does not occur, the two cannot be identical. Thus, nazar and ru'ya are distinct.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
النظرة (al-naẓra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) are the same, and that looking necessarily implies vision.
Correction: Razi shows that looking can occur even when vision is impossible, as in the poem where the lover looks after the riders are out of sight.

Checkpoint

A) While the riders are present
B) After the riders have passed a mile
C) Before the riders arrive
D) At the moment of seeing

Key Takeaway
The seventeenth argument proves that nazar and ru'ya are distinct because looking can be affirmed when vision is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poem describes a lover looking after his beloved even when they are out of sight. In your relationship with Allah, do you have a 'glance' of longing that persists even when you do not 'see' immediate answers or results? What does that reveal about your love?
?
Razi proves that looking can exist without seeing. Are there any acts of worship you have abandoned because you felt you could not 'see' their benefit? How does this proof challenge you to resume them, trusting that the 'looking' itself is valuable?
?
When you pray, do you merely 'look' through the motions or do you truly 'see' the presence of Allah? How can you transform your 'nazar' into 'ru'ya' through greater presence of heart?

First Aspect: Distinguishing Idrak from Ru'ya
First aspect: distinguishing idrak from ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that negating perception (idrak) does not negate vision (ru'ya) and how he grounds this in linguistic usage.

Core Idea
Perception means encompassing, not seeing; the verse only denies encompassing.

Al-Razi begins his response by challenging the Mu'tazili equation of idrak and ru'ya. He uses Arabic linguistic evidence to show that idrak means reaching or encompassing, not mere vision. This distinction allows him to affirm ru'ya while denying idrak, just as we know Allah without encompassing Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first aspect
Read the Arabic passage from 'والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى' to 'فكذلك نراه ولا ندركه'. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi defines idrak.
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' is the key target.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Al-Razi gives examples: 'adraka al-ghulam' (reached puberty) and 'adrakat al-thamara' (ripened). He also cites Quran 26:61 where 'mudrakun' means overtaken. These show idrak implies reaching/completing, not just seeing.
Notice the contrast: ru'ya is simple sight, idrak is sight that encompasses.
3
Apply the distinction to the verse
Since idrak involves encompassing limits, and Allah has no limits, the verse only negates encompassing vision, not vision itself. So the Mu'tazili proof fails.
The conclusion: we see Allah but do not perceive (encompass) Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى وهي تمسكهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] من وجوه: الأول: لا نسلم أن الإدراك عبارة عن الرؤية بل هو عبارة عن الوصول، يقال: أدرك الغلام إذا صار بالغا وأدركت الثمرة إذا وصلت إلى حد النضج. قال تعالى: ﴿لَمَّا قَالَ أَصْحَابُ مُوسَىٰ إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ﴾ [الشعراء: ٦١] أي لملحقون. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن من رأى شيئا ورأى أطرافه ونهاياته، قيل: إنه أدركه - على تقدير أن يكون قد أحاط به من جملة جوانبه - وهذا المعنى إنما يتحقق في الشيء الذي له أطراف ونهايات والباري تعالى منزه عن ذلك. فلم تكن رؤيته إدراكا البتة. فلم يلزم من نفي الإدراك نفي الرؤية. فالحاصل أن الإدراك رؤية مكيفة ولا يلزم من نفي الرؤية المكيفة، نفي أصل الرؤية. وكما أنا نعرف الله ولا نحيط به، فكذلك نراه ولا ندركه. الوجه الثاني في الجواب: هب أن إدراك العين عبارة عن الرؤية، إلا أن قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ نقيض لقولنا: تدركه الأبصار، وقولنا: تدركه الأبصار يقتضي أن يدركه كل أحد لأن الألف واللام إذا دخلا على اسم الجمع، يفيدان الاستغراق. ونقيض الموجبة الكلية: السالبة الجزئية. فكان قوله لا تدركه الأبصار معناه: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار جميعها، ونحن نقول بموجبه فإنه لا يراه جميع المبصرين. فإن الكافرين لا يرونه بل يراه بعض الأبصار. الوجه الثالث في الجواب: أنا نقول بموجب الآية: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار: فلم قلتم بأنه لا يدركه المبصرون؟ فإن قالوا: المراد من الأبصار في الآية: المبصرون، وإلا خرجت الآية عن أن تكون مفيدة، فنقول: إذا حملنا الأبصار على المبصرين، وجب أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] أنه يدرك جميع المبصرين، ولا نزاع في أنه تعالى مبصر فيلزم بحكم هذه الآية أن يدرك نفسه، وأنتم لا تقولون به. الوجه الرابع في الجواب: هب أن ظاهره يدل على نفي الرؤية عن جميع المبصرين إلا أنه عام وقوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَىٰ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٢-٢٣] خاص. والخاص مقدم على العام.
And the answer to the first objection, which is their holding to His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103], from several aspects: The first: we do not concede that perception (idrak) means vision (ru'ya); rather it means reaching. It is said: the boy has 'perceived' (adraka) when he reaches puberty, and the fruit has 'perceived' when it reaches ripeness. Allah said: 'When the companions of Moses said: We are indeed overtaken (mudrakun)' [al-Shu'ara 61], that is, overtaken. If you know this, then we say: whoever sees a thing and sees its extremities and ends, it is said that he has 'perceived' it — on the assumption that he has encompassed it from all its sides — and this meaning is only realized in something that has extremities and ends, and the Creator is transcendent above that. So His vision is not perception at all. So it does not follow from the negation of perception the negation of vision. The conclusion is that perception is qualified vision, and the negation of qualified vision does not entail the negation of vision as such. Just as we know Allah but do not encompass Him, so we see Him but do not perceive Him. The second aspect of the answer: Grant that perception by the eye means vision, yet His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' is the contradictory of our saying 'Vision perceives Him', and our saying 'Vision perceives Him' would imply that everyone perceives Him, because when 'al-' enters a plural noun, it indicates generality. The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. So His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' means: not all eyes perceive Him, and we affirm its implication, for not all seers see Him — disbelievers do not see Him, but some seers do. The third aspect of the answer: We affirm according to the verse that eyes do not perceive Him; so why do you say that seers do not perceive Him? If they say: what is meant by 'absar' (eyes) in the verse is seers (mubsirun), otherwise the verse would not be informative, we say: if we interpret 'absar' as seers, then the meaning of His saying 'And He perceives the eyes' [al-An'am 103] would be that He perceives all seers, and there is no dispute that He is seer, so according to this verse He would be required to perceive Himself, which you do not affirm. The fourth aspect of the answer: Grant that its apparent meaning indicates the negation of vision from all seers, but it is general, and His saying 'Faces that Day shall be radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyama 22-23] is specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the first four aspects (wujuh) of al-Razi's response to the first Mu'tazili objection, which is based on the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'. The first aspect distinguishes idrak (perception) from ru'ya (vision): idrak means encompassing or reaching, not mere seeing. The verse negates idrak, not ru'ya. The second aspect uses logic: the verse is a universal negation ('no eyes perceive Him'), which is the contradictory of universal affirmation. The contradictory is a particular negative, meaning 'not all eyes perceive Him' — thus some eyes (believers) may see Him. The third aspect responds if the Mu'tazila interpret 'absar' as 'seers': then the second part of the verse 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar' would mean He perceives all seers, implying He perceives Himself, which they deny. The fourth aspect argues that even if the verse negates vision for all seers, it is general, whereas the verse about looking at the Lord is specific and takes precedence.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrāk)
الرؤية (al-ru’ya)
الوصول (al-wuṣūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think idrak and ru'ya are synonyms, so negating idrak negates ru'ya.
Correction: Al-Razi shows idrak means encompassing; the verse only negates that, not simple vision.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's first aspect, what does 'idrak' mean?
A) Vision
B) Encompassing or reaching
C) Understanding
D) Sensory contact

Key Takeaway
Negating 'idrak' (encompassing perception) does not negate 'ru'ya' (vision) because Allah can be seen without being encompassed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', did you ever feel a sense of distance from Allah, as if seeing Him is impossible? How does knowing that this verse only denies encompassing vision—not the vision itself—change the way you hope to meet your Lord? Will you now actively cultivate that hope in your daily worship?
?
You claim to know Allah, but do you truly feel that you know Him without encompassing Him? In your prayer and reflection, are you satisfied with a limited knowledge, or are you yearning for a direct vision? What would it mean for you to long to see Allah without trying to confine Him to your mental images?
?
The analogy compares knowing Allah without encompassing Him to seeing Him without perceiving Him. In your relationship with Allah, are you more focused on 'encompassing' Him through your intellect or on simply 'seeing' Him with your heart? Which approach dominates your spiritual life, and what needs to change?

Second Aspect: Logical Interpretation of the Negation
Second aspect: logical interpretation using quantification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses logical quantification to argue that the verse only denies universal perception, allowing for vision by some.

Core Idea
The verse 'no eyes perceive Him' is a universal negative, whose contradictory is a particular affirmative.

In the second aspect, al-Razi employs logical terminology. Even if idrak meant vision, the verse's form—'la tudrikuhu al-absar'—is a universal negation. Its logical contradictory is a particular affirmative: 'some eyes perceive Him'. Therefore, the verse only denies that all eyes see Allah, not that some do. This allows believers to see Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second aspect
Read from 'الوجه الثاني في الجواب' to 'بل يراه بعض الأبصار'. Focus on the logical terms 'muujiba kulliyya' and 'saliba juz'iyya'.
Al-Razi grants the Mu'tazili definition for argument's sake.
2
Understand the logical rule
A universal affirmative (every A is B) is contradicted by a particular negative (some A is not B). Similarly, a universal negative (no A is B) is contradicted by a particular affirmative (some A is B).
Note: 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' is a universal negative.
3
Apply the rule to the verse
If the verse is a universal negative ('no eyes perceive Him'), its contradictory is 'some eyes perceive Him' (particular affirmative). Thus the verse only negates that all eyes perceive Him, not that some do.
Al-Razi concludes that disbelievers do not see, but believers may.

And the answer to the first objection, which is their holding to His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103], from several aspects: The first: we do not concede that perception (idrak) means vision (ru'ya); rather it means reaching. It is said: the boy has 'perceived' (adraka) when he reaches puberty, and the fruit has 'perceived' when it reaches ripeness. The conclusion is that perception is qualified vision, and the negation of qualified vision does not entail the negation of vision as such. Just as we know Allah but do not encompass Him, so we see Him but do not perceive Him.
