Third Aspect: Consequences of Interpreting 'Absar' as 'Seers'
Third aspect: reductio ad absurdum on the interpretation of 'absar'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that if 'absar' means seers, the verse would imply Allah perceives Himself, which the Mu'tazila deny.

Core Idea
If 'absar' means seers, then 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar' would mean God perceives all seers, including Himself.

In the third aspect, al-Razi considers the Mu'tazili claim that 'absar' in the verse means 'seers' rather than 'eyes'. He then applies this interpretation to the second part of the verse: 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar'. If 'absar' means seers, then God perceives all seers, which includes Himself. This leads to an absurdity that the Mu'tazila reject, thus undermining their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third aspect
Read from 'الوجه الثالث في الجواب' to 'وأنتم لا تقولون به'. Notice the conditional argument.
Al-Razi is turning the Mu'tazili's own interpretation against them.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
The Mu'tazila argue that 'absar' in 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' means 'seers' (mubsirun), otherwise the verse would be trivial. Al-Razi grants this for argument.
They say: 'If it meant eyes, then the verse would be saying eyes do not perceive Him, which is obvious; so it must mean seers.'
3
Apply the interpretation to the second part
The verse continues: 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar'. If 'absar' means seers, then 'He perceives the seers' — meaning all seers. Since Allah is a seer, He would perceive Himself. This conclusion is unacceptable to the Mu'tazila.
Thus the interpretation leads to a contradiction, so it cannot be maintained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى وهي تمسكهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] من وجوه: الأول: لا نسلم أن الإدراك عبارة عن الرؤية بل هو عبارة عن الوصول، يقال: أدرك الغلام إذا صار بالغا وأدركت الثمرة إذا وصلت إلى حد النضج. قال تعالى: ﴿لَمَّا قَالَ أَصْحَابُ مُوسَىٰ إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ﴾ [الشعراء: ٦١] أي لملحقون. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن من رأى شيئا ورأى أطرافه ونهاياته، قيل: إنه أدركه - على تقدير أن يكون قد أحاط به من جملة جوانبه - وهذا المعنى إنما يتحقق في الشيء الذي له أطراف ونهايات والباري تعالى منزه عن ذلك. فلم تكن رؤيته إدراكا البتة. فلم يلزم من نفي الإدراك نفي الرؤية. فالحاصل أن الإدراك رؤية مكيفة ولا يلزم من نفي الرؤية المكيفة، نفي أصل الرؤية. وكما أنا نعرف الله ولا نحيط به، فكذلك نراه ولا ندركه. الوجه الثاني في الجواب: هب أن إدراك العين عبارة عن الرؤية، إلا أن قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ نقيض لقولنا: تدركه الأبصار، وقولنا: تدركه الأبصار يقتضي أن يدركه كل أحد لأن الألف واللام إذا دخلا على اسم الجمع، يفيدان الاستغراق. ونقيض الموجبة الكلية: السالبة الجزئية. فكان قوله لا تدركه الأبصار معناه: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار جميعها، ونحن نقول بموجبه فإنه لا يراه جميع المبصرين. فإن الكافرين لا يرونه بل يراه بعض الأبصار. الوجه الثالث في الجواب: أنا نقول بموجب الآية: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار: فلم قلتم بأنه لا يدركه المبصرون؟ فإن قالوا: المراد من الأبصار في الآية: المبصرون، وإلا خرجت الآية عن أن تكون مفيدة، فنقول: إذا حملنا الأبصار على المبصرين، وجب أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] أنه يدرك جميع المبصرين، ولا نزاع في أنه تعالى مبصر فيلزم بحكم هذه الآية أن يدرك نفسه، وأنتم لا تقولون به. الوجه الرابع في الجواب: هب أن ظاهره يدل على نفي الرؤية عن جميع المبصرين إلا أنه عام وقوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَىٰ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٢-٢٣] خاص. والخاص مقدم على العام.
And the answer to the first objection, which is their holding to His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103], from several aspects: The first: we do not concede that perception (idrak) means vision (ru'ya); rather it means reaching. It is said: the boy has 'perceived' (adraka) when he reaches puberty, and the fruit has 'perceived' when it reaches ripeness. Allah said: 'When the companions of Moses said: We are indeed overtaken (mudrakun)' [al-Shu'ara 61], that is, overtaken. If you know this, then we say: whoever sees a thing and sees its extremities and ends, it is said that he has 'perceived' it — on the assumption that he has encompassed it from all its sides — and this meaning is only realized in something that has extremities and ends, and the Creator is transcendent above that. So His vision is not perception at all. So it does not follow from the negation of perception the negation of vision. The conclusion is that perception is qualified vision, and the negation of qualified vision does not entail the negation of vision as such. Just as we know Allah but do not encompass Him, so we see Him but do not perceive Him. The second aspect of the answer: Grant that perception by the eye means vision, yet His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' is the contradictory of our saying 'Vision perceives Him', and our saying 'Vision perceives Him' would imply that everyone perceives Him, because when 'al-' enters a plural noun, it indicates generality. The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. So His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' means: not all eyes perceive Him, and we affirm its implication, for not all seers see Him — disbelievers do not see Him, but some seers do. The third aspect of the answer: We affirm according to the verse that eyes do not perceive Him; so why do you say that seers do not perceive Him? If they say: what is meant by 'absar' (eyes) in the verse is seers (mubsirun), otherwise the verse would not be informative, we say: if we interpret 'absar' as seers, then the meaning of His saying 'And He perceives the eyes' [al-An'am 103] would be that He perceives all seers, and there is no dispute that He is seer, so according to this verse He would be required to perceive Himself, which you do not affirm. The fourth aspect of the answer: Grant that its apparent meaning indicates the negation of vision from all seers, but it is general, and His saying 'Faces that Day shall be radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyama 22-23] is specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the first four aspects (wujuh) of al-Razi's response to the first Mu'tazili objection, which is based on the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'. The first aspect distinguishes idrak (perception) from ru'ya (vision): idrak means encompassing or reaching, not mere seeing. The verse negates idrak, not ru'ya. The second aspect uses logic: the verse is a universal negation ('no eyes perceive Him'), which is the contradictory of universal affirmation. The contradictory is a particular negative, meaning 'not all eyes perceive Him' — thus some eyes (believers) may see Him. The third aspect responds if the Mu'tazila interpret 'absar' as 'seers': then the second part of the verse 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar' would mean He perceives all seers, implying He perceives Himself, which they deny. The fourth aspect argues that even if the verse negates vision for all seers, it is general, whereas the verse about looking at the Lord is specific and takes precedence.

Key Terms
الأبصار (al-absār)
المبصرون (al-mubṣirūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili interpretation of 'absar' as 'seers' strengthens their case.
Correction: Al-Razi shows it leads to an absurdity (God perceiving Himself), thus undermining their position.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi argue is the consequence if 'absar' is interpreted as 'seers'?
A) The verse becomes meaningless
B) God would perceive Himself
C) No one can see God
D) The verse only applies to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili interpretation of 'absar' as 'seers' backfires because it implies God perceives Himself, a conclusion they reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that you think is supported by a text, but someone shows you it leads to a contradiction you reject, would you be willing to abandon your interpretation? Why or why not?
?
Al-Razi exposes the Mu'tazili inconsistency. Are there any views you hold that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would contradict other things you affirm? What would it take for you to confront that inconsistency?
?
If you were in the Mu'tazili position, would you have the intellectual humility to admit your interpretation fails, or would you double down? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to your own reasoning?

Fourth Aspect: Specific Over General
Fourth aspect: hermeneutic precedence of specific over general

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that even if the verse is general, the specific verse about believers seeing God takes precedence.

Core Idea
Even if the verse is general, the specific verse about seeing God in the afterlife overrides it.

In the fourth and final aspect, al-Razi concedes for argument that the general verse negates vision for all seers. However, he invokes the principle that a specific text (khas) takes precedence over a general one (amm). The specific verse from Surah al-Qiyama (75:22-23) states that faces will look at their Lord. This specific, positive text overrides the general negation, allowing vision for believers in the hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the fourth aspect
Read from 'الوجه الرابع في الجواب' to the end. Note the terms 'amm' and 'khas'.
Al-Razi uses a well-known Usul al-Fiqh principle.
2
Understand the principle
In Islamic jurisprudence, when a general text (amm) and a specific text (khas) conflict, the specific is given precedence and restricts the general.
This principle is widely accepted across legal schools.
3
Apply to the verses
The verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' is general (negating vision for all). The verse 'wujuhun yawma'idhin nadirah ila rabbiha nazirah' is specific (affirming vision for believers on that day). The specific text overrides the general, so the negation does not apply to believers in the afterlife.
Thus the Mu'tazili objection fails because the specific verse qualifies the general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى وهي تمسكهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] من وجوه: الأول: لا نسلم أن الإدراك عبارة عن الرؤية بل هو عبارة عن الوصول، يقال: أدرك الغلام إذا صار بالغا وأدركت الثمرة إذا وصلت إلى حد النضج. قال تعالى: ﴿لَمَّا قَالَ أَصْحَابُ مُوسَىٰ إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ﴾ [الشعراء: ٦١] أي لملحقون. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن من رأى شيئا ورأى أطرافه ونهاياته، قيل: إنه أدركه - على تقدير أن يكون قد أحاط به من جملة جوانبه - وهذا المعنى إنما يتحقق في الشيء الذي له أطراف ونهايات والباري تعالى منزه عن ذلك. فلم تكن رؤيته إدراكا البتة. فلم يلزم من نفي الإدراك نفي الرؤية. فالحاصل أن الإدراك رؤية مكيفة ولا يلزم من نفي الرؤية المكيفة، نفي أصل الرؤية. وكما أنا نعرف الله ولا نحيط به، فكذلك نراه ولا ندركه. الوجه الثاني في الجواب: هب أن إدراك العين عبارة عن الرؤية، إلا أن قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ نقيض لقولنا: تدركه الأبصار، وقولنا: تدركه الأبصار يقتضي أن يدركه كل أحد لأن الألف واللام إذا دخلا على اسم الجمع، يفيدان الاستغراق. ونقيض الموجبة الكلية: السالبة الجزئية. فكان قوله لا تدركه الأبصار معناه: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار جميعها، ونحن نقول بموجبه فإنه لا يراه جميع المبصرين. فإن الكافرين لا يرونه بل يراه بعض الأبصار. الوجه الثالث في الجواب: أنا نقول بموجب الآية: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار: فلم قلتم بأنه لا يدركه المبصرون؟ فإن قالوا: المراد من الأبصار في الآية: المبصرون، وإلا خرجت الآية عن أن تكون مفيدة، فنقول: إذا حملنا الأبصار على المبصرين، وجب أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] أنه يدرك جميع المبصرين، ولا نزاع في أنه تعالى مبصر فيلزم بحكم هذه الآية أن يدرك نفسه، وأنتم لا تقولون به. الوجه الرابع في الجواب: هب أن ظاهره يدل على نفي الرؤية عن جميع المبصرين إلا أنه عام وقوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَىٰ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٢-٢٣] خاص. والخاص مقدم على العام.
And the answer to the first objection, which is their holding to His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103], from several aspects: The first: we do not concede that perception (idrak) means vision (ru'ya); rather it means reaching. It is said: the boy has 'perceived' (adraka) when he reaches puberty, and the fruit has 'perceived' when it reaches ripeness. Allah said: 'When the companions of Moses said: We are indeed overtaken (mudrakun)' [al-Shu'ara 61], that is, overtaken. If you know this, then we say: whoever sees a thing and sees its extremities and ends, it is said that he has 'perceived' it — on the assumption that he has encompassed it from all its sides — and this meaning is only realized in something that has extremities and ends, and the Creator is transcendent above that. So His vision is not perception at all. So it does not follow from the negation of perception the negation of vision. The conclusion is that perception is qualified vision, and the negation of qualified vision does not entail the negation of vision as such. Just as we know Allah but do not encompass Him, so we see Him but do not perceive Him. The second aspect of the answer: Grant that perception by the eye means vision, yet His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' is the contradictory of our saying 'Vision perceives Him', and our saying 'Vision perceives Him' would imply that everyone perceives Him, because when 'al-' enters a plural noun, it indicates generality. The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. So His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' means: not all eyes perceive Him, and we affirm its implication, for not all seers see Him — disbelievers do not see Him, but some seers do. The third aspect of the answer: We affirm according to the verse that eyes do not perceive Him; so why do you say that seers do not perceive Him? If they say: what is meant by 'absar' (eyes) in the verse is seers (mubsirun), otherwise the verse would not be informative, we say: if we interpret 'absar' as seers, then the meaning of His saying 'And He perceives the eyes' [al-An'am 103] would be that He perceives all seers, and there is no dispute that He is seer, so according to this verse He would be required to perceive Himself, which you do not affirm. The fourth aspect of the answer: Grant that its apparent meaning indicates the negation of vision from all seers, but it is general, and His saying 'Faces that Day shall be radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyama 22-23] is specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the first four aspects (wujuh) of al-Razi's response to the first Mu'tazili objection, which is based on the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'. The first aspect distinguishes idrak (perception) from ru'ya (vision): idrak means encompassing or reaching, not mere seeing. The verse negates idrak, not ru'ya. The second aspect uses logic: the verse is a universal negation ('no eyes perceive Him'), which is the contradictory of universal affirmation. The contradictory is a particular negative, meaning 'not all eyes perceive Him' — thus some eyes (believers) may see Him. The third aspect responds if the Mu'tazila interpret 'absar' as 'seers': then the second part of the verse 'wa huwa yudriku al-absar' would mean He perceives all seers, implying He perceives Himself, which they deny. The fourth aspect argues that even if the verse negates vision for all seers, it is general, whereas the verse about looking at the Lord is specific and takes precedence.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm)
الخاص (al-khāṣṣ)
التقديم (al-taqdīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What principle does al-Razi use in the fourth aspect?
A) General takes precedence over specific
B) Specific takes precedence over general
C) Both are equally valid
D) The later verse abrogates the earlier

Key Takeaway
Even if the verse negates vision generally, the specific verse about believers seeing Allah overrides it, establishing the Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems absolute, do you automatically assume it applies in all situations? How does this lesson's principle of 'specific over general' challenge you to pause and look for other verses before concluding?
?
Is there a belief or practice in your life that you hold onto because of one isolated text, without considering the broader context of other revealed texts? How would applying al-Razi's method change that?
?
The principle here requires intellectual humility: even a general verse may be qualified by a specific one. Where in your understanding of Islam do you need to be more open to letting a specific text refine or restrict a general understanding you have?

Defending the Possibility of Vision: Praise and Counter-Argument
Al-Razi counters the second argument and presents a praise-based argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi counters the Mu'tazila's second argument from the verse and establishes the possibility of seeing Allah through a logical praise-based argument.

Core Idea
Praise for not being seen proves vision is possible.

Al-Razi continues his refutation by addressing the second aspect of the Mu'tazila's interpretation of the verse. He argues that even if 'perception' implies encompassing, vision remains distinct. Furthermore, he presents a clever counter-argument: if vision were absolutely impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be meaningless—just as we don't praise non-existent things. This shows that vision must be possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the second-aspect response
Identify how al-Razi concedes a point but denies its implication. He grants that idrak may mean encompassing but denies that vision is encompassed.
Notice the shift from idrak to ru'ya.
2
Examine the praise argument
Al-Razi argues that if ru'ya were impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be pointless. Compare with non-existent things.
This is a qiyas argument.
3
Connect to verse
Realize that the very verse used by the Mu'tazila now supports al-Razi's position.
The verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' becomes evidence for possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني في تمسكهم بالآية فنقول: ذلك إنما يلزم لو حملنا الإدراك على الإحاطة قلنا هب أن الإدراك محال على الله تعالى، فلم قلتم بأن الرؤية ممتنعة؟ وأيضا نقول: هذا الاستدلال معارض بأن رؤية الله تعالى لو كانت ممتنعة لما حصل المدح بأنه لا يرى. ألا ترى أن المعدومات تمتنع رؤيتها، وليس لها مدح، بل المدح إنما يحصل لو كانت رؤيته جائزة. ثم إنه سبحانه وتعالى يقدر على منع الأبصار عن ذلك. إذا ثبت هذا فتقول: هذه الآية تدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى جائزة من هذا الوجه. وإذا ثبت الجواز وجب القول بالوقوع في القيامة ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق. وأيضا فقولهم: إن كل ما كان عدمه مدحا كان وجوده ممتنعا منقوض بأنه تعالى يمدح بنفي الظلم والعبث عن نفسه حيث قال: ﴿وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٦] و﴿مَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا بَاطِلًا﴾ [ص: ٢٧] مع أن مذهب المعتزلة أنه تعالى قادر على فعل الظلم والعبث.
As for the second aspect of their argument from the verse, we say: that would only follow if we interpreted 'perception' as encompassing; we say: grant that perception is impossible for Allah, but why do you say that vision is impossible? Also we say: This argument is countered by the fact that if the vision of Allah were impossible, then praise would not occur for His not being seen. Do you not see that non-existent things are impossible to see, and there is no praise for them; rather praise only occurs if His vision were possible. Then He, exalted, is able to prevent eyes from that. If this is established, then we say: This verse indicates that the vision of Allah is possible from this perspective. And if possibility is established, then occurrence in the Hereafter must be affirmed because no one differentiates between possibility and occurrence [in the Hereafter]. Also their statement: Whatever is praiseworthy to be absent, its existence is impossible, is refuted by the fact that He praises Himself for the negation of injustice and futility from Himself, as He said 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants' [Fussilat 46] and 'We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in vain' [Sad 27], while the school of the Mu'tazila holds that He is able to do injustice and futility.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage completes al-Razi's response to the first objection. First, he addresses the second aspect of the Mu'tazila's argument from the verse: even if idrak means encompassing, the verse only negates encompassing, not vision. Second, he presents a counter-argument: if vision were impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be meaningless (non-existents are invisible but not praised); therefore, vision must be possible. He then concludes that the very verse they cite actually indicates the possibility (jawaz) of vision. From this possibility, he deduces actual occurrence (wuqu') in the Hereafter, since no one separates possibility from occurrence in this context. Finally, he refutes a broader Mu'tazili principle: 'If non-existence of something is praiseworthy, its existence is impossible.' Al-Razi points out that Allah praises Himself for being free from injustice and futility, yet the Mu'tazila themselves hold that He is capable of these acts — so the principle is invalid.

Key Terms
الإحاطة (al-iḥāṭa)
المدح (al-madḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'no vision can encompass Him' proves that seeing Allah is impossible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the verse only negates encompassing (idrak), not vision (ru'ya) itself, and that the verse actually supports the possibility of vision when interpreted through the praise argument.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Razi's praise argument?
A) Praising Allah for not being seen proves non-existence
B) If vision were impossible, praise would be meaningless
C) The verse proves vision is impossible
D) Non-existents can be praised

Key Takeaway
By distinguishing idrak from ru'ya and using the praise argument, al-Razi establishes that the verse actually indicates the possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah in the Hereafter, or has your heart become content with distant faith? Reflect: if vision were impossible, would your soul still yearn? And if it is possible, how does that hope shape your actions today?
?
When you praise Allah for being veiled from sight, do you realize that this praise only has meaning because seeing Him is possible? Examine your worship: is it grounded in a belief that He can be seen, or a comfortable distance?
?
Al-Razi argued that the very verse used to deny vision actually proves its possibility. Are there areas in your own faith where you assume something is impossible, but the texts actually leave room? What would change if you embraced that possibility?

From Possibility to Occurrence: Refuting the Mu'tazili Principle
Al-Razi deduces occurrence from possibility and refutes the Mu'tazili principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi moves from the possibility of seeing Allah to its actual occurrence on the Day of Judgment, and how he refutes the Mu'tazili principle linking praise to impossibility.

Core Idea
Possibility leads to occurrence; the praise principle collapses under its own weight.

Having established the possibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah from the verse, al-Razi now asserts that possibility entails actual occurrence (wuqu') in the Hereafter, since no one distinguishes between them in this context. He then tackles a broader Mu'tazili principle: if something's absence is praiseworthy, its existence must be impossible. Al-Razi refutes this by pointing out that Allah praises Himself for being free from injustice and futility, yet the Mu'tazila themselves believe He is capable of these acts—thus breaking the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the possibility-to-occurrence argument
Notice how al-Razi asserts that once possibility is established, occurrence follows because no one differentiates.
This is an important kalam principle: jawaz implies wuqu' unless there is a reason to differentiate.
2
Examine the refutation of the principle
The Mu'tazila claim that if non-existence of something is praiseworthy, then its existence is impossible. Al-Razi provides counterexamples: Allah praises Himself for not being unjust or futile, yet the Mu'tazila hold He is capable of these acts.
This refutes the principle by showing it leads to contradiction.
3
Reflect on the implications
Understand how this refutation strengthens the case for the possibility of vision.
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently hold their principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني في تمسكهم بالآية فنقول: ذلك إنما يلزم لو حملنا الإدراك على الإحاطة قلنا هب أن الإدراك محال على الله تعالى، فلم قلتم بأن الرؤية ممتنعة؟ وأيضا نقول: هذا الاستدلال معارض بأن رؤية الله تعالى لو كانت ممتنعة لما حصل المدح بأنه لا يرى. ألا ترى أن المعدومات تمتنع رؤيتها، وليس لها مدح، بل المدح إنما يحصل لو كانت رؤيته جائزة. ثم إنه سبحانه وتعالى يقدر على منع الأبصار عن ذلك. إذا ثبت هذا فتقول: هذه الآية تدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى جائزة من هذا الوجه. وإذا ثبت الجواز وجب القول بالوقوع في القيامة ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق. وأيضا فقولهم: إن كل ما كان عدمه مدحا كان وجوده ممتنعا منقوض بأنه تعالى يمدح بنفي الظلم والعبث عن نفسه حيث قال: ﴿وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٦] و﴿مَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا بَاطِلًا﴾ [ص: ٢٧] مع أن مذهب المعتزلة أنه تعالى قادر على فعل الظلم والعبث.
As for the second aspect of their argument from the verse, we say: that would only follow if we interpreted 'perception' as encompassing; we say: grant that perception is impossible for Allah, but why do you say that vision is impossible? Also we say: This argument is countered by the fact that if the vision of Allah were impossible, then praise would not occur for His not being seen. Do you not see that non-existent things are impossible to see, and there is no praise for them; rather praise only occurs if His vision were possible. Then He, exalted, is able to prevent eyes from that. If this is established, then we say: This verse indicates that the vision of Allah is possible from this perspective. And if possibility is established, then occurrence in the Hereafter must be affirmed because no one differentiates between possibility and occurrence [in the Hereafter]. Also their statement: Whatever is praiseworthy to be absent, its existence is impossible, is refuted by the fact that He praises Himself for the negation of injustice and futility from Himself, as He said 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants' [Fussilat 46] and 'We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in vain' [Sad 27], while the school of the Mu'tazila holds that He is able to do injustice and futility.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage completes al-Razi's response to the first objection. First, he addresses the second aspect of the Mu'tazila's argument from the verse: even if idrak means encompassing, the verse only negates encompassing, not vision. Second, he presents a counter-argument: if vision were impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be meaningless (non-existents are invisible but not praised); therefore, vision must be possible. He then concludes that the very verse they cite actually indicates the possibility (jawaz) of vision. Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawāz)
الوقوع (al-wuqū‘)
الظلم (al-ẓulm)
العبث (al-‘abath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something's non-existence is praiseworthy, then its existence is impossible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that Allah praises Himself for not being unjust or futile, yet He is capable of both, so the principle is invalid.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi refute the Mu'tazili principle?
A) By denying that non-existence can be praiseworthy
B) By citing verses where Allah praises Himself for negating injustice and futility, while the Mu'tazila say He is capable of them
C) By claiming the principle is self-evident
D) By arguing that vision is impossible

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that possibility of vision necessitates its occurrence in the Hereafter, and he dismantles the Mu'tazili principle by showing it contradicts their own stance on divine capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the vision of Allah is not merely possible but certain for those who affirm possibility, how does that certainty reshape your daily longing for the Hereafter? Are you living as if you will see Him, or has doubt crept in?
?
Allah praises Himself for not being unjust, even though He could be. How does this truth affect your relationship with His justice and mercy? Does it make you fear His justice more or hope in His mercy more?
?
What intellectual objections or cultural assumptions have you absorbed that might subtly deny or weaken your belief in the vision of Allah? Will you actively refute them using al-Razi's method, or will you let them linger in your heart?

Two Responses to the Vision Denial Verse
Al-Razi presents the third and fourth responses to the denial of vision from the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two of al-Razi's responses to the verse denying vision of Allah, specifically how he uses logical and interpretive arguments to affirm that believers will see Allah while disbelievers will not.

Core Idea
Al-Razi uses logical and hermeneutic arguments to reconcile 'vision perceives Him not' with the promise of seeing Allah.

Al-Razi addresses a key objection from those who deny the beatific vision. He offers multiple rejoinders. In this passage, he presents two sophisticated arguments: one based on the logical interpretation of the verse, and the other on the principle that specific Qur'anic verses qualify general ones. This lesson will walk through these responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the logical rule at the start about collective nouns.
2
Understand the logical rule
Al-Razi begins by stating that a collective noun implies generality, and the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. Understand this as the basis for his first interpretation.
This is key to his initial move.
3
Examine the third response
In the third response, al-Razi turns the opponents' interpretation against them: if 'vision' means 'beholders', then the other verse would require Allah to perceive Himself, which they deny.
Notice the logical consistency argument.
4
Examine the fourth response
In the fourth response, al-Razi concedes the apparent generality of the negation but argues that the specific verse about seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection takes precedence.
This uses the usul al-fiqh principle that the specific qualifies the general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر اسم الجمع، يفيدان الاستغراق. ونقيض الموجبة الكلية : السالبة الجزئية . فكان قوله لا تدركه الأبصار معناه : أنه لا تدركه الأبصار، ونحن نقول بموجبه فإنه لا يراه جميع المبصرين. فإن الكافرين لا يرونه بل يراه بعض الأبصار. الوجه الثالث في الجواب : أنا نقول بموجب الآية: أنه لا تدركه الأبصار : فلم قلتم بأنه لا يدركه المبصرون؟ فإن قالوا : المراد من الأبصار في الآية: المبصرون، وإلا خرجت الآية عن أن تكون مفيدة، فنقول : إذا حملنا الأبصار على المبصرين، وجب أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] أنه يدرك جميع المبصرين ولا نزاع في أنه تعالى مبصر فيلزم بحكم هذه الآية أنه يدرك نفسه، وأنتم لا تقولون به. الوجه الرابع في الجواب: هب أن ظاهره يدل على نفي الرؤية عن جميع المبصرين إلا أنه عام وقوله تعالى : ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَىٰ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: ۲۲-۲۳] خاص . والخاص مقدم على العام.
The nineteenth issue: The word for plural indicates generality. And the contradictory of the universal affirmative is the particular negative. So His saying "Vision perceives Him not" means that vision does not perceive Him, and we say by its positive that He is not seen by all beholders. For the disbelievers do not see Him, but He is seen by some beholders. The third aspect in the answer: We say in accordance with the verse: that vision does not perceive Him. So why do you say that beholders do not perceive Him? If they say: the intended meaning of 'vision' in the verse is beholders, otherwise the verse would cease to be useful, we say: If we interpret 'vision' as beholders, then the meaning of His saying "And He perceives [all] vision" [6:103] must be that He perceives all beholders, and there is no dispute that He, exalted, is a beholder, so by the ruling of this verse it follows that He perceives Himself, and you do not hold that. The fourth aspect in the answer: Suppose its apparent meaning indicates the negation of seeing from all beholders, but it is general, and His saying "[Some] faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord" [75:22-23] is specific. And the specific takes precedence over the general.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents two responses (al-wajh al-thalith and al-wajh al-rabi') from Imam al-Razi to those who use the verse "لا تدركه الأبصار" (Vision perceives Him not) to deny the possibility of seeing Allah. The passage begins by affirming the rule that a collective noun (ism al-jam') implies generality, and the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. Al-Razi then interprets the verse to mean that no vision perceives Allah, but that does not negate that some beholders see Him—since disbelievers do not see, but believers may. In the third rejoinder, al-Razi argues that if the opponents interpret "الأبصار" (vision) as "المبصرون" (beholders), then the verse "وهو يدرك الأبصار" (And He perceives [all] vision) would entail that Allah perceives all beholders, including Himself, which the opponents deny. In the fourth rejoinder, he concedes the apparent generality of the negation but holds that the specific verse about seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection ("وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة") qualifies it, and in Islamic legal theory, the specific takes precedence over the general.

Key Terms
الأبصار
المبصرون
العام
الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume 'الأبصار' always means the organ of vision.
Correction: Al-Razi shows it may be interpreted as 'beholders' (المبصرون), and that even then the verse does not negate all vision.

Checkpoint
In the fourth response, how does al-Razi resolve the apparent contradiction between the general verse and the specific verse?
A) He denies the specificity of the second verse.
B) He argues that the specific verse abrogates the general one.
C) He states that the specific takes precedence over the general.
D) He reinterprets the general verse to mean the opposite.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's responses show that the verse denying vision does not contradict the promise of seeing Allah, using logical interpretation and the principle of specific precedence over general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponents insisted that a general negation cannot be overridden. Are you holding onto any general ruling or assumption in your life that prevents you from accepting a specific allowance or command from Allah? Will you reexamine it today?
?
Al-Razi used logical analysis to reconcile two seemingly contradictory verses. When you encounter verses that appear to conflict, do you seek to understand their deeper harmony, or do you dismiss or ignore one? What step will you take this week to reconcile a difficult Quranic issue?
?
If you were asked to defend the possibility of seeing Allah in Paradise to a skeptic, would you be able to present al-Razi's arguments convincingly? If not, what does that reveal about your engagement with the Quran and the scholarly tradition? Will you commit to studying one such argument in depth?

Al-Razi's Refutation: Praise Implies Possibility
Al-Razi refutes the claim that impossibility of vision is praiseworthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the very praise for Allah not being seen actually proves that vision is possible, and how he refutes the principle that praiseworthy absence implies impossibility.

Core Idea
Praise for not being seen only makes sense if seeing is possible but prevented.

Al-Razi continues his refutation of those who use the verse "Visions attain Him not" to deny the possibility of seeing Allah. He presents a sharp logical turn: the very fact that Allah is praised for not being seen actually supports the permissibility of vision. In this passage, he dismantles the argument that impossibility is praiseworthy, using both the logic of praise and a counterexample from Allah's own self-praise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recall that opponents claim the verse shows vision is impossible because Allah is beyond perception, and that this impossibility is a praiseworthy attribute.
2
Follow al-Razi's critique
Al-Razi first questions the link between perception and vision. Then he argues that if vision were impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be meaningless. Praise only applies when something is possible but prevented.
Note the key shift: praise requires possibility.
3
See the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the verse actually indicates permissibility of vision, and since no one distinguishes between permissibility and occurrence, vision must occur in the afterlife.
4
Examine the refutation of the principle
Finally, al-Razi refutes the general rule that 'if absence is praiseworthy, existence is impossible' by pointing to Allah's own praise for lacking injustice, yet the Mu'tazila claim He is capable of it.
This uses a counterexample from the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني في تمسكهم بالآية فنقول : ذلك إنما يلزم لو حملنا الإدراك على الإحاطة قلنا هب أن الإدراك محال على الله تعالى، فلم قلتم : بأن الرؤية ممتنعة؟ وأيضا نقول : هذا الاستدلال معارض بأن رؤية الله تعالى لو كانت ممتنعة لما حصل المدح بأنه لا يُرى . فإن المعدومات تمتنع رؤيتها، وليس لها مدح، بل المدح إنما يحصل لو كانت رؤيته جائزة . ثم إنه سبحانه وتعالى يقدر على منع الأبصار عن ذلك. إذا ثبت هذا فتقول : هذه الآية تدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى جائزة من هذا الوجه. وإذا ثبت الجواز وجب القول بالوقوع في القيامة ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق. وأيضا فقولهم : إن كل ما كان عدمه مدحا كان وجوده ممتنعا منقوض بأنه تعالى يمدح بنفي الظلم والعبث عن نفسه . حيث قال: ﴿وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ﴾ [فصلت : ٤٦] و ﴿وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا بَاطِلًا﴾ [ص: ٢٧] مع أن مذهب المعتزلة أنه تعالى قادر على فعل الظلم والعبث.
As for the second aspect in their clinging to the verse, we say: That follows only if we interpret 'perception' as encompassment. We say: Grant that perception is impossible for Allah, so why do you say that vision is impossible? And also we say: This inference is opposed by the fact that if the vision of Allah, exalted, were impossible, then the praise for His not being seen would not occur. For nonexistent things are impossible to be seen, and they have no praise. Rather, praise only occurs if His vision is permissible. Then He, glorified and exalted, is able to prevent vision from that. If this is established, we say: This verse indicates that the vision of Allah is permissible from this aspect. And if permissibility is established, it is necessary to affirm occurrence on the Day of Resurrection, since there is no one who makes a distinction. And also their saying: Everything whose nonexistence is praiseworthy, its existence is impossible, is refuted by the fact that He, exalted, praises Himself for denying injustice and frivolity to Himself, as He said: "And your Lord is not unjust to [His] servants" [41:46] and "And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in play" [38:27], while the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that He, exalted, is able to commit injustice and frivolity.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's second response (al-wajh al-thani) to the objection from the verse. Al-Razi first notes that the objection only holds if 'perception' (idrak) is interpreted as 'encompassment' (ihata). He then concedes that perception is impossible for Allah but questions why that would entail the impossibility of vision (ru'ya). Furthermore, he argues that if vision were impossible, praising Allah for not being seen would be meaningless, because nonexistent things are also impossible to see yet are not praised. Praise only makes sense if vision is possible but prevented. Al-Razi then concludes that the verse actually indicates the permissibility of vision, and since permissibility is established, occurrence in the afterlife must be affirmed, as no one distinguishes between permissibility and occurrence in this context. Finally, he refutes the objection that anything whose absence is praiseworthy must be impossible by pointing to verses where Allah praises Himself for lacking injustice and frivolity, even though the Mu'tazila claim He is capable of them.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الإحاطة
الرؤية
المدح
المعدومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that praising Allah for not being seen implies vision is impossible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that praise for not being seen actually implies vision is possible (permissible) but prevented; otherwise, the praise would be meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why does the praise for not being seen actually support the permissibility of vision?
A) Because praise only applies to something that is possible to occur.
B) Because nonexistent things are praised.
C) Because Allah is incapable of being seen.
D) Because the verse explicitly states vision is permissible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that praising Allah for not being seen actually proves that vision is possible, and that the general principle linking praiseworthy absence to impossibility is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that praise for not being seen only makes sense if vision is possible, how does that affect your longing to see your Lord? Are you satisfied with the promise of vision in the hereafter, or do you take it lightly?
?
Al-Razi argues that Allah is able to prevent vision. Reflect on your own encounters with the unseen. Do you live as though you truly expect to see Allah, or as though it's an abstract impossibility? How does that change your worship?
?
The refutation shows that praising Allah for not being unjust does not mean He cannot be unjust. In your life, do you ever assume that because something seems praiseworthy, it must be impossible? Where are you limiting Allah's power by your assumptions?

The 'Lan' Particle Does Not Imply Eternal Negation
Al-Razi refutes the objection from 'lan tarani' by arguing that 'lan' does not denote perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Arabic particle 'lan' does not necessarily indicate eternal negation, and how al-Razi uses Quranic evidence to support this.

Core Idea
The particle 'lan' is not always eternal; it can refer to temporary situations.

One of the key scriptural objections against seeing Allah is the verse 'You will not see Me' addressed to Moses. Critics argue that 'lan' here indicates eternal negation. However, al-Razi demonstrates that the same particle is used in the Quran for temporary states. In this lesson, we explore his linguistic and scriptural argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the meaning of 'lan'
Note that al-Razi claims 'lan' does not imply perpetuity. Identify the evidence he gives.
He cites verse 2:95.
3
Examine the evidence
Compare the usage in 2:95: 'And they will never wish for it, forever' yet they will wish for it in the Hereafter. This shows 'lan' + 'abad' can be non-eternal.
4
Apply to the objection
Conclude that 'lan tarani' does not prove eternal non-vision, as 'lan' can be temporary.
This opens the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الثانية - وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] - فنقول : كلمة (لن) لا تدل على التأبيد، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَنْ يَتَمَنَّوْهُ أَبَداً﴾ [البقرة : ۹۵] مع أنهم يتمنونه في الآخرة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة - وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللَّهُ إِلَّا وَحْياً﴾ [الشورى: ٥۱] - فنقول : الوحي هو أن يسمع ذلك الكلام بسرعة، وليس فيه أن يكون محجوباً عن رؤية الله تعالى أم لا.
As for the answer to the second objection—which is their clinging to His saying, exalted: "You will not see Me" [7:143]—we say: The word 'lan' does not indicate perpetuity, evidence being His saying, exalted: "And they will never wish for it, forever" [2:95] while they will wish for it in the Hereafter. And the answer to the third objection—which is their clinging to His saying, exalted: "And it is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation" [42:51]—we say: Revelation is that he hears that speech quickly, and it does not entail whether he is veiled from seeing Allah or not.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage gives al-Razi's responses to two scriptural objections against the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection cites the verse "لَنْ تراني" (You will not see Me) said to Moses. Al-Razi argues that the Arabic particle 'lan' does not imply eternal negation. He supports this with the verse "ولن يتمنوه أبدا" (And they will never wish for it, forever) about the Jews, who are reported to wish for death in the Hereafter, showing that 'lan' can refer to a limited time. The second objection cites "وما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحيا" (It is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation). Al-Razi explains that revelation means hearing speech quickly, and this verse does not address whether a person is veiled from seeing Allah or not; it only concerns communication.

Key Terms
لن
التأبيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume 'lan' always indicates eternal negation.
Correction: Al-Razi shows 'lan' can be temporal, as in Quran 2:95.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that 'lan' does not indicate perpetuity?
A) By citing a verse where 'lan' is used for a temporary state.
B) By quoting a grammatical rule.
C) By arguing that Allah cannot use 'lan' for eternity.
D) By saying that Moses saw Allah despite 'lan'.

Key Takeaway
The objection from 'lan tarani' is not decisive because 'lan' can refer to temporary negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'lan tarani' in the Quran, do you automatically assume it means Allah will never be seen in any realm? How does al-Razi's linguistic analysis challenge your assumption, and what does that reveal about your need to always examine context before forming beliefs?
?
Think of a belief you hold that is based on a single verse or hadith without considering other evidence. Will you commit to studying the full picture before settling your heart? What would that look like?
?
Al-Razi uses another Quranic verse to correct a misunderstanding. Where in your life have you clung to a partial understanding of a religious matter? Are you willing to let scholars challenge your comfort zones and deepen your faith?

Revelation Does Not Preclude Vision of Allah
Al-Razi refutes the objection from 'ma kana libasharin' by defining revelation and limiting the verse's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi interprets the verse about revelation (42:51) to show that it does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The verse on revelation only describes how Allah speaks, not whether He is seen.

Another objection against seeing Allah is the verse that states Allah only speaks to humans through revelation. Opponents argue that if revelation is the only mode of communication, then vision is excluded. Al-Razi clarifies that revelation simply means hearing speech quickly, and the verse does not address the issue of being veiled from sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation to understand al-Razi's response to the third objection.
2
Define 'revelation'
Al-Razi defines 'revelation' (al-wahy) as hearing speech quickly. Identify this definition.
3
Analyze the verse's scope
Note his point: the verse only discusses communication, not whether the recipient sees Allah or not. It does not deny vision.
Thus, the objection is irrelevant.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
This defense supports the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Source Text

As for the answer to the second objection—which is their clinging to His saying, exalted: "You will not see Me" [7:143]—we say: The word 'lan' does not indicate perpetuity, evidence being His saying, exalted: "And they will never wish for it, forever" [2:95] while they will wish for it in the Hereafter. And the answer to the third objection—which is their clinging to His saying, exalted: "And it is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation" [42:51]—we say: Revelation is that he hears that speech quickly, and it does not entail whether he is veiled from seeing Allah or not.

Commentary
This passage gives al-Razi's responses to two scriptural objections against the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection cites the verse "لَنْ تراني" (You will not see Me) said to Moses. Al-Razi argues that the Arabic particle 'lan' does not imply eternal negation. He supports this with the verse "ولن يتمنوه أبدا" (And they will never wish for it, forever) about the Jews, who are reported to wish for death in the Hereafter, showing that 'lan' can refer to a limited time. The second objection cites "وما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحيا" (It is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation). Correction: Al-Razi shows the verse only describes communication, not vision.

Obstinacy as the Reason for Censure
Al-Razi defends the possibility of vision against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi defends the possibility of seeing Allah by arguing that the censure of Moses' people's request was due to their obstinacy, not the impossibility of the request itself.

Core Idea
Allah's censure was about attitude, not content.

Al-Razi addresses a key objection: if seeing Allah is possible, why did Allah strongly censure the request for vision? He offers a sharp answer: the censure was not because the request was inherently wrong, but because it was made with obstinacy. He supports this with a parallel example—the request for angels—where the same censure was clearly about the manner, not the act itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi frames his answer as a rhetorical question: 'Why is it not permissible that...'
The structure is: objection → answer → evidence → analogy.
2
Identify the core argument
Al-Razi claims that the censure (استعظام) was due to the manner of asking (obstinacy/defiance), not the request itself. Find the evidence he provides.
He points to the parallel request for angels, which is permissible in itself.
3
Understand the analogy
The request for angels was censured when done defiantly, even though the act itself is permissible. Al-Razi applies the same logic to the request for vision.
This shows that the attitude, not the object, triggered the censure.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument preserves the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife while explaining away the negative scriptural response.
Al-Razi defends the Sunni position that vision of Allah is possible in the Hereafter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة: أن نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاستعظام لأجل أنهم طلبوا الرؤية على سبيل التعنت والعناد؟ والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى استعظم أيضا طلبهم لإنزال الملائكة. ولا نزاع في جواز ذلك، إلا أنهم لما طلبوه على سبيل العناد، استعظم الله ذلك فكذا في سؤال الرؤية.
As for the answer to the fourth objection: we say why is it not permissible that that censure was because they requested vision in a manner of obstinacy and defiance? The evidence for it is that He, exalted, also censured their request for the descent of angels. And there is no dispute about the permissibility of that, but when they requested it in a manner of defiance, Allah censured that. So likewise in the request for vision.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection based on Allah's strong reaction to the request of Moses' people to see Him. Al-Razi argues that the censure was not due to the request itself being impossible, but because it was made in a spirit of obstinacy (ta'annut) and defiance (inad). He supports this by noting that Allah similarly censured their request for the descent of angels, even though the descent of angels is permissible. The censure was due to the defiant attitude, not the content of the request. Thus, the same applies to the request for vision.

Key Terms
التعنت
العناد
الاستعظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why did Allah censure the request for vision?
A) Because seeing Allah is impossible.
B) Because the request was made with obstinacy.
C) Because the people were not sincere.
D) Because the request was for the descent of angels.

Key Takeaway
Allah's censure of the request for vision was due to the obstinate attitude, not the inherent impossibility of seeing Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever asked Allah for something with a demanding or defiant heart, expecting that He must answer? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the attitude behind your du'as?
?
The lesson shows that even permissible requests can incur Allah's censure if made with the wrong spirit. Are there any Islamic practices you engage in that you do with a sense of entitlement or obstinacy, rather than humility?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between the content of a request and the manner of asking. In your daily life, do you focus more on getting what you want from Allah or on the way you approach Him? What changes might you make to ensure your heart is soft and submissive?

Refuting the Necessity of Vision from the Eight Conditions
Al-Razi begins refuting the rational objection about obstacles to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi denies that the presence of the eight conditions makes vision necessary, and how he uses the example of a large body seen as small to prove that vision is not rationally necessitated.

Core Idea
The eight conditions do not guarantee vision; perception is not mechanically necessary.

Al-Razi begins addressing the rational objection that if all obstacles to vision are removed, vision must occur. He introduces two stations of response, and in the first station he denies this necessity. His first proof draws on everyday experience: a large object seen from a distance appears small, yet all its parts are equally subject to the conditions. This shows that vision does not follow necessarily from the conditions. He then anticipates a geometrical objection about the distances of parts, but the text cuts off before completing the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two stations
Notice that al-Razi says the answer has two stations (maqamayn). Focus on the first station for this lesson.
The second station is not covered in this passage.
2
Understand the first station's claim
Al-Razi denies that vision is rationally necessary when the eight conditions are present.
This is a key move: he shifts from physical necessity to rational necessity.
3
Analyze the first proof
Walk through the example of a large body seen as small from a distance. Consider the three possibilities: seeing all parts, none, or some. The third case shows non-necessity.
The conditions are equal for all parts, so if they necessitated vision, all parts would be seen.
4
Examine the anticipated objection
Al-Razi presents a geometrical argument that parts are not equidistant due to the triangle of vision. He begins to refute it by assuming a discrepancy of a span.
The refutation is incomplete; we must look to later text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبه العقلية فنقول : أما الشبهة الأولى - وهي شبهة الموانع - فالجواب عنها على مقامين : المقام الأول: لا نسلم أن عند حصول الشرائط الثمانية، يجب حصول الإبصار. ويدل على أنه غير واجب عقلا وجهان الحجة الأولى : أنا نرى الجسم الكبير من البعد صغيرًا . وإن رأينا جميع أجزائه وجب أن لا نراه صغيرًا ، بل كبيرًا وإن لم نر شيئا من أجزائه وجب أن لا نراه البتة. وإن رأينا بعض أجزائه دون البعض، مع أن الأجزاء بالنسبة إلى القرب والبعد واللطافة والكثافة وعدم الحجاب وسلامة الحاسة وصحة الرؤية متساوية، لزم أن لا يكون الإدراك مع حصول هذه الشرائط واجباً. لا يقال : إنا إذا أبصرنا شيئاً، اتصل بطرفيه من العين خطان شعاعيان كساقي المثلث، وصار عرض المرئي كالخط الثالث، فحصل هناك مثلث. ثم يخرج من الناظر خط آخر إلى وسط المرئي، قائم عليه، يقسم ذلك المثلث الأول إلى مثلثين. وكل واحد منهما مثلث قائم الزاوية وهذا يصلح أن يكون وترا، لكل واحد من الزاويتين الحادتين الواقعتين على طرفي المثلث الأول الكبير، والخطان الطرفيان كل واحد منهما وتر للزاويتين القائمتين ولاشك أن وتر القائمة أعظم من وتر الحادة. فالخطان الطرفيان كل واحد منهما أطول من الخط الوسطي إذا كان كذلك، لم تكن أجزاء المرئي بالنسبة إلى الرائي متساوية في القرب والبعد . لأنا نقول : لنفرض أن هذا التفاوت واقع بمقدار شبر فلو كان المانع من الرؤية هذا القدر من التفاوت في البعد، لكنا إذا جعلنا المرئي أبعد مما كان قبل ذلك بمقدار
As for the rational objections, we say: As for the first objection—which is the objection of obstacles—the answer to it is in two stations: The first station: We do not concede that when the eight conditions are present, vision must occur. And it is indicated that it is not rationally necessary by two proofs: The first proof: We see a large body from a distance as small. If we have seen all its parts, it would be necessary that we not see it as small, but as large; and if we have not seen any of its parts, it would be necessary that we not see it at all. And if we have seen some of its parts without others, while the parts are equal with respect to proximity, distance, subtlety, density, absence of veil, soundness of the sense, and correctness of vision, it follows that perception is not necessary given the presence of these conditions. It cannot be said: When we see a thing, two radial lines connect its two ends from the eye like the legs of a triangle, and the width of the seen object becomes like the third line, so a triangle occurs. Then another line exits from the viewer to the middle of the seen object, perpendicular to it, dividing that first triangle into two triangles. Each one of them is a right-angled triangle, and this [middle line] can serve as the hypotenuse for each of the two acute angles at the ends of the first large triangle, and the two outer lines each serve as the hypotenuse for the right angles. There is no doubt that the hypotenuse of a right angle is greater than the hypotenuse of an acute angle. So the two outer lines are each longer than the middle line. If this is the case, then the parts of the seen object are not equal in proximity and distance relative to the viewer. For we say: Assume that this discrepancy is by a span. So if the obstacle to vision were this amount of discrepancy in distance, then if we make the seen object more distant by a span than it was before...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage begins al-Razi's response to rational objections against the possibility of seeing Allah, specifically the 'objection of obstacles' (shubhat al-mawani'). The objection asserts that when eight conditions (such as distance, opacity, etc.) are absent, vision is necessitated. Al-Razi denies this necessity in the first station (maqam) of his answer. He provides the first proof: we observe a large object appearing small from a distance. If we saw all its parts, it would appear large; if we saw none, we would not see it at all. But we see only some parts, though all parts are equal in conditions, showing that perception is not necessitated by those conditions. Al-Razi then anticipates an objection based on geometrical optics, which argues that parts are not equidistant due to the geometry of vision, so the conditions are not equal. He begins to refute that by a hypothetical scenario, but the text cuts off before completing the argument.

Key Terms
الشرائط الثمانية
الإبصار
المثلث
الوتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all eight conditions are met, vision must necessarily occur.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that vision does not follow necessarily; the example of a large object appearing small demonstrates that even when conditions are equal, not all parts are perceived.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's first proof that vision is not necessitated by the eight conditions?
A) Different people see differently.
B) A large object appears small from a distance despite equal conditions.
C) The conditions are never all present simultaneously.
D) Vision requires divine intervention.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof demonstrates that the eight conditions do not necessitate vision, as seen in the example of a large body appearing small from a distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi challenges the assumption that physical conditions alone determine vision. In your own life, do you often rely on external circumstances to 'see' the truth, or do you recognize that Allah is the ultimate cause of every perception? How can you train yourself to attribute guidance to Allah alone?
?
This passage refutes objections to seeing Allah in the afterlife. Does your heart truly yearn for this vision, or have you allowed intellectual doubts to overshadow the promise of Paradise? What can you do today to strengthen your hope in meeting your Lord?
?
The objector's geometrical proof seems sophisticated, yet al-Razi shows its flaw. When you encounter seemingly convincing arguments against your faith, do you hastily accept them, or do you critically examine them with the tools of rational theology? Commit to studying one such objection with a scholar or reliable source this month.

Response to the Objection on Necessity of Perception
Razi refutes Mu'tazila objection about necessity of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's argument that perception must be necessary in God fails due to the fundamental difference between God and creation.

Core Idea
Conditions for created vision do not transfer to the Uncreated.

The Mu'tazila objected that if perception were not inevitable, we might fail to see mountains or hear trumpets right in front of us. In this passage, Imam Razi responds by first acknowledging the point for ordinary objects, but then showing that the same reasoning cannot be applied to Allah because His essence is entirely different from creation. This lesson unpacks Razi's argument, which hinges on the principle that different essences have different necessary consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's initial reply
Read the first part where the Mu'tazila objection is presented and Razi counters it with ordinary experience. Notice the structure of an objection-and-response.
The objection uses the hypothetical of mountains and trumpets.
2
Understand the key distinction between witnessed and unseen
Razi grants that in the witnessed world, given certain conditions, perception is necessary. But then he asks why the same should hold for God. Focus on the phrase 'ذات الله مخالفة بالحقيقة والماهية' – God's essence is fundamentally different.
3
Appreciate the argument from conditions
Razi points out that perception in the witnessed requires eight specific conditions, which are impossible to apply to the unseen. That seals the argument. Reflect on why Razi claims this point was overlooked.
Razi notes that neither the Mu'tazila nor his own school had raised this before.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : لو لم يجب الإدراك، لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال وبوقات، ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها. قلنا هذا معارض بجملة العاديات. سلمنا أن عند حضور هذه الشرائط في الشاهد يكون الإدراك واجب الحصول فلم قلتم : إن في حق الله تعالى يجب يكون كذلك؟ وتحقيقه هو أن ذات الله تعالى مخالفة بالحقيقة والماهية لهذه الحوادث والمختلفان في الماهية لا يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم فلم يلزم من كون الإدراك واجبا في الشاهد عند حضور هذه الشرائط، كونه واجبا في الغائب عند حضورها؟ ومما يدل عليه أن الإدراك في الشاهد مشروط بشرائط ثمانية، وفي الغائب نقطع بأنه لا يمكن اعتباره. فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يكون الإدراك في الشاهد واجب الحصول، وفي الغائب لا يكون واجبا. وهذا سؤال متعين لم يتنبه له أحد من المعتزلة، ولا نبه أحد من أصحابنا عليه.
And their saying: 'If perception were not necessary, then it would be possible that there are mountains and trumpets in our presence while we do not see them nor hear them.' We say: This is countered by the custom of ordinary things. We grant that in the witnessed world, upon the occurrence of these conditions, perception is necessarily obtained, but why do you say that in the case of God it must be likewise? Its verification is that the essence of God is essentially and really different from these contingent things, and things differing in essence are not required to be equal in their consequences. So it does not follow from perception being necessary in the witnessed upon the occurrence of these conditions that it is necessary in the unseen upon their occurrence? And among the evidence is that perception in the witnessed is conditioned by eight conditions, while in the unseen we are certain that they cannot be considered. Thus it is not impossible that perception in the witnessed is necessarily obtained, while in the unseen it is not necessary. This is a necessary question that none of the Mu'tazila attended to, nor did any of our companions draw attention to it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila object: if perception is not guaranteed by conditions, we might fail to see mountains or hear trumpets. Razi responds: The objection is countered by ordinary experience. He then concedes the point for the witnessed world but argues that God's essence is fundamentally different from created things, so conditions valid for the witnessed do not transfer to the unseen. He emphasizes that perception in the witnessed requires eight specific conditions; in the unseen, those conditions are entirely inapplicable. Thus, there is no necessity that perception in God's case follows the same rule. Razi notes that this critical point was overlooked by both the Mu'tazila and his own school.

Key Terms
ذات الله
مخالفة
ماهية
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may assume that conditions of vision in the created world apply equally to God.
Correction: Razi shows this is invalid due to essential difference and the inapplicability of conditions.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the Mu'tazila's objection about mountains and trumpets does not apply to God?
A) Because God is not a body
B) Because the conditions for vision in the witnessed world do not apply to the unseen
C) Because we can always see mountains if we look
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny the possibility of vision

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that the rules governing perception in created things cannot be applied to Allah because His essence is fundamentally different and the conditions of perception are inapplicable to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's actions or attributes, do you ever catch yourself expecting Him to follow the same rules you experience in daily life? What would it take to truly internalize that His essence is utterly unlike anything else?
?
The Mu'tazila felt they could demand rational necessity from God. Do you ever find yourself feeling that Allah 'should' act a certain way based on your own sense of fairness or logic? How can you surrender that demand and trust His absolute uniqueness?
?
This lesson teaches that the conditions of perception in creation cannot be applied to Allah. Are there other areas—like love, justice, or mercy—where you might be unconsciously measuring Allah by human standards? What step will you take today to correct that?

The First Aspect: Exposing the Circular Argument
Razi exposes the circularity of the Mu'tazila's second argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi identifies the Mu'tazila's claim that every visible must be opposite as a mere restatement of their position, not a valid proof.

Core Idea
A circular argument that rephrases the claim is not a proof.

Razi begins his response to the second doubt by insisting on a clear definition of the issue: whether a transcendent being can be seen. He then points out that the Mu'tazila's argument—that every visible must be opposite—is simply a restatement of their conclusion. By calling it a restatement, he shows it lacks independent logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the start up to 'bal yaqribu hadha min an yakuna i'adatan lil-matlab bi-ibaratin ukhra'. Then read the translation.
Notice the shift from defining the locus to analyzing the Mu'tazila's argument.
2
Identify the locus of dispute
Razi emphasizes that the precise issue is whether an existent transcendent of place and direction can be seen. This sets the boundaries for the discussion.
3
Understand why Razi dismisses the Mu'tazila's claim
He argues that saying 'every visible must be opposite' is equivalent to saying 'what is not in a direction is not visible'—just in different words. This does not constitute a proof.
The contrapositive of a claim does not prove the claim; it only restates it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why is this important? It clears away a false argument and forces the Mu'tazila to bring a real proof if they have one.
This is a typical method in kalam: first clarify the point of dispute, then evaluate the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : فالجواب عنها من وجهين : الأول : أنا بينا في المقدمة أن ذكر الدلائل لابد أن يكون مسبوقا بتعيين محل النزاع فنقول : محل النزاع أن الموجود المنزه عن المكان والجهة. هل تجوز رؤيته أم لا ؟ فإن ادعيتم أن العلم بامتناع رؤيته ضروري، فذلك باطل - على ما بيناه في المقدمة - وإن ادعيتم أن هذا العلم استدلالي فلا بد فيه من الدليل وقولكم : "إن كل مرئي، لا بد وأن يكون مقابلاه يقرب من أنه إعادة للدعوى لأن المقابل هو الذي يكون مختصا بجهة قدام الرائي فكأنكم قلتم : الدليل على أن ما لا يكون في الجهة لا يكون مرئيا. هو أن كل ما كان مرئيا في جهة والمنطقيون يسمون هذه القضية الثانية عكس نقيض القضية الأولى. وفي الحقيقة لا فرق بين القضيتين في الظهور والخفاء فلم يجز جعل أحدهما حجة في صحة الأخرى، بل يقرب هذا من أن يكون إعادة للمطلوب بعبارة أخرى. والوجه الثاني في الجواب : ثبت أن المقابلة شرط للرؤية في الشاهد فلم قلتم : إنه في الغائب كذلك؟ وتقريره ما ذكرناه في الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى. وتمام الكشف والتحقيق أنا ذكرنا في المقدمات : أن المراد من الرؤية أن يحصل لنا انكشاف بالنسبة إلى ذاته المخصوصة، وهو يجري مجرى الانكشاف الحاصل عند إبصار الألوان والأضواء، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فإذن الانكشاف يجب أن يكون على وفق المكشوف، فإن كان المكشوف مخصوصا بالجهة والحيز وجب أن يكون الانكشاف كذلك. وإن كان المكشوف منزها عن الجهة وجب أن يكون انكشافه منزها عن الحيز والجهة . وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها أيضا على هذا القانون. فالرؤية عبارة عن الانكشاف التام فإن كان الشيء له صورة كان انكشافه بانكشاف صورته ولونه، وإن كان منزها عن الصورة واللون كان انكشافه كذلك أيضا لأن شرط الانكشاف أن يحصل على وفق ماهية المكشوف وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الشبهة الرابعة. واعلم أن من تأمل في هذه الكلمات على سبيل الإنصاف، علم قطعا أنه ليس للمعتزلة في نفي الرؤية شبهة تحيله البتة. وبالله التوفيق.
As for the second doubt, the answer to it is from two aspects. First: We have shown in the introduction that mentioning proofs must be preceded by specifying the locus of dispute. So we say: The locus of dispute is the existent that is transcendent of place and direction: is its vision possible or not? If you claim that knowledge of the impossibility of seeing it is necessary, that is false—as we have shown in the introduction. And if you claim that this knowledge is inferential, then it must have a proof. And your statement: 'Every visible thing must be opposite' is close to being a restatement of the claim because 'opposite' is what is specific to the direction in front of the seer. So it is as if you said: 'The proof that what is not in a direction is not visible is that everything visible is in a direction.' And logicians call this second proposition the contrapositive of the first. In reality, there is no difference between the two propositions in clarity and obscurity, so it is not permissible to make one a proof for the correctness of the other. Rather, this is close to being a restatement of the claim in other words. The second aspect of the answer: It is established that being opposite is a condition for vision in the witnessed world, so why do you say that it is so in the unseen? Its explanation is what we mentioned in the answer to the first doubt. And the complete disclosure and verification is that we have stated in the introductions: the meaning of 'vision' is that unveiling occurs for us with respect to His specific essence, and it is analogous to the unveiling that occurs upon seeing colors and lights. If this is the case, then unveiling must be in accordance with the unveiled object. If the unveiled object is specific to direction and space, then unveiling must be so. And if the unveiled object is transcendent of direction, then its unveiling must be transcendent of space and direction. As for the third doubt, its answer is also according to this principle. So vision is complete unveiling. If the thing has a form, its unveiling is by unveiling its form and color. And if it is transcendent of form and color, its unveiling is likewise, because the condition of unveiling is that it occur in accordance with the quiddity of the unveiled. And this is the very answer to the fourth doubt. And know that whoever reflects upon these words with fairness will know with certainty that the Mu'tazila have no doubt in negating vision that can overturn it at all. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer, specifically the response to the second Mu'tazilite argument against the vision of God. It is presented in two aspects. First aspect: Razi insists on defining the locus of dispute: the issue is whether a transcendent being can be seen. He dismisses the Mu'tazila's claim that the impossibility of seeing God is self-evident as false. If it is inferential, their argument that 'every visible must be opposite' is merely repeating the claim in different terms—since 'opposite' implies direction. They are essentially saying the same thing in different words, which is not a proof. Second aspect: Even if being opposite is a condition for vision in the witnessed world, that does not necessitate it for the unseen. Vision is defined as an unveiling that must correspond to the nature of the object. For a spatial object, unveiling is spatial; for a transcendent object, unveiling is transcendent. Thus, God's vision is possible without spatial conditions. The same principle answers the third and fourth doubts. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila have no valid argument against the vision of God.

Key Terms
شبهة
مقابلة
محل النزاع
ضروري
استدلالي
عكس نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a valid argument can be based on the contrapositive of the conclusion, but Razi shows that this is circular if the premises are not independently established.
Correction: The contrapositive of a claim is logically equivalent; using it as a proof for the original claim is simply restating the claim in different words.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the logical problem with the Mu'tazila's claim that every visible must be opposite?
A) It is a false empirical claim
B) It is a restatement of the claim in different words
C) It contradicts the Qur'an
D) It is a self-evident truth

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument that every visible must be opposite is a circular restatement of their position, not a valid proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief about Allah, do you ever find yourself repeating the same point in different words, assuming it counts as a new proof? How can you ensure your reasoning is sound?
?
Razi insists that we must first specify the locus of dispute before arguing. In your own discussions about religion, do you rush to arguments without clarifying what is actually being disagreed upon?
?
Are you willing to admit when your argument is circular? Or do you cling to it because it feels familiar? What would it take for you to abandon a flawed defense of your beliefs?
