Defining Vision as Unveiling: The Second Aspect
Razi defines vision as unveiling and applies it to answer the doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of vision as an unveiling that must correspond to the nature of the object, and how this resolves the second, third, and fourth doubts.

Core Idea
Vision is an unveiling that adapts to the object's nature, not a spatial relationship.

Having exposed the circularity, Razi now offers a constructive argument. He defines vision as 'unveiling' (inkishaf) that must be proportionate to the object. This allows vision of a transcendent being without spatial conditions, and it also answers the third and fourth doubts. The passage concludes with a confident assertion that the Mu'tazila have no real argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect of Razi's response
Read from 'wal-wajh al-thani fi al-jawab' to the end of the passage. Focus on the definition of vision.
2
Understand Razi's definition of vision
Razi says vision is an unveiling (inkishaf) that occurs with respect to the object's specific essence, analogous to the unveiling when seeing colors. The key is that unveiling must be in accordance with the unveiled object.
3
Apply the principle to God
Since God is transcendent of place and direction, the unveiling of Him is also transcendent. Thus, vision of God does not require spatial conditions.
This directly answers the second doubt's premise that vision requires being opposite.
4
Note how it resolves other doubts
Razi mentions that the same principle answers the third and fourth doubts: for any object, unveiling matches its nature (form, color, or transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : فالجواب عنها من وجهين : الأول : أنا بينا في المقدمة أن ذكر الدلائل لابد أن يكون مسبوقا بتعيين محل النزاع فنقول : محل النزاع أن الموجود المنزه عن المكان والجهة. هل تجوز رؤيته أم لا ؟ فإن ادعيتم أن العلم بامتناع رؤيته ضروري، فذلك باطل - على ما بيناه في المقدمة - وإن ادعيتم أن هذا العلم استدلالي فلا بد فيه من الدليل وقولكم : "إن كل مرئي، لا بد وأن يكون مقابلاه يقرب من أنه إعادة للدعوى لأن المقابل هو الذي يكون مختصا بجهة قدام الرائي فكأنكم قلتم : الدليل على أن ما لا يكون في الجهة لا يكون مرئيا. هو أن كل ما كان مرئيا في جهة والمنطقيون يسمون هذه القضية الثانية عكس نقيض القضية الأولى. وفي الحقيقة لا فرق بين القضيتين في الظهور والخفاء فلم يجز جعل أحدهما حجة في صحة الأخرى، بل يقرب هذا من أن يكون إعادة للمطلوب بعبارة أخرى. والوجه الثاني في الجواب : ثبت أن المقابلة شرط للرؤية في الشاهد فلم قلتم : إنه في الغائب كذلك؟ وتقريره ما ذكرناه في الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى. وتمام الكشف والتحقيق أنا ذكرنا في المقدمات : أن المراد من الرؤية أن يحصل لنا انكشاف بالنسبة إلى ذاته المخصوصة، وهو يجري مجرى الانكشاف الحاصل عند إبصار الألوان والأضواء، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فإذن الانكشاف يجب أن يكون على وفق المكشوف، فإن كان المكشوف مخصوصا بالجهة والحيز وجب أن يكون الانكشاف كذلك. وإن كان المكشوف منزها عن الجهة وجب أن يكون انكشافه منزها عن الحيز والجهة . وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها أيضا على هذا القانون. فالرؤية عبارة عن الانكشاف التام فإن كان الشيء له صورة كان انكشافه بانكشاف صورته ولونه، وإن كان منزها عن الصورة واللون كان انكشافه كذلك أيضا لأن شرط الانكشاف أن يحصل على وفق ماهية المكشوف وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الشبهة الرابعة. واعلم أن من تأمل في هذه الكلمات على سبيل الإنصاف، علم قطعا أنه ليس للمعتزلة في نفي الرؤية شبهة تحيله البتة. وبالله التوفيق.
As for the second doubt, the answer to it is from two aspects. First: We have shown in the introduction that mentioning proofs must be preceded by specifying the locus of dispute. So we say: The locus of dispute is the existent that is transcendent of place and direction: is its vision possible or not? If you claim that knowledge of the impossibility of seeing it is necessary, that is false—as we have shown in the introduction. And if you claim that this knowledge is inferential, then it must have a proof. And your statement: 'Every visible thing must be opposite' is close to being a restatement of the claim because 'opposite' is what is specific to the direction in front of the seer. So it is as if you said: 'The proof that what is not in a direction is not visible is that everything visible is in a direction.' And logicians call this second proposition the contrapositive of the first. In reality, there is no difference between the two propositions in clarity and obscurity, so it is not permissible to make one a proof for the correctness of the other. Rather, this is close to being a restatement of the claim in other words. The second aspect of the answer: It is established that being opposite is a condition for vision in the witnessed world, so why do you say that it is so in the unseen? Its explanation is what we mentioned in the answer to the first doubt. And the complete disclosure and verification is that we have stated in the introductions: the meaning of 'vision' is that unveiling occurs for us with respect to His specific essence, and it is analogous to the unveiling that occurs upon seeing colors and lights. If this is the case, then unveiling must be in accordance with the unveiled object. If the unveiled object is specific to direction and space, then unveiling must be so. And if the unveiled object is transcendent of direction, then its unveiling must be transcendent of space and direction. As for the third doubt, its answer is also according to this principle. So vision is complete unveiling. If the thing has a form, its unveiling is by unveiling its form and color. And if it is transcendent of form and color, its unveiling is likewise, because the condition of unveiling is that it occur in accordance with the quiddity of the unveiled. And this is the very answer to the fourth doubt. And know that whoever reflects upon these words with fairness will know with certainty that the Mu'tazila have no doubt in negating vision that can overturn it at all. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer, specifically the response to the second Mu'tazilite argument against the vision of God. It is presented in two aspects. First aspect: Razi insists on defining the locus of dispute: the issue is whether a transcendent being can be seen. He dismisses the Mu'tazila's claim that the impossibility of seeing God is self-evident as false. If it is inferential, their argument that 'every visible must be opposite' is merely repeating the claim in different terms—since 'opposite' implies direction. They are essentially saying the same thing in different words, which is not a proof. Second aspect: Even if being opposite is a condition for vision in the witnessed world, that does not necessitate it for the unseen. Vision is defined as an unveiling that must correspond to the nature of the object. For a spatial object, unveiling is spatial; for a transcendent object, unveiling is transcendent. Thus, God's vision is possible without spatial conditions. The same principle answers the third and fourth doubts. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila have no valid argument against the vision of God.

ماهية

Misconception: One might think that vision always requires a spatial relationship (like facing) and that God cannot be seen because He is not in space.
Correction: Razi clarifies that vision is an unveiling that adapts to the object; for a transcendent God, the unveiling is also transcendent, so vision is possible without spatial conditions.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Razi says the Mu'tazila have no valid doubt left after his explanation. Yet within you, is there still a whisper of doubt about seeing God in the afterlife? What is the source of that doubt—a desire for physical proof? Are you ready to trust God's promise even when it transcends your worldly experience?
?
If vision is complete unveiling that reveals God as He truly is, what does that mean for your relationship with Him now? Are you content to know Him through His attributes, or do you seek to limit Him to what your intellect can grasp? Commit to studying divine transcendence with humility, asking God to grant you a true unveiling of His majesty.

First Proof That Allah's Essence Is Not Knowable
Razi introduces the 20th issue and gives the first proof that Allah's essence is not known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that Allah’s essence (kunn) is not knowable, based on the distinction between knowing attributes and knowing the essence itself.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah's attributes is not knowing His essence.

This lesson presents the first proof from Imam al-Razi’s ‘Arba‘in on why we cannot know Allah’s essence (kunn). The proof argues that all we know of Allah are His existence, negative attributes (what He is not), and relational attributes (how He relates to creation). These do not reveal the specific reality of His essence. As you read, pay attention to how Razi distinguishes between attributes and essence, and why our intellect cannot bridge that gap.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the issue
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how Razi frames the 20th issue and mentions the two positions: many theologians say the essence is known, but the majority of investigators say it is not.
The chosen view is that the essence is not known.
2
Analyze the first proof: what is known about Allah
Razi claims that what is known is only three categories: existence (wujūd), negative attributes (salbiyya), and relational attributes (iḍāfiyya). Examine the examples he gives for each category.
For negations: not a body, not a substance, etc. For relational attributes: Qādir, 'Ālim, Murīd.
3
Understand the distinction argument
Razi argues that knowledge of these attributes does not give knowledge of the specific essence. He appeals to introspection: our intellect does not infer the essence from the attributes; it only knows that the essence is a distinct reality.
This is the core of the proof: attributes are not the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العشرون
في بيان أن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى هل هو معلوم للبشر أم لا؟
والكلام فيه مرتب على فصلين :
الفصل الأول في أن العلم بالكنه هل هو الآن حاصل أم لا ؟ قال الكثيرون من المتكلمين هذا العلم حاصل. وقال جمهور المحققين بأنه غير حاصل . وهو المختار . ويدل عليه وجوه :
4
الحجة الأولى: أن المعلوم منه ليس إلا الوجود والصفات السلبية، والصفات الإضافية. والعلم بهذه الأمور مغاير للعلم بالذات المخصوصة والحقيقة المخصوصة. فوجب أن لا يكون العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة حاصلا . إنما قلنا : إن المعلوم ليس إلا الوجود والسلوب والإضافيات وذلك لأنا إذا استدللنا الممكنات على بوجود وجود واجب الوجود علمنا أنه موجود وما وراء ذلك فهو من باب الصفات . مثل أن نقول إنه واجب الوجود ومعناه إنه الموجود الذي لا يقبل العدم ونقول إنه قديم ومعناه أنه كان موجودا من الأزل إلى الآن . ونقول : إنه أبدي ومعناه : إنه موجود من الآن لا إلى آخر ونهاية ونقول : إنه ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر، ولا في مكان، وليس له ضد ولاند، وكل ذلك سلوب ونقول : إنه قادر، أي أنه الفعل والترك ونقول إنه عالم أي أنه إنه مريد، أي أنه يصح منه إيقاع الفعل على سبيل التخصيص. وكل ذلك إضافات. فثبت أنه ليس المعلوم للخلق منه إلا الوجود والسلوب والإضافات. وإنما قلنا : إن العلم بهذه الأمور لا يقتضي العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة لأنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا لم نجد عقلنا جازما بأنه متى كانت الصفات هي هذه وجب أن تكون الذات الحقيقة المخصوصة الفلانية على التعيين، بل نجد عقلنا جازما بأنه لا بد وأن تكون تلك الحقيقة في نفسها حقيقة مخصوصة متميزة عن سائر الحقائق وأما أن هي يصح منه يصح منه إيقاع الفعل على وجه الإحكام ونقول : يعرف
۲۱۵
The Twentieth Issue: On the statement whether the essence (kunn) of the reality of Allah Almighty is known to humans or not. The discussion on it is arranged in two chapters: The first chapter: On whether knowledge of the essence is actually obtained now. Many of the theologians (mutakallimūn) say this knowledge is obtained, and the majority of the investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) say it is not obtained, and this is the chosen view. Evidences (wujūh) indicate it: The first proof: That which is known of Him is nothing but existence (wujūd), negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya), and relational attributes (ṣifāt iḍāfiyya). Knowledge of these matters is distinct from knowledge of the specific essence (al-dhāt al-makhṣūṣa) and the specific reality (al-ḥaqīqa al-makhṣūṣa). Therefore, knowledge of the specific reality is not obtained. We said that what is known is only existence, negations, and relational attributes because when we infer from contingent things to the existence of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd), we know that He exists, and beyond that it is in the category of attributes. For example, we say He is Necessary Existent—meaning He is the existent that does not admit non-existence. We say He is Eternal (qadīm)—meaning He existed from eternity past until now. We say He is Everlasting (abadī)—meaning He exists from now to no end or limit. We say He is not a body, not a substance, not in a place, and He has no opposite and no equal—all of these are negations. We say He is Powerful (qādir)—i.e., He is capable of doing and refraining. We say He is Knowing ('ālim)—i.e., He is a Willer (murīd)—i.e., it is valid for Him to perform an action with specification (takhṣīṣ). All of these are relational attributes. So it is established that what is known of Him by creation is nothing but existence, negations, and relational attributes. We said that knowledge of these matters does not necessitate knowledge of the specific reality because when we refer to ourselves, we do not find our intellect firmly convinced that when the attributes are these, the specific essence must be such-and-such particular reality; rather, we find our intellect firmly convinced that that reality in itself must be a specific reality distinct from all other realities, but that [knowing what it is] is not [attained]. And [the text corrupted].
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage introduces the twentieth issue: whether the essence (kunn) of Allah is knowable. It states the discussion is in two chapters: the first chapter addresses whether knowledge of the essence is actually attained. The author notes that many theologians claim it is, but the majority of investigators affirm it is not, which he selects. Then the first proof is presented: what is known of Allah is only existence (wujūd), negative attributes (salb), and relational attributes (iḍāfa). These include: wājib al-wujūd (necessary existence), qadīm (eternal), abadī (everlasting), and negations like not being a body, not a substance, etc., and relational attributes like qādir (powerful), 'ālim (knowing), murīd (willing) which imply connections to creation. The author argues that knowledge of these attributes is different from knowledge of the specific essence. He then gives a reasoning: when we introspect, our intellect does not firmly judge that given these attributes, the essence must be a specific determined reality; rather we know the essence is a distinct reality but not its specific nature. The text ends with some corruption, but the intended conclusion remains: the specific essence is not known.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing Allah's attributes, such as His power and knowledge, means one knows His essence (dhat).
Correction: Razi argues that attributes are separate from the essence; knowing attributes does not give knowledge of the specific reality of the essence, as our intellect does not infer the essence from the attributes.

Key Takeaway
We can know that Allah exists and describe Him with negations and relational attributes, but His specific essence remains beyond our knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah with attributes like 'not a body' or 'knowing everything,' do you feel that you truly know Him? ?
Razi says our intellect does not conclude that the specific essence must follow from the attributes. Have you ever assumed that your mental image of Allah is accurate? What if He is not as you imagine? Will you recommit to worshipping Him as He has described Himself, without trying to picture His essence?
?
If knowing the essence is impossible, how does that affect your relationship with Allah? Does it reduce your closeness, or can intimacy be based on what He has revealed? What can you do today to deepen your love for the One whose essence you cannot fully know?

Introduction to the Issue and First Proof of Unknowability
Razi introduces the debate on knowing Allah's essence and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that the essence of Allah is unknowable, based on the distinction between knowledge of existence/attributes and knowledge of the specific quiddity.

Core Idea
We can know Allah's existence and attributes, but not His essence.

Imam Razi begins the twentieth issue of his Arba'in by posing a fundamental question: Can human beings know the inner reality of Allah? He presents two views, then argues for the chosen view that such knowledge is not attained. The first proof he offers is based on the limited categories of what we can know—existence, negations, and relational attributes—and shows that these do not yield knowledge of the specific essence. In this lesson, we will trace this argument and grasp why Razi concludes that the essence remains beyond our grasp.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the issue and opinions
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that Razi frames the issue of whether Allah's essence is known, and states that many mutakallimun say yes, but the verifiers say no, and he chooses the latter.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وهو المختار' (and that is the chosen view).
2
Understand the first proof's premise: the three categories
The proof begins by stating that what is known of Allah is only existence, negative attributes, and relational attributes. Razi elaborates each category with examples.
Examples of negations: not a body, not in a place. Examples of relationals: powerful, knowing, willing.
3
Follow the reasoning: why categories don't give essence
Razi argues that knowledge of these matters is different from knowledge of the specific reality. He uses an analogy: knowing a magnet's effects does not give knowledge of its essence.
The key move is that attributes are distinct from the quiddity they modify.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that knowledge of the specific essence is not obtained. This is the first proof for the unknowability of Allah's essence.
This conclusion is central to later discussions on divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العشرون في بيان أن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى هل هو معلوم للبشر أم لا؟ والكلام فيه مرتب على فصلين: الفصل الأول في أن العلم بالكنه هل هو الآن حاصل أم لا؟ قال الكثيرون من المتكلمين هذا العلم حاصل. وقال جمهور المحققين بأنه غير حاصل. وهو المختار. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن المعلوم منه ليس إلا الوجود والصفات السلبية، والصفات الإضافية. والعلم بهذه الأمور مغاير للعلم بالذات المخصوصة والحقيقة المخصوصة. فوجب أن لا يكون العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة حاصلاً. إنما قلنا: إن المعلوم ليس إلا الوجود والسلوب والإضافيات وذلك لأنا إذا استدللنا الممكنات على وجود واجب الوجود علمنا أنه موجود وما وراء ذلك فهو من باب الصفات. مثل أن نقول إنه واجب الوجود ومعناه إنه الموجود الذي لا يقبل العدم ونقول إنه قديم ومعناه أنه كان موجوداً من الأزل إلى الآن. ونقول: إنه أبدي ومعناه: إنه موجود من الآن لا إلى آخر ونهاية ونقول: إنه ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر، ولا في مكان، وليس له ضد ولا ند، وكل ذلك سلوب. ونقول: إنه قادر، أي إنه يصح منه الفعل والترك. ونقول إنه عالم، أي إنه يصح منه إيقاع الفعل على وجه الإحكام. ونقول: إنه مريد، أي أنه يصح منه إيقاع الفعل على سبيل التخصيص. وكل ذلك إضافات. فثبت أنه ليس المعلوم للخلق منه إلا الوجود والسلوب والإضافات. وإنما قلنا: إن العلم بهذه الأمور لا يقتضي العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة لأنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا لم نجد عقلنا جازماً بأنه متى كانت الصفات هي هذه وجب أن تكون الذات الحقيقة المخصوصة الفلانية على التعيين، بل نجد عقلنا جازماً بأنه لا بد وأن تكون تلك الحقيقة في نفسها حقيقة مخصوصة متميزة عن سائر الحقائق. وأما أن نعرف تعيين تلك الحقيقة فهذا غير حاصل. وهذا كما أنا لما شاهدنا الأثر المخصوص عن المغناطيس قلنا: إن له حقيقة مخصوصة مميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم تلك الحقيقة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. فكذا ههنا لما علمنا اختصاص ذاته بهذه الصفات على أصل الوجوب واللزوم، علمنا أن له حقيقة متميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم من هذه الصفات تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. والعلم به ضروري. فعلمنا أن العلم بحقيقته المخصوصة غير حاصل.
The twentieth issue: concerning the explanation of whether the essence of the reality of Allah the Exalted is known to humans or not. The discussion on it is arranged in two chapters: The first chapter concerning whether knowledge of the essence is now obtained or not. Many of the mutakallimun said this knowledge is obtained. The majority of the verifiers said it is not obtained. And that is the chosen view. Evidence indicates it through aspects: The first proof: That what is known of Him is only existence, negative attributes, and relational attributes. And knowledge of these matters is different from knowledge of the specific essence and specific reality. So it is necessary that knowledge of the specific reality is not obtained. We said: What is known is only existence, negations, and relationals because when we infer from contingent things the existence of the Necessary Existent, we know that He exists, and beyond that is of the category of attributes. Such as we say He is Necessary Existent, meaning He is the being that does not accept non-existence; and we say He is eternal (qadim), meaning He was existing from eternity to now; and we say He is everlasting (abadi), meaning He is existing from now without end; and we say He is not a body nor a substance nor in a place, and He has no opposite nor equal, and all of that are negations; and we say He is powerful (qadir), meaning He can do and refrain; and we say He is knowing ('alim), meaning He can perform actions with perfection; and we say He is willing (murid), meaning He can perform actions with specification. And all of that are relationals. So it is established that what is known of Him by creation is only existence, negations, and relationals. And we said: Knowledge of these matters does not necessitate knowledge of the specific reality because if we turn to ourselves, we do not find our intellect decisively judging that when the attributes are these, then the specific essence must be such-and-such a specific reality by determination; rather, we find our intellect decisively judging that that reality must in itself be a specific reality distinguished from other realities. But as for knowing the determination of that reality, that is not obtained. This is like when we observed the specific effect of the magnet, we said it has a specific reality distinguished from other realities; as for knowing that reality itself, that is not obtained. Similarly here, when we know the particularity of His essence with these attributes based on the principle of necessity and perpetuity, we know that He has a reality distinguished from other realities; as for knowing from these attributes that specific reality itself, that is not obtained. And knowledge of it is necessary. So we know that knowledge of His specific reality is not obtained.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage defines the twentieth issue and presents the first proof that Allah's essence is unknowable. The author begins by stating the question: whether the essence (kunh) of Allah's reality is known to humans. He divides the discussion into two chapters, the first of which concerns whether knowledge of the essence is currently obtained. He notes that many mutakallimun claim it is obtained, but the majority of verifiers (muhaqqiqun) hold it is not, and that is the chosen view. He then provides proofs, starting with the first proof. The proof argues that what humans know of Allah is limited to three categories: His existence (wujud), negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) such as not being a body, and relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) such as being powerful, knowing, willing. Knowledge of these is different from knowledge of the specific essence. The author supports this by analyzing how we derive knowledge of Allah: from contingent things we infer a Necessary Existent, then we ascribe attributes like necessity, eternity, power, etc., which are either negations or relations. None of these inform us of the quiddity of the essence. He then argues that even when we have all these attributes, we do not find our intellect determinately concluding that the essence must be exactly this specific reality; rather, we only know there is a distinct reality. He uses the analogy of magnetism: we know magnets have a specific reality because of their effects, but we do not know that reality in itself. Similarly, from the attributes we know Allah has a distinct reality, but not its specific identity. Thus, knowledge of the specific essence is not obtained. The argument is a negative one, establishing a limitation on human knowledge.

Key Terms
كنه
حقيقة
الصفات السلبية
الصفات الإضافية
الوجود
السلوب
الإضافيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing attributes like existence and power gives us knowledge of the essence itself.
Correction: The author distinguishes between knowledge of attributes and knowledge of the specific quiddity; attributes do not entail knowledge of the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what are the three categories of what is known of Allah?
A) Existence, essence, and attributes
B) Existence, negative attributes, and relational attributes
C) Power, knowledge, and will
D) Eternity, necessity, and oneness

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that what we know of Allah is limited to existence, negative attributes, and relational attributes, and these do not yield knowledge of His specific essence, so His essence remains unknowable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi argues that even the relational attributes (like power and knowledge) only tell you about Allah's actions, not His inner reality. Are you content to worship a God whose essence you can never grasp, or does this unsettle you? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on your own understanding?

Razi's Third Proof: The Essence is Unknown
Razi argues that the need for proofs like oneness shows the essence is not directly known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that Allah's essence is unknowable, based on the need for additional proofs for oneness despite knowing attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes do not guarantee uniqueness; the essence itself is beyond direct knowledge.

This passage presents a logical argument that although we know some of Allah's attributes, these attributes do not by themselves prove that He is unique. Therefore, we need separate proofs to establish oneness. This shows that His specific essence is not directly known to humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: 'If attributes are shared, then we need proof for oneness; but the essence is unique; therefore the essence is unknown.'
2
Identify the syllogism
Razi states this is a clear syllogism of the second figure. Identify the premises and conclusion.
Second figure: middle term is predicate in both premises.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof distinguishes between knowledge of attributes and knowledge of the essence.
Knowing attributes like existence does not automatically mean we know the essence uniquely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: أن كل ما نعلمه منه سبحانه وتعالى فإن مجرد تصوره غير مانع من وقوع الشركة فيه، ولذلك فإنا بعد معرفة هذه الصفات نفتقر إلى إقامة الدلائل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى واحد. وأما ذاته المخصوصة فإنها من حيث إنها هي لا تقبل الشركة. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه من حيث إنه هو غير معلوم للبشر. وهذا قياس جلي من الشكل الثاني.
The third proof: That everything we know of Him, may He be glorified and exalted, its mere conception does not prevent participation in it. Therefore, after knowing these attributes, we need to establish proofs that He, may He be glorified and exalted, is one. As for His specific essence, it is such that inasmuch as it is itself, it does not admit participation. And if that is so, it must be categorically concluded that inasmuch as He is Himself, He is unknown to humans. And this is a clear syllogism of the second figure.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that Allah's essence is unknowable, based on the need for additional proofs beyond attributes. The author notes that everything we know of Allah—meaning the attributes we have—their mere conception does not prevent the possibility of participation (shirka) in them. For example, the attributes of existence, power, knowledge could theoretically be shared by others. That is why after knowing these attributes, we still need to establish proofs that He is one (wahid). That is, we need a separate argument to show that He is unique and that these attributes belong to Him alone. But His specific essence (dhat makhsusa) is such that, by virtue of being itself, it does not admit participation—it is inherently unique. However, we do not have direct knowledge of that essence; we only know it through attributes that do not guarantee uniqueness. Therefore, we must conclude that inasmuch as He is Himself, He is unknown to humans. The author states this is a clear syllogism of the second figure (a logical form where the middle term is predicate in both premises). This proof highlights the distinction between knowing attributes and knowing the essence.

Correction: Razi argues attributes do not guarantee uniqueness; we need additional proofs for oneness, showing the essence itself is not directly known.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Arguments That Conflict Is Impossible
The objector presents two arguments that conflict between two gods is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments proposed by an objector to show that conflict between two gods is impossible, one based on divine wisdom and the other on divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Conflict between two gods is impossible because each would will the same best action and know only one future.

Imam Razi has argued that conflict between two gods is possible in itself. Now an objector responds, conceding that possibility but insisting that given the attributes of wisdom and knowledge, conflict cannot actually occur. The objector presents two arguments that both gods would necessarily will the same thing. This passage shows how the objector tries to defend the impossibility of conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objector begins by conceding the opponent's point.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two arguments, each with a separate premise. Find each one and note how they are introduced: 'One of them is...' and 'The second aspect...'.
3
Understand the wisdom argument
The first argument uses the attribute of wisdom (hikma). The wise god does only what is best (al-afdal wa al-awla). Since the best is one, both gods will the same thing.
This prevents any difference in actions.
4
Understand the knowledge argument
The second argument uses the attribute of knowledge (ilm). Each god knows all things, including what will and will not occur. They cannot will what they know will not occur, and only one thing is known to occur, so both will the same.
This also prevents conflict.
5
Consider the overall purpose
Ask yourself: How do these arguments serve the objector's position? They aim to show that even if conflict is logically possible, it cannot actually happen given divine attributes.
Note that these are not the author's own arguments but those of an imaginary objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على جواز المخالفة بينهما ، لكن معنا ما يدل على أن مع القول بوجود الإلهين يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما . وهو من وجهين : أحدهما : هو أنا لو فرضنا ،إلهين لوجب كون كل واحد منهما حكيما . والحكيم هو الذي لا يفعل إلا الأفضل والأولى. ولا شك أن الأفضل والأولى في كل شيء واحد. وإذا كان كذلك، كان كل واحد منهما لكونه حكيما، لا يريد إلا ذلك الوجه الواحد. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما. الوجه الثاني : هو أن كل واحد منهما، لما كان إلها وجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما عالما بكل المعلومات وكان كل واحد منهما عالما بأن أي المعلومات يقع؟ وأيها لا يقع ؟ وإرادة ما علم أنه لا يقع يكون محالا . وإذا كان الشيء الذي هو معلوم الوقوع، ليس إلا الواحد ويستحيل أن يريد إلا ما كان معلوم الوقوع، وجب يكون كل واحد منهما مريدا لوقوع شيء واحد بعينه. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما.
We concede that what you have mentioned indicates the permissibility of conflict between them, but we have what indicates that with the statement of the existence of two gods, the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible. That is in two aspects: One of them is that if we suppose two gods, then each of them must be wise. The wise one is he who does only what is best and most fitting. There is no doubt that what is best and most fitting in everything is one. And if this is so, then each of them, being wise, wills only that one aspect. And if this is the case, the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible. The second aspect: that each of them, since he is a god, must be knowledgeable of all known things, and each of them knows which of the known things will occur and which will not occur. Wiling what he knows will not occur is impossible. And since the thing that is known to occur is only one, and it is impossible for him to will except what is known to occur, each of them must will the occurrence of one and the same thing. On this estimation, the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is the objector's rejoinder to Imam Razi's earlier argument. The objector concedes that conflict is possible in itself but argues it cannot actually occur. He gives two reasons: First, each god is wise (hakim), and the wise only does what is best and most fitting (al-afdal wa al-awla). Since the best is one and the same for a given action, both gods will will the same thing, preventing conflict. Second, each god knows all things (alim), including what will and will not occur. It is impossible for them to will what they know will not happen. Since only one thing is known to occur, both gods must will that same thing, so conflict cannot occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: These are the objector's counterarguments, not the author's position. Razi later responds to them.

Key Takeaway

Does Divine Wisdom Force Gods to Agree?
Razi refutes the claim that divine wisdom necessitates harmony between two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the claim that divine wisdom necessitates agreement between two gods, by analyzing the role of motives in divine action.

Core Idea
Either actions depend on motives or they don't; either way, agreement is not necessary.

One might think that if two gods existed, their wisdom would make them always choose what is best, leading to harmony. But Razi challenges this assumption. By examining whether divine actions are motivated by benefits, he shows that agreement is not forced. This lesson unpacks his clever logical move.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: if two gods exist, each is wise (حكيم), so they would agree. Grasp the reasoning that wisdom requires choosing the best, so there would be no conflict.
This is the starting point Razi responds to.
2
Grasp Razi's dichotomy
Razi splits the problem into two cases: either actions depend on a motive (داعي) or they do not. Each branch leads to a different conclusion that undermines the objection.
This is the core of his argument.
3
Analyze the first branch
If actions depend on motive, then determinism (الجبر) follows, meaning God's agency is not bound by benefit. Hence, even if an action lacks benefit, God may still will it, so unity of benefit does not guarantee agreement.
4
Analyze the second branch
If actions do not depend on motive, then equal benefit/harm does not prevent God from choosing arbitrarily. So again, conflict is possible.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both cases show that the objection fails, Razi establishes that conflict between two gods is not impossible.
The objection does not prove its point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : " لو حصل في الوجود إلهان لكان كل واحد منهما حكيما . وذلك يوجب توافقهما " قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي أو لا يتوقف. فإن توقف لزم الجبر . وإذا لزم الجبر لم تكن فاعلية الله تعالى موقوفة على رعاية المصالح، فلم يلزم من خلو الفعل عن المصلحة أن لا يكون مراد الوقوع لله تعالى . فلم يلزم من كون المصلحة ،واحدة عدم المخالفة وإن لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي، لم يلزم من استواء الضدين في المصلحة والمفسدة، عدم الترجيح في القصد والإرادة. فثبت أن هذا السؤال لا يمنع من صحة المخالفة.
His statement: 'If two gods existed in existence, each one of them would be wise, and that would necessitate their agreement.' We say: The action either depends on the motive or does not depend. If it depends, then determinism (al-jabr) is necessitated. And if determinism is necessitated, then God's agency is not dependent upon observing benefits. So it does not follow from an action lacking benefit that it is not intended to occur by God. So it does not follow from the benefit being one that there is no conflict. And if the action does not depend on the motive, then it does not follow from the equality of two opposites in benefit and harm that there is no preference in intention and will. So it is established that this objection does not prevent the possibility of conflict.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if there were two gods, each would be wise (حكيم), and wisdom requires acting in the best interest, so both would agree. Razi responds by analyzing the basis of divine action. He presents a dichotomy: either actions depend on a motive (داعي) or not. If they do depend, that would imply determinism (جبر), meaning God's agency is not bound by considerations of benefit. In that case, an action lacking benefit does not make it undesirable for God, so unity of benefit does not guarantee agreement. If actions do not depend on a motive, then equality of benefit and harm does not prevent God from preferring one action over another arbitrarily. Thus, the objection fails to prove that conflict is impossible.

Key Terms

الداعي
الجبر
المصالح
المصلحة
المفسدة
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People assume divine wisdom necessitates consistent choices, so two wise gods would always agree.
Correction: Razi shows that either actions are necessitated (jabr) or free; in either case, agreement is not forced. Wisdom does not require choosing the best if God is not bound by benefit.

Checkpoint

A) God would always choose the best action
B) Determinism (jabr) is necessitated
C) Actions would be random
D) God would not be wise

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that whether or not divine actions depend on motives, agreement between two gods is not necessitated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's Rebuttal: Foreknowledge Does Not Determine Will
Razi rebuts the claim that foreknowledge of an event forces unity of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical rebuttal to the objection that divine foreknowledge necessitates unity of will.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge does not cause will; will precedes the event, and knowledge follows.

This passage addresses a key objection against the unity of God. If both gods foreknow a single outcome, does that force their wills to agree? Razi shows the flaw by clarifying the temporal order: will, then occurrence, then knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the objector's claim that a single known outcome prevents difference in will.
2
Identify Razi's temporal sequence
Razi presents a sequence: will of occurrence → occurrence → knowledge of occurrence. Trace each step and note which is prior to which.
The will is prior to the event, and knowledge comes after the event.
3
Understand the circularity argument
Razi says if knowledge caused will, then cause would follow effect, creating circularity (دور). Reflect on why this refutes the objection.
The objector assumed knowledge constrains will, but Razi shows will precedes knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "الشيء الذي هو معلوم الوقوع واحد وذلك يمنع من الاختلاف في الإرادة " . قلنا : إرادة الوقوع متقدمة على الوقوع ، الذي هو متقدم على العلم بالوقوع فيمتنع أن يكون العلم بالوقوع سببا لإرادة الوقوع، وإلا لزم الدور.
His statement: 'The thing that is known to occur is one, and that prevents difference in will.' We say: The will of occurrence is prior to occurrence, which is prior to knowledge of occurrence. Therefore, it is impossible for knowledge of occurrence to be a cause of the will of occurrence, otherwise circularity would result.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: only one outcome is known to occur; therefore, both gods must will that same outcome, preventing any difference. Razi's response is a logical-temporal clarification. He asserts that the will (إرادة) to bring about an event is temporally prior to the event itself, and the event is prior to knowledge of the event. Thus, knowledge (العلم) comes after the will. Consequently, knowledge cannot be the cause of the will; if it were, a circular dependency (دور) would arise. Therefore, the fact that only one outcome is known does not force the wills to be identical, because the wills are what determine the outcome, not the other way around.

Key Terms
معلوم الوقوع
إرادة الوقوع
الوقوع
العلم بالوقوع
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say knowledge of occurrence cannot cause the will of occurrence?
A) Because knowledge is eternal
B) Because will is prior to occurrence, and occurrence is prior to knowledge, so cause would follow effect
C) Because God does not have knowledge
D) Because the will is the same for both gods

Key Takeaway
Will precedes its object's occurrence, and knowledge follows; therefore foreknowledge does not determine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used Allah's foreknowledge as an excuse to avoid making a difficult decision, thinking 'it's already written'? What would it mean for you today to truly believe that your will precedes Allah's knowledge of your action—will you take responsibility for your choices?
?
In a situation where you feel powerless because you think Allah already knows the outcome, how does Razi's argument restore your sense of agency? What concrete step will you take this week to act in accordance with your faith rather than passive fate?
?
When you sin, do you ever console yourself by thinking 'Allah knew I would do it anyway'? How can the lesson that will precedes knowledge transform your repentance and motivate you to fight harder against your lower self?

The Difference Between Divine and Human Will
Razi responds to an objection about divine causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends God's free will against the objection that His actions are necessary like human actions, by distinguishing between created will and eternal will.

Core Idea
God's eternal will is self-sufficient; human will is created and dependent.

In this passage, Razi confronts a powerful objection: if human actions require a necessary cause, doesn't the same reasoning force us to say God is a necessary cause? Razi's answer hinges on a sharp distinction between the witnessed and unseen realms. He argues that human will is originated and therefore needs an originator, leading to infinite regress unless we stop at a will created by God. But God's will is eternal, needing no other will, thus preserving His free agency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: it claims Razi's argument in the witnessed realm (human acts) applies equally to the unseen realm (God), making God a necessary cause.
2
Analyze Razi's distinction
Notice how Razi differentiates human will (originated, dependent) from divine will (eternal, independent). Identify the key terms: al-irāda al-muḥdatha vs al-irāda al-qadīma.
3
Connect to Ash'ari theology
Reflect on how this argument fits into the Ash'ari concept of kasb: human acts are created by God but acquired by humans, while God's acts are from His eternal will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الذي ذكرتم في الشاهد يلزمكم مثله في الغائب، فوجب أن يكون الباري تعالى علة موجبة بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار. قلنا الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على الإرادة. وهذه إرادة في الشاهد محدثة، فافتقرت إلى محدث فإن كان ذلك المحدث هو العبد لزم التسلسل . وهو محال فوجب القول بانتهاء الإرادات إلى إرادة ضرورية، يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد ابتداء. وعند هذا يصير الجبر لازما أما إرادة الله تعالى فهي قديمة عندنا، فاستغنت تلك الإرادة لأجل قدمها عن إرادة أخرى فظهر الفرق بين الصورتين
If it is said: This that you have mentioned in the witnessed realm applies similarly in the unseen realm, so the Creator must be a necessary cause by essence, not an agent by choice. We say: The difference between the witnessed and the unseen is that the occurrence of the act from the capable is dependent on will. And this will in the witnessed realm is originated, so it requires an originator. If that originator is the servant, then an infinite regress follows—which is impossible. So it is necessary to say that wills terminate at a necessary will, which God creates in the servant initially. At this point determinism becomes necessary. As for the will of God Almighty, it is eternal in our view, so that will due to its eternity is independent of another will. Thus the difference between the two cases becomes clear.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The objector argues that if Razi's reasoning about human acts requiring determination applies to the seen world, it must also apply to God, making Him a necessary cause (illa mūjiba) rather than a free agent. Razi's answer distinguishes between seen and unseen: human acts depend on a created will, which itself requires another originator, leading to infinite regress unless we posit a necessary will created by God—thus determinism (jabr) follows. But God's will is eternal, so it does not need another will; it is self-sufficient. Therefore, the analogy fails: the necessity that applies to human acts does not apply to God. This preserves God's free agency while explaining the deterministic nature of human actions within the Ash'ari framework of kasb.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
علة موجبة
فاعل بالاختيار
الإرادة المحدثة
الإرادة القديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying human will entirely.
Correction: Razi says human will is created by God, leading to a form of determinism, while God's will is eternal and uncaused.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's response, what is the key difference between human will and God's will?
A) Human will is free, while God's will is determined.
B) Human will is originated and needs an originator, while God's will is eternal and self-sufficient.
C) Both wills are originated.
D) Human will is eternal, and God's will is created.

Key Takeaway
Razi preserves divine free agency by distinguishing between created human will and eternal divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you truly believe your will is independent, or do you recognize that even your desire to choose is created by God? How does this change the humility with which you plan your day?
?
If God's will is eternal and self-sufficient, what does that mean for your prayers? Are you asking God to change His eternal will, or are you acknowledging your dependence on His decree?
?
Razi says that human wills ultimately depend on a necessary will created by God. Does this make you feel resigned or liberated? Why does your heart resist the idea that your choices are ultimately determined by God?

The Chain of Ignorance Ends in God
Razi proves that all ignorance ultimately traces to a first ignorance created by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the first ignorance cannot be chosen by humans and must be directly created by God.

Core Idea
The first ignorance cannot be chosen; it must come from God.

In this lesson, Razi tackles the problem of the origin of ignorance. He considers an objection that ignorance arises from a mistaken belief, but shows that this leads to an infinite regress. The only solution is that there is a first ignorance directly created by God, from which all other ignorances derive. This proof is part of Razi's broader discussion of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the objection-and-response structure.
2
Identify the objection and Razi's reply
Find where Razi presents an objection: that ignorance occurs because one thought it was knowledge. Then see how Razi responds by showing this leads to a regress.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
Trace Razi's reasoning: if every ignorance requires a prior ignorance to motivate it, we never reach a starting point. That is impossible, so there must be a first ignorance not chosen by humans.
This is the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية والعشرون
له الجهل، علمنا أن وقوعه ليس بإيقاعه بل بإيقاع غيره.
فإن قيل : إنما حصل ذلك الجهل لأنه ظن أنه علم، فلا جرم قصد إيقاعه. قلنا : فهو إنما اختار الجهل بسبب أنه كان ذلك الجهل المتقدم حاصلا له فإن كان القول فيه كما في الأول لزم أن يكون كل جهل مسبوقا بجهل آخر، لا إلى بداية وذلك محال، فإذن لابد من انتهاء هذه الجهالات إلى جهل أول غير مسبوق بجهل آخر وذلك الجهل الأول يستحيل أن يكون بسبب أن الإنسان اختاره لأن الإنسان لا يختار ذلك البتة . فثبت أن ذلك الجهل الأول إنما حصل بإيجاد الله تعالى وتخليفه وسائر الجهالات تفرعت عليه فكان الكل مستندا بالحقيقة إلى تخليق الله تعالى وتكوينه.
The twenty-second issue. He has ignorance; we know that its occurrence is not by his own causing but by the causing of another. If it is said: That ignorance only occurred because he thought it was knowledge, so he inevitably intended to cause it. We say: He only chose ignorance because that prior ignorance was already present in him. If the same reasoning applies as in the first case, it would entail that every ignorance is preceded by another ignorance, without beginning, and that is impossible. Therefore, these ignorances must terminate at a first ignorance not preceded by another ignorance. That first ignorance cannot possibly be due to the human choosing it, because the human does not choose that at all. So it is established that that first ignorance only occurred through the creation of God Most High and His bringing into existence, and all other ignorances branched off from it, so all are in reality dependent on God's creation and bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a proof about the origin of ignorance. Razi argues that whenever a person is ignorant, that ignorance cannot ultimately be caused by the person's own choice, because that would require an infinite regress of prior ignorances. He presents an objection: someone might say that ignorance occurs because the person thought it was knowledge and thus chose it. Razi replies that this choice itself depends on a prior ignorance (the mistaken belief that it is knowledge). This leads to an infinite regress unless we stop at a first ignorance that is not chosen. Since humans do not choose ignorance at the most basic level, that first ignorance must be directly created by God. Thus all ignorance ultimately traces back to God's creative act.

Key Terms
الجهل
تناهي
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof might be mistaken as denying human responsibility for all ignorance.
Correction: Razi only applies the argument to the first ignorance; subsequent ignorances can be chosen based on that first one, preserving human responsibility for many acts of ignorance.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Razi gives for why the first ignorance cannot be chosen by the human?
A) The human always chooses knowledge.
B) The human does not choose ignorance at all in any case.
C) The human would need a prior ignorance to choose ignorance, leading to infinite regress.
D) God directly forces humans to be ignorant.

Key Takeaway
All ignorance ultimately depends on a first ignorance created by God, not chosen by humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a fundamental ignorance you have carried for years—something you believed was true but later learned was false. Did you choose that ignorance? How does Razi's argument that the first ignorance comes from God affect how you see your own past mistakes?
?
If the first ignorance is created by God and not chosen by you, then your pursuit of knowledge is not entirely your own doing. Does this make you more humble in your learning, or does it feel like an excuse? What responsibility do you still bear?
?
Razi locates the origin of all ignorance in God's creation. Does this thought make you trust Him more or wrestle with Him? How will you respond the next time you discover a deep ignorance in yourself?

The Philosophers' First Argument: Concepts and Composition
Razi presents the philosophers' first argument using exhaustive division of concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that multiple distinct concepts about God would imply composition or infinite regress, rendering multiple effects impossible.

Core Idea
Multiple distinct concepts about God would either make Him composite or lead to infinite regress.

The philosophers argued that if God is the source of two different effects, then the concepts 'source of A' and 'source of B' are distinct. This distinction forces us to consider whether these concepts are part of God's essence or merely accidental. Through a three-way division, Razi shows that each possibility leads to absurdity, challenging the view that multiple effects can proceed directly from the simple divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the three-fold division: both concepts inside the essence, both outside, or one inside and one outside.
The division is exhaustive.
2
Analyze each division's consequence
For each division, the passage derives an impossibility: composition, infinite regress, or return to the first division. Understand why each is impossible for the Necessary Existent.
3
Grasp the overarching conclusion
The argument concludes that only one effect can proceed from the simple essence. This sets up the philosophers' position that the First Principle can only produce one effect directly.
This is a shubhah (sophistical argument) that Razi will later refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : أن مفهوم أنه تعالى مصدر (أ) غير مفهوم أنه مصدر (ب) بدليل يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالآخر. وهذان المفهومان إما أن يكونا داخلين في الماهية، أو خارجين عنها ، أو يكون أحدهما داخلا في الماهية، والآخر خارجا عنها. فإن كانا داخلين في الماهية وهو القسم الأول - كانت الماهية مركبة. وهذا في حق واجب الوجود محال . وإن كانا خارجين عن الماهية - وهو القسم الثاني - فلا شك أنهما من لواحق هذه الماهية وكل ما كان خارجا عن الماهية لاحقا لها غير مستقل بنفسه فإنه يكون ممكنا بذاته واجبا بغيره وحينئذ يعود التقسيم من أن تكون تلك الماهية مصدرا لأحد هذين اللازمين مغايرة لكونها مصدرا للازم الثاني. وإن كان هذان المفهومان أيضا خارجين عن الماهية لزم إما التسلسل وإما الانتهاء إلى الكثرة في الماهية - وهو القسم الأول .. وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون أحد القسمين داخلا في الماهية والآخر خارجا عنها فهذا يقتضي أن تكون هذه الماهية مركبة ; لأن كل ما له جزء، فأقله أنه مركب من جزأين وذلك يقتضي أن يكون المعلول واحدًا؛ لأن الجزء متقدم والمعلول متأخر، والجزء لا يكون باطلا. فثبت أن القول بصدور الشيئين عنه يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة، فيكون [؟].
The first sophistical argument: that the concept that He, exalted, is the source of (a) is different from the concept that He is the source of (b), as evidenced by the validity of knowing one while being ignorant of the other. And these two concepts are either within the essence, or outside it, or one within and the other outside. If they are within the essence - the first division - then the essence is composite. And this is impossible with respect to the Necessary of Existence. And if they are outside the essence - the second division - then there is no doubt that they are among the accidents of this essence, and everything that is outside the essence, an accident of it, not independent by itself, is contingent by itself and necessary by another. Then the division returns as to whether that essence is the source of one of these two necessary attributes different from being the source of the second attribute. And if these two concepts are also outside the essence, then either an infinite regress follows or the ending in multiplicity in the essence - and that is the first division. As for the third division - that one is within the essence and the other outside - this necessitates that the essence is composite, because everything that has a part is at least composed of two parts, and that necessitates that the effect is one, because the part is prior and the effect is posterior, and the part is not invalid. So it is established that the statement that two things proceed from Him leads to these false divisions, so it is [?].
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an exhaustive objection (shubhah) from the philosophers against the possibility of two effects proceeding from God. The argument proceeds by division: the two distinct concepts (source of A and source of B) must be either both inside the essence, both outside, or mixed. If inside, God becomes composite—impossible. If outside, they are contingent accidents, requiring another cause, leading to regress or multiplicity. If mixed, composition again follows. Hence, only one effect can proceed directly from the simple divine essence.

Key Terms
مفهوم
ماهية
مركب
لاحق
تسلسل
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple attributes imply multiple entities, but the philosophers use this to restrict emanation to one, not to deny attributes altogether.
Correction: The argument is about effects, not attributes; it concludes only one effect, not necessarily one attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if both concepts are outside the essence?
A) The essence becomes composite.
B) The concepts are necessary and independent.
C) Infinite regress or multiplicity in the essence follows.
D) The concepts are identical.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument uses an exhaustive division to show that multiplicity of concepts about God leads to composition or regress, thus only one effect can proceed from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask God for multiple things in your du'a, do you ever unconsciously act as if He has separate 'compartments' for each need? How does the truth of His absolute simplicity and oneness challenge you to trust that He alone is the single sufficient source for everything you need? Write down one specific request you will present to Him today with renewed confidence in His simple, undivided power.
?
The philosophers argued that from the One only one can proceed. Have you ever felt torn between multiple loyalties or priorities, fearing that focusing on one means neglecting another? How does reflecting on God's perfect simplicity free you to be fully present in each moment, knowing that He holds all aspects of your life together without division? What is one area where you need to let go of trying to manage everything and instead rely on His single, all-encompassing will?
?
Razi presents this argument as a 'sophistical' one that he will later refute. Have you ever accepted a belief about God that seemed logically airtight but later realized it was incomplete? What assumptions about God's nature have you held too rigidly? Are you willing to have your understanding challenged, just as Razi challenges the philosophers? Choose one belief you hold about God and ask yourself if it truly honors His transcendence or if it might be a limitation you have imposed.

Response to the First Argument: The Analogy of the Unit
Razi refutes the first argument by showing that one thing can have multiple concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the philosophers' argument that multiple attributes imply composition by using the analogy of the number one (unit).

Core Idea
One thing can have many true descriptions without becoming many things.

The philosophers argued that if Allah has multiple attributes, He must be composite. Razi counters with a simple mathematical analogy: the number one can be described as half of two, third of three, yet it remains a single unit. This shows that multiple true descriptions do not imply multiplicity in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the core argument.
Notice how Razi uses a simple analogy to make a powerful point.
2
Understand the analogy
The number one (unit) can be called half of two, third of three, quarter of four. These are different concepts but they all refer to the same simple unit.
The unit itself does not become multiple or composite.
3
See how it refutes the philosophers' argument
The philosophers claimed that multiple attributes would make Allah composite. Razi shows that if their logic were applied to the unit, it would falsely conclude the unit is composite. Since that is absurd, their argument is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the philosophers' reasoning.
4
Reflect on the principle
One thing can have multiple true descriptions (concepts) without being multiple in reality. This defends the unity of God while affirming many attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن الواحدة يصدق عليها أنها نصف الاثنين، وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة. فنقول : مفهوم أنها نصف الاثنين، مغاير لمفهوم أنها ثلث الثلاثة، ويعود التقسيم الذي ذكرتم فيها فيلزم وقوع الكثرة في الوحدة. وذلك محال ولما كان هذا الكلام باطلا، فكذا ما ذكرتم
And the answer to the first sophistical argument: that the one (unit) is truly described as being half of two, and a third of three, and a quarter of four. So we say: the concept that it is half of two is different from the concept that it is a third of three, and the division you mentioned returns in it, necessitating the occurrence of multiplicity in unity, and that is impossible. And since this statement is false, so too what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first shubhah. Razi uses the analogy of the number one (the unit). The unit can be described as half of two, third of three, quarter of four—these are different concepts, yet the unit remains one without composition. If the philosophers applied their own divisional argument to the unit, they would be forced to conclude that multiplicity occurs in unity, which they themselves would reject. Therefore, their argument against God is invalid.

Key Terms
الواحدة
نصف
ثلث
ربع
مفهوم
كثرة
وحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple true descriptions of a thing imply that the thing is composite.
Correction: Razi's analogy shows that one thing (the unit) can be described in multiple ways (half, third, quarter) without being composite; therefore multiple attributes do not imply composition in God.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's analogy of the unit demonstrate?
A) That the unit is composite.
B) That one thing can have multiple true descriptions without being composite.
C) That concepts must be identical.
D) That the philosophers' argument is correct.

Key Takeaway
The unit analogy demonstrates that multiple concepts can apply to a single entity without implying composition, thereby refuting the philosophers' argument against divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the unit can have multiple descriptions without being multiple, does your heart accept that God can have many attributes without being many? Or do you secretly imagine God as a bare, featureless essence? What would change if you truly embraced that His attributes are real and distinct yet not separate from Him?
?
Razi uses a simple mathematical example to refute a deep philosophical error. How often do you rely on clear, straightforward reasoning from revelation to guard your faith? Will you commit to learning the logical arguments that protect your belief in Allah's perfection, even if it requires effort? What is one step you can take this week?
?
The philosophers' argument would have led to absurdity if applied consistently. Examine your own life: are there any beliefs or practices that, if taken to their logical conclusion, contradict the core of your faith? Are you willing to abandon them for the sake of truth, even if they are comfortable?

Refuting Al-Nazzam on Divine Power Over Evil
Razi refutes al-Nazzam's argument that God cannot create evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes al-Nazzam's argument that God cannot create evil, both from an Ash'ari perspective and using Mu'tazili premises.

Core Idea
God's power includes evil acts; they are not actual only due to absence of motive.

Al-Nazzam of the Mu'tazila argued that God cannot create evil because it would imply ignorance or need. In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi dismantles this argument in two ways: first by rejecting the premise that creation implies need, and second by showing that even on Mu'tazili grounds, evil acts are within God's power though not actualized.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Nazzam's argument
Read the first part where al-Nazzam claims God cannot create evil because it would necessitate ignorance or need. Note the chain: evil emanation → ignorance/need → impossible → not an object of power.
Pay attention to the use of 'muḥāl' (impossible).
2
Examine Razi's first response (Ash'ari)
In the second paragraph, Razi says creation does not imply ignorance or need in God. This directly breaks the chain.
This is a straightforward premise rejection.
3
Examine Razi's second response (Mu'tazili internal)
In the third paragraph, Razi uses Mu'tazili concepts: evil acts are 'predetermined' in that power is suitable, but they are not actual because the motive is absent. Thus, they are possible in terms of power.
Notice the distinction between impossibility of the act and absence of motive.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Understand that Razi preserves divine omnipotence by showing that the impossibility is only due to the motive, not the power itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأربعين في أصول الدين
الفرقة الخامسة من المخالفين في هذه المسألة المعتزلة. أما (النظام) فقال : إنه تعالى غير قادر على خلق القبيح. قال: لأن صدور هذه الأشياء عن الله تعالى محال وكل محال فهو غير مقدور. وإنما قلنا إنه محال لأن صدور هذه الأشياء عن الله تعالى يستلزم الجهل أو الحاجة وكل واحد منهما محال. ومستلزم المحال محال. فصدور هذه الأشياء عن الله تعالى محال . وإنما قلنا : إن المحال غير مقدور، لأن المحال ما يمتنع وقوعه والمقدور هو الذي يمكن وقوعه. والجمع بينهما محال.
والجواب على مذهبنا إن خلق شيء من الأشياء لا يدل في حق الله تعالى على الجهل ولا على الحاجة. فزال هذا السؤال.
وأما على مذهب المعتزلة فالجواب عن هذه الشبهة : إن معنى كون القبائح مقدرة صالحة لإيجادها. فالحاصل أن فعل هذه القبائح وإن كان ممتنعا لامتناع الداعي، لكنه غير ممتنع بالنظر إلى صلاحية القدرة.
The Book of Forty in Usul al-Din.
The fifth group of dissenters on this issue: the Mu'tazila. As for al-Nazzam, he said: God Most High is not capable of creating evil. He said: Because the occurrence of these things from God Most High is impossible (muḥāl), and everything impossible is not an object of power (ghayr maqdūr). We say it is impossible because the occurrence of these things from God Most High necessitates ignorance or need, and each of them is impossible. The necessitator of the impossible is impossible. So the occurrence of these things from God Most High is impossible. And we say: The impossible is not an object of power, because the impossible is what cannot occur, and the object of power is what can occur. Combining both is impossible.
The response according to our school: The creation of anything does not indicate ignorance or need on the part of God Most High. So this objection is removed.
And as for according to the school of the Mu'tazila, the response to this sophism: The meaning of evil acts being 'predetermined' (muqaddara) is that powers are suitable for bringing them into existence. The result is that the doing of these evil acts, although impossible due to the impossibility of the motive (al-dā'ī), is not impossible with respect to the suitability of power (ṣalāḥiyyat al-qudra).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage deals with al-Nazzam's argument that God cannot create evil (al-qabīḥ). Al-Nazzam argues that evil acts emanating from God would imply either ignorance or need, both impossible for God. Therefore, such acts are impossible, and what is impossible is not an object of power. Razi then gives two responses: first, according to his own (Ash'ari) school, creating does not imply ignorance or need in God, so the premise is rejected. Second, according to Mu'tazili premises, evil acts are 'predetermined' in the sense that power is suitable to bring them about, but they are not actual because the motive (dā'ī) is absent. Thus, they are possible in terms of power but not actualized. This preserves divine omnipotence while explaining why evil is not created.

Key Terms
النظام
القبيح
المحال
المقدور
الداعي
صلاحية القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi agrees that God cannot create evil because it is impossible.
Correction: Razi only denies that evil acts are actual, not that they are within God's power. He affirms that God's power is suitable for creating evil, but the motive is absent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's Mu'tazili-internal response, why are evil acts not created by God?
A) They are absolutely impossible.
B) Power is not suitable for them.
C) The motive (dā'ī) to create them is absent.
D) God does not know them.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends God's omnipotence by showing that evil acts are possible in terms of power, but their non-occurrence is due to the absence of a motive, not any deficiency in divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter evil in your life, do you truly believe that God has full power over it but refrains due to a wisdom you may not see? How can you cultivate trust in His perfect motive, even when you cannot understand it? Will you commit to surrendering your confusion to His wisdom today?
?
Razi distinguishes between power and motive. In your own decisions, do you ever confuse having the ability to do something with having the right motivation? How can you ensure your actions align with what is pleasing to God rather than merely possible? Will you pause before acting to examine your motive?
?
This lesson shows that God's power is not limited by the nature of acts. Do you ever subconsciously limit God's power in your mind, thinking certain situations are beyond His control? How can you actively remind yourself of His absolute power over all things, including evil? Will you make a habit of affirming His power in prayer?
