God's Will Follows His Knowledge
Al-Razi states the dispute and gives two proofs and two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'arites argued that God's will conforms to His knowledge, not His command, and how they responded to Mu'tazilite objections.

Core Idea
God's will follows His knowledge, not His command.

Al-Razi presents one of the most critical Ash'arite doctrines on divine will: that God wills whatever He knows will occur, even if He commands otherwise. This lesson introduces the dispute, two rational proofs, and the first two Mu'tazilite objections, setting the stage for a deeper understanding of predestination and free will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two positions
Read the opening lines where Al-Razi contrasts the Mu'tazilite view (will follows command) with the Ash'arite view (will follows knowledge). Notice the practical implication: Abu Jahl's faith is commanded but not willed.
This is the core doctrinal difference.
2
Examine the first proof
Identify the argument that God is the creator of all acts, and since creation without coercion implies will, God wills all acts of servants.
3
Examine the second proof
Follow the reasoning: God knew Abu Jahl would not believe, that knowledge makes faith impossible, so God cannot will faith from him.
This proof uses the principle of opposites (ضدان).
4
Consider the Mu'tazilite objections
Read the first two objections (command implies will, and willing evil is foolishness). Think how an Ash'arite might answer.
The third objection is incomplete in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة والعشرون
في بيان أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات
مذهب المعتزلة : أن الإرادة توافق الأمر : فكل كما أمر الله فقد أراده وكل ما نهى عنه، فقد كرهه.
ومذهبنا : أن الإرادة توافق العلم. فكل ما علم وقوعه فهو مراد الوقوع، وكل ما علم عدمه فهو مراد العدم فعلى هذا إيمان أبي جهل مأمور به وهو غير مراد، وكفره منهي عنه . وهو مراد.
لنا وجهان
الحجة الأولى : قد دللنا على أنه تعالى خالق أفعال العباد وكل من خلق شيئا لا على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، فهو مريد لذلك الشيء، فوجب القطع بأنه تعالى مريد لجميع أفعال العباد. الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى علم من أبي جهل أنه لا يؤمن. وقد بينا أن العلم بعدم الإيمان مضاد لوجود الإيمان. وعند قيام أحد الضدين كان الضد الثاني ممتنع الوجود بذاته. فإذن إيمان أبي جهل ممتنع الحصول مع ذلك العلم. وكل المعلومات معلوم لله تعالى. فالله تعالى لا بد وأن يكون عالما بامتناع وجود هذا يدل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى يمتنع أن يكون مريدا لصدور الإيمان عن الكافر. وأما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على قولهم بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : إنه تعالى أمر الكافر بالإيمان وكل من أمر بشيء فهو مريد لوجود المأمور به فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر. الشبهة الثانية : إرادة الكفر والفسق سفه، والسفه لا يليق بالحكيم. وأيضا : إن منه الكفر والفسق ، ثم إنه يعاقب عليهما كان ذلك غاية السفاهة. الشبهة الثالثة : الطاعة عبارة عن تحصيل مراد المطاع فلو أراد الله تعالى من أراد .
The twenty-fourth issue: On explaining that He, exalted, is the willer of all things. The doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that will agrees with command: so whatever God has commanded, He has willed, and whatever He has forbidden, He has disliked. Our doctrine: that will agrees with knowledge. So whatever He knows will occur, its occurrence is willed, and whatever He knows will not occur, its non-occurrence is willed. Thus, on this basis, the faith of Abu Jahl is commanded but not willed, and his disbelief is forbidden but willed. We have two proofs. The first proof: we have demonstrated that He, exalted, is the creator of the acts of servants, and whoever creates something not by way of coercion and compulsion is a willer of that thing, so it must be conclusively affirmed that He, exalted, is the willer of all acts of servants. The second proof: He, exalted, knew of Abu Jahl that he would not believe. And we have shown that knowledge of the non-occurrence of faith is opposite to the existence of faith. And when one of two opposites exists, the second opposite is impossible in itself. So the faith of Abu Jahl is impossible of occurrence with that knowledge. And all objects of knowledge are known to Allah, exalted. So Allah, exalted, must be knowing of the impossibility of this, indicating that He, glorified and exalted, cannot be willing the occurrence of faith from the disbeliever. As for the Mu'tazila, they have argued for their position with aspects: The first doubt: He, exalted, commanded the disbeliever to believe, and whoever commands something is a willer of the existence of the commanded thing, so it would follow that Allah, exalted, willed faith from the disbeliever. The second doubt: willing disbelief and sin is foolishness, and foolishness is not befitting the Wise. Also, if He creates disbelief and sin, then punishes for them, that is extreme foolishness. The third doubt: obedience is the realization of the will of the obeyed, so if Allah, exalted, willed...
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines the dispute between the Ash'arites and the Mu'tazila regarding the scope of God's will and presents two proofs for the Ash'arite position. Al-Razi begins by stating the Mu'tazilite view: that God's will agrees with His command—whatever He commands He wills, and whatever He forbids He dislikes. He then states the Ash'arite view: that God's will agrees with His knowledge—whatever He knows will occur is willed to occur, and whatever He knows will not occur is willed not to occur. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl (a notorious unbeliever) is commanded by God but not willed, and his disbelief is forbidden by God but willed. Al-Razi then provides two proofs. The first proof: God is the creator of all acts of servants, and whoever creates something without coercion is a willer of that thing, so God wills all acts of servants. The second proof: God knew that Abu Jahl would not believe. Knowledge of the non-existence of faith is opposite to the existence of faith, and when one opposite exists, the other is impossible. So Abu Jahl's faith is impossible given God's knowledge. God knows this impossibility, so He cannot will faith from him. Al-Razi then presents three Mu'tazilite objections (shubah) to this view. The first objection: God commands the disbeliever to believe, and whoever commands something wills it, so God must will faith from the disbeliever. The second objection: Willing disbelief and sin is foolishness, unfitting for the Wise, especially if He creates them and then punishes. The third objection (incomplete in the OCR): Obedience is the realization of the will of the obeyed, so if God wills... (the text breaks off).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God wills whatever He commands.
Correction: The Ash'arites hold that God's will follows His knowledge, not His command. He may command what He does not will (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl) and forbid what He wills (e.g., disbelief).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite view presented by Al-Razi, what does God's will follow?
A) His command
B) His knowledge
C) His wisdom
D) His justice

Key Takeaway
God's will is determined by His knowledge, so He may command what He does not will and forbid what He does will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills the disbelief of Abu Jahl while commanding him to believe, how does that challenge your assumption that God always wills what He commands? Is there a part of your life where you assume God's will aligns with your own desires for what is commanded?
?
When you pray for guidance, do you assume that if God wills goodness for you, He will make it easy and comfortable? How does the fact that God may will what He forbids (like trials) reshape your understanding of what is truly good for you?
?
This lesson shows that God's will is based on His perfect knowledge, not on His commands. Are there areas in your life where you resist God's decree because you think it contradicts what He has commanded? Will you submit to His will even when it is hard and seems to go against your understanding of His command?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
God's Will: Command vs. Knowledge
Razi contrasts Mu'tazilite and Ash'arite views on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite distinction between God's will and His command, and how it differs from the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
God's will aligns with His knowledge, not His command.

This passage from Imam Razi's al-Arba'een introduces a critical theological dispute. The question is whether God's will is identical to His command or to His knowledge. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Ash'arite position on divine will and human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the English translation to grasp the two positions.
The word 'به' after the title may be a stray artifact; ignore it.
2
Contrast the two positions
Notice that the Mu'tazilites say will equals command; the Ash'arites say will equals knowledge. How does this change what God wills?
The Ash'arite view allows for commanded things that are not willed, like faith from a disbeliever.
3
Reflect on the example of Abu Jahl
Consider the example: Abu Jahl's faith is commanded but not willed; his unbelief is forbidden but willed. This shows the real-world implication of the Ash'arite doctrine.
This illustrates that God's command does not necessitate His will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة والعشرون
في بيان أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات
به
مذهب المعتزلة : أن الإرادة توافق الأمر : فكل كما أمر الله فقد أراده وكل ما نهى عنه، فقد كرهه.
ومذهبنا : أن الإرادة توافق العلم. فكل ما علم وقوعه فهو مراد الوقوع، وكل ما علم عدمه فهو مراد العدم فعلى هذا إيمان أبي جهل مأمور به وهو غير مراد،
وكفره منهي عنه . وهو مراد.
The twenty-fourth issue: Concerning the explanation that He, exalted, is the Willer of all existent things. The doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that will corresponds to command: so everything as God commanded, He willed it, and everything He forbade, He disliked it. And our doctrine: that will corresponds to knowledge. So everything whose occurrence He knew, its occurrence is willed, and everything whose non-existence He knew, its non-existence is willed. Thus, according to this, the faith of Abu Jahl is commanded but not willed, and his unbelief is forbidden and it is willed.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage opens the twenty-fourth issue, which is about God's will concerning all created things. Razi first presents the Mu'tazilite position: for them, God's will aligns with His command. Whatever God commands, He wills; whatever He forbids, He dislikes. Then he presents the Ash'arite position: God's will aligns with His knowledge. Whatever God knows will occur, He wills its occurrence; whatever He knows will not occur, He wills its non-occurrence. To illustrate, he uses the example of Abu Jahl: his faith is commanded by God (since God commands all to believe) but it is not willed by God because God knows it will not happen. Conversely, Abu Jahl's unbelief is forbidden by God but it is willed (since God knows it will happen). This shows the Ash'arite view that God's will is not identical to His command; rather it follows His knowledge of what will be.

Key Terms
الإرادة
العلم
الأمر
الكائنات
مراد
مأمور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's command and His will are always the same.
Correction: The Ash'arite view distinguishes them: God can command something He does not will (e.g., faith from a disbeliever) and forbid something He does will (e.g., disbeliever's unbelief).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite view presented here, what is the relationship between God's will and His knowledge?
A) God's will corresponds to His command.
B) God's will corresponds to His knowledge.
C) God's will is independent of both command and knowledge.
D) God's will is the same as His pleasure.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite view distinguishes God's will from His command, basing will on His knowledge of what will actually occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills the unbelief of Abu Jahl even though He forbids it, how does that confront your assumption that God's will always matches what He commands? In what area of your life have you assumed that because God commands something, He must also will it for you? What if He doesn't? Will you still obey?
?
God's will is based on His knowledge of what will happen, not on your efforts. Does this make you feel powerless or free? When you pray for guidance, are you asking God to change His will—or asking for strength to accept what He already knows? Which is more difficult?
?
Consider a sin you commit repeatedly. According to this lesson, God may forbid it but still will its occurrence. Does this truth drive you to despair or to deeper trust in God's wisdom? How does your view of God's justice need to expand to embrace this reality?
